stories are famous throughout the world. It is hard to

from this, our standing injunction to our contributors :
“ Whatever you are, never be commonplace." The re-

ality of their own. The Argonaut endeavors to present a
weekly review of the literary movement. Brief notes on
American newspaper.

THE RED PAGE.
1“ Cripps Clark” writes of chanteys and chantey-
men —
A “shantey”—probably a corruption of the
French chanson —serves two very useful purposes
aboard ship : by timing the pulls on a rope by cer-
tain words in the chorus it ensures the watch bend-
ing their backs as one man ; and it enables the
shanty-man to voice his shipmates’ opinions upon
unpopular officers, and all their grievances
against the “blanky old basket” they’ve been
“shanghaied” into. These remarks are inter-
polated in time and tune with the chantey they
are singing.
A real good chanteyman must be a bit of a wit;
with a good voice, an extensive stock of assorted
chanteys suitable for the various occasions, and a
gift for hastily composing topical verses when a
lengthy pull has exhausted the stock edition. He
is the soloist, and sings the loudest and pulls the
least of the whole crowd. He rarely joins in the
chorus, as he requires the time to get breath and
arrange his rhyme for the next couplet. Imagine
him and his mates in position for hoisting the
main tops’l. The chanteyman stands by the
ha’ly’ds, gripping them well above his
head ; the rest of the motley crew tail on
along the deck with the “brass-bounders” in the
rear, where they are only credited with taking in
the slack. Sea-apprentices’ motto is, “We do
the grunting ; let those who are paid for it do the
pulling.” +
The chantey-man gives a preliminary yell and
leads off with :
Extra verses may be grafted on as required; but
the above are kept in type, so to speak.
Another tops’l chantey goes :
O ! up aloft that yard must go,
Away-ay ! blow the man down 1
0 ! shake her up from down below—
Give us some time to blow the man down.
If, early in the voyage, the mate shows a disposi-
tion to bully and “haze” the crew, and they
happen to be hard-cases and white, the next verse
may convey this gentle warning :
0 ! There is that man who stands on the poop,
Away-ay ! blow the man down !
If he hazes us round we’ll give him the boot,
0 ! give us a chance to knock the man down !
On the other hand, when the ship and her officers
are satisfactory, the chanty-man signifies the same
in the usual manner :
I thought I heard our “ old man " say,
Hooray ! Heisrho !
“0, steward, serve out some grog to-day,
John’s bound for Rio ! ”
Should the captain happen to appear at the break
o’ the poop, and the chances are favorable, the
chantey-man catches his attention with
A bully ship, and a bully crew.
Hooray! (putt) Heigho ! (pull).
A bully mate to see us through.
John’s (pull) gone to Rio (pull).
This is a hint direct; but, as a rule, the chantey-
man’s allusions to those in authority are so artfully
veiled that only the officer aimed at, and the
grinning crow, can sec the drift of them. Some-
times they are met with a sharp order to Hand-
over-hand on those ha’ly’ds,” which means more
pulling and less singing. When Jack is real savage,
he won’t sing at all, but gives a “ dry pull, timed
by a single grunt, for well he knows that nothing
is more irritating to the officer of the watch than
sulky silence. *
In these days of donkey-engines and smaller
crews, capstan-songs are going out of use. They
are not so incoherent as the haulmg-chanteys, m
which sense is sacrificed to sound. A favorite up-
anchor chantey is the homeward-bound chorus —
At the windlass Jack always has a kind word
for the girls he is leaving* behind, mingled with
pleasurable anticipations of the girls ahead of him.
Outward bound, he will inform his weeping Poll
on the pier -head that
Sometimes we’re bound for Liverpool,
Sometimes we’re bound for France ;
Heave away-ay, my bullies—heave away 1
But now we’re bound for Sydney town
To give those girls a chance ;
Heave away, my bully boys, we’re all bound to go 1
Sometimes, when “ hot coppers ” and a sore head
induce a repentant mood, he walks around the
capstan, exclaiming:
“ I’ll go no more a-ro-oving, with you fair maid a-roving !
a-roving ! since roving’s been my ruining,” &c., &c.
Another rousing windlass song is “Marching
through Georgia ”; but for plaintive melody I
prefer “Old Stormy’s Dead.” I well remember
lying on the fo’c’s’le-head one still, moonlight
night at Mauritius listening to it. The ship
getting up anchor was far enough away to tone
down the voices of the crew; and as the song, and
the clank, clank, of the windlass palls, floated to
me in the spice-scented air, I thought it one of
the prettiest tunes I had ever listened to. The
minor cadence of the air suited the hour and the
scene, and I shall ever remember the chanting of:
We dug his grave with a silver spade,
We lowered him in with a golden chain,
He stormed to-day, but he’ll storm no more,
Poor old Stormy’s dead.
It was a tuneful dirge. All the chanteys Jack
loves are musical, and even fashionable composers
have been known to appropriate the airs. The
words, of course, are impossible. They “carry no
moral.” Often enough they carry, a lot that is
most decidedly immoral. ,\
* * * *
Sailors singing at their work, and sailors singing
during their watch below, are totally different in
their choice of subjects. In his few leisure hours,
Jack has a decided leaning towards purely senti-
mental ditties. He will sit on his “donkey” in
the fo’c’sle, or outside on the spare top-mast in
warm weather, and sadden all hands with the
lament that
The white man came to Maryland
With slaves to buy and sell,
And so enticed that he bore away
My pretty, O ! my pretty Flora Bell.
Or he will relate in a tuneful jodel, and with many
peculiar chantey variations, that he will
Ne’er forget the day my Nellie lost her way,
And I traced her little footsteps in the snow.
And so on, from “Mother kissed me in my
dreams” to “I walked out one May morning to
view the flowers in Spring. ” He is not at all par-
ticular, and if the “old man” invites him to a
gospel-meeting aboard ship, in a foreign port,
Jack will go and tackle all the hymns with just as
much gusto as he puts into a chantey. Indeed,
Jack has often more religion than he gets credit
for ; his awful profanity —which is really only
automatic —being against him. But, if there is a
heated argument going on in the fo’c’sle, it is safe
to bet 6 to 4 on that the subject is either a knotty
point in women or—Bible history.
The most deeply-religious “shell” I was eVer
shipmates with was “Old Dad”; he was not
much over 40, but the oldest member of the ship’s
crew generally has that title thrust upon him. lie
used to kneel down in prayer every night before
turning-in, and was really a good fellow. But
one very wild night all the watch were stowing the
mainsail. Dad was next to me on the lee-yard-
arm, and was remarking, in a casual sort of way,
that, “ Gordam my soul to h —l if I can feel the
gasket, ” when the flogging of the canvas whipped
him into outer darkness, as he was “lost and
given over ” before I reached the deck. A parson
may not think his exit very promising, but I
believe, if there is any chanteying to be done aloft,
“ Old Dad ” will be there lifting up his voice with
the best of them.
Writes “ Thorold Dalrymple ”
What injustice is done to an admirable lyrist by
printing the “ best ” of Ogilvie’s unfortunate son-
net experiments ! It may be pleaded that our
sonneteers have hitherto neglected their Austra-
lian atmosphere; that Ogil vie is striving to
acclimatise the sonnet; and it is pleaded, seem-
ingly, that his last two lines,
The lifeless form bound to the saddle-gear,
The blood-drops falling on the stirrup-bars,
extenuate a metrical exercise which realises none
of the sonnet-ideals of dignity, heart, or grace.
Possibly the Bush will yet furnish us with a read-
able sonnet, “a little picture painted well” —and
little more. But it seems a sin to encourage this
hope at the cost of irregular verses upon themes
that do not suit an arbitrary form—and at the
risk of transforming an uncommonly fine lyrist
into a very ordinary sonneteer. For the methods
are dissimilar, and it seems more than possible
that the man who'meddles with artificial forms
will lose the spontaneity that is essential in the
lyrical poet, and sounds in every line of Ogilvie’s
—excepting, always, his lamentable “sonnet.”
All this, as timely introduction to some views
upon the sonnet as Australians have it. Dismiss
Kendall (for all save the thoughtful and melodious
“Rest”), on the grounds that his attempts were
colorless and in most instances irregular, and we
are left almost entirely at the mercy of the new-
comers. Of'this lately-risen, importunate band,
Arthur Adams sounds the deepest note —gets near-
est to the resounding bass chords of the Miltonic
organ. His “A.D. 1895” gives but a hint of
power—
How the line
Shall find in one fierce, vast convulsion, vent :
grates upon one’s sense of music, though, when
the sonnet is read, for sake of comparison,
between Wordsworth’s “Earth has not anything
to show more fair ” and Rossetti’s imperishable
“Sonnet for a Venetian Pastoral”,! Still, the
Maorilander is young, and progressive. He treats
his sonnet upon this year’s doings in China with
more adequate art. And, only for the interde-
pendence of each upon the other, there is good
work, also, in his series on the four cities of Maori-
land.
Next in order, place Arthur Bayldon. Next in
order, because he is not spontaneous as Adams.
Next in order, also, because he has gone near to
defining high-water mark of Australian sonnet
achievement with his admirable ‘ ‘ Marlowe ”
Yet, halt! How little of the poet is distinguish-
able in the line following—
He recked not that a compeer calm and strong
Ringing falsely as a cracked florin, it delivers to
us the secret of Bayldon’s assiduity with literary
hammer and chisel. Any real poet who had
written that majestio opening quatrain could not
help being whirled to the uttermost end of his
sonnet by the very torrent of his soul’s music.
One recent Australian sonnet has impressed me
even more than those already quoted or referred
to. It was printed without signature in The
Bulletin a couple of years ago, and notwithstand-
ing a certain failure of restraint, is a fine model of
exuberant fancy and artistic word-weaving ■
I“‘ ere are other writers whose works demands
erence, stick as Hubert Church, another M.L.
•ser. His sonnets (in a quieter groove that
ght have been Wordsworth’s, had not Words-
rth chosen to throw away the shepherd’s crook
d become a sonneteering Titan) are perhaps
3 neatest and most exquisite of local pro-
ctions ; “ Akaroa Heads/’ too recently in print
allow of quotation, for example. And I have
jmory of some others. Daley’s “Gleaner”
Imirable, but cold), William Gay’s clean-cut,
t lustreless jewels, an old-time triad by Roderic
linn, and a good half-dozen, at least, by writers
lose work is otherwise unknown to me.
From Current Literature :
The recent and irreparable los3 of Anton Seidl Bur-
sts oertain animadversions on music ; on certain
lases of it that are not always taken into considera-
m by laymen, for Seidl’s relations with mu9ic were
>t directly those of a producer or executant, al-
rnugh he certainly did, in one sense, produce music of
le supreme type, with supreme art. But music is far
ore than sound, more even than melody and har-
ony. There is more to music than the ear dis-
jvers. To those that know it, one of the most
nportant qualities is its appeal to the eye. Experts
>ad a musical classic as they do a famous poem or
standard novel, and they skim or peruse new music as
aey skim a newspaper. They claim, indeed, to derive
Imost as much pleasure from what is literally “ read-
ier ’ music as from playing it or hearing it played. One
jams to read music, as to read books ; picking out each
ote with hesitant deliberation, as a beginner cons the
jtters of his primer; constructing the chords laboriously
s a child groups the letters in a word, and combining
he chords into a phrase, as a child builds up its under-
tanding of a sentence.
* * * *
In time, the reader of books learns to grasp a word as a
,'bole without any conscientious spelling of it. Gradually
le is able to take in a whole sentence at a time without
lausing to study its separate words. So the practised
nusician reads his notes, and such a virtuoso as Liszt is
aid to have constantly read eighteen measures ahead of
he measure he was playing. To the physiological psycho-
ogist, one of the most marvellous abilities of the human
oind is a trained pianist’s rapid performance at first
ight of a brilliant composition. We are too sadly
Lccustomed to the übiquitous piano player either to realise
>r admire the astounding ingenuity of his mind ; but
;o appreciate it thoroughly one has only to make
i calculation of the myriad messages and the lightning-
ike volitions required for the playing, at a high rate of
speed, of complicated passages ; for they are written in
;wo clefs and on a staff which serves for any key, the per-
ormer being compelled to alter the significance of every
lote throughout ttpe piece according to the signature of
the key. The necessities for deciding the time to be
spent on each note, the quality of tone to be produced,
the force of that tone and its relation to everything that
follows it or preceded it, or is struck simultaneously with
it, are so appalling that one really ought to forgive the
average pianist for not adding'to'the miraole by playing
with large intellectuality and emotion.
* * * *
The reading of a piano composition is wonderful
enough, but there is something stupefying about the
reading of an orchestral score. The composition is likely
to be quite as brilliant as most brilliant piano pieces, and
it is scattered among a horde of instruments, the notes
for which are written in several different keys and clefs at
the same time. The main theme is tossed about from one
family of instruments to another, and contra melodies of
all sorts and descriptions are thrown in at every crevice.
Different instruments must be kept at different de-
grees of force, and they must express different emo-
tions at the same time. The problems presented to
an orchestral conductor at the first sight of a score for
grand orchestra would seemingl.v swamp the most agile
intellect in existence ; yet the trained student takes up
such a score with the iight-hearted comfort of a summer
girl opening a paper-covered romance to be read in a sea-
side hammock. The musician sits back in his seat at
home or in a street car or a railroad train —or, perhaps,
even in a carriage ! —and reads rapidly and understand-
inglv till the whole place about him resounds and quivers
with music that has no being except in the secret porches
of his soul. Many an old musician is biought to tears by
this silent reading of page after page--of orchestral score.
Music that makes no appeal to the eye is not likely to be
music of much prominence. Music that does so stir the
reader is surely' a sort of exalted literature.

•“Journalist”: A sub-editor advised me never
to use the words Colony or Larrikin. “Colonial poetry ”
is the disparaging label for Kendall, Gordon, Daley, Banjo
and co., in Melb. Public Library.
“ Eulbertie ”: Rougemont’s fables recall Olive
Schreiner's satirical dictum that “ the histories of wild
adventure are best written in Piccadilly or in the Strand,
where the gifts of the creative imagination, untrammelled
by contact with any fact, may spread their wings.”

K, or bull; 2s. (kl. —half-bull, half-caser, or two-and-a-
buclc; Is.— keener, bob, colonial robcrt, or penny; (Vl.—
tizzie, sprat, or tanner; 3d. — thrum, half-Hz, tray, or

course, some of these are not new, but I give you the
whole bagful.

As in some quiet city swathed in sleep,
Where, like a kiss, the twilight lingereth,
When suddenly the earth stirs far beneath—
Just moves, then pauses—and a silence deep
Falls on all ere the second shock should sweep
Spire, column, turret —all to shapeless death !
And white face peers at white face, while no breath
Is drawn, and every heart forgets to leap !
So now across this quiet, dreaming world
The first faint shock has thrilled, and men, aghast,
Wait for the second, whose blind forces pent
Shall find in one fierce, vast convulsion, vent :
When all the builded fabrics of the past
Shall be in ruins on their builders hurled.
With Eastern banners flaunting in the breeze,
Royal processions, sounding- fife and gong
And showering jewels o’er the jostling throng
March to the tramp of Marlowe’s harmonies. -
He drained life’s brimming goblet to the lees, —
Night in mid-Aututhn Bridge and bay and cliff,
Seethed in the moon’s milk, slumber silently.
A dying tide swoons to the distant sea.
The bland airs breathing round our little skiff
Sigh heavily from the gulfs of Space, as if
Faint with the fragrance of Eternity,
And in a web of silver symmetry
Dark ripples weave their mystic hieroglyph.
How glorious is Dian in her pride !l
And near her, with a lustre interblent,
Throned at the zenith see imperial ride
A single star 1 She passes; he, grief-spent,
Pales for one instant; then, a suicide
Superb, leaps flashing down the firmament.

Sydney’s leading Specialists,

DESPAIRING
YOUM MEN.
Symptoms
of Dreaded Diseases which
destroy the peace of mind and hap-
piness of thousands every month.
The great number of men, young men, middle-
aged men and men of mature years, afflicted with
chronio ailments of a more or less serious nature
is little short of appalling. Drs. FREEMAN
& WALLACE have been gathering statistics
along this line, and the result is alarming.
The ghost of chronic affliction hovers about and
haunts two-thirds of the men you meet on the
street or in business. It is the dreaded nightmare
of thousands. When they would make merry and
be joyous it points its bony finder and says,
“Stop—be sad!” It turns light into darkness.
It lowers a cloud of gloom and despondency over
its victims. It makes life distasteful. It destroys
the peace of mind and blights every hope of the
future. It says to the young, unmarmed man,
“Beware of matrimony, for your offspring would
only perpetuate your affliction.”
It is to this class of chronic sufferers that Drs.
FREEMAN & WALLACE address this
straightforward business proposition.
Blood PoiSofy-
This is a most loathsome affliotion. Blood poison is a disease very insidious in its first stage.
Its first appearance is not attended by pain, as some suppose, but when once contracted it is a very
troublesome disease to eradicate from the system. Paint a picture as dark as the darkest night, en-
compassed with all the horrors imaginable—with shrieks, groans, cries and utter despair, and then
you’ll have a picture of blood poison in its last stages. It is then that the bones begin to decay, and
chunks of flesh drop from your body. Your eyes become weak and watery, your throat and tongue
are affected, and life becomes indeed a burden. If you are so unfortunate as to be afflicted with blood

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A sarcastic Chinese yarn goes that when
man was created his brains were accidentally left
out on a saucer, and the cat ate them.. The cat
has been a knowledgeable animal ever since.
Extract from an old time-yellowed Wes-
tralian Government Gazette
Tenders are invited for the simply of one draught
horse. Plans and specifications may be seen at the office
of the Under-Secretary for Public Works.
Whenever the question of a State tobacco
monopoly comes up, as it did in Vie. Assembly,
the other week, the invariable argument used
against it is that there is a State monopoly in
France, and that French tobacco is bad. Yet the
Frenchman doesn't regard his tobacco as bad. It
is good according to his taste, and it is made to
suit his taste. Australian tobacco isn’t a State
monopoly and it is also none too good. Aus-
tralian beer isn’t a State monopoly either, and
most of it is awful. These should be equally good
points in favor of State monopoly. Moreover,
the badness of the French tobacco is mainly a
wheeze, like the mother-in-law joke. Fully one-
sixth of the cigars smoked in N.S.W. are French,
and their alleged awfulness doesn’t come notice-
ably to the surface.
The Prehistoric God.
The picture-tale of the Azteo,
The runic verse of the Finn ;
The sacred lore of the Teuton,
With Greece and Rome thrown in.
Nineveh, Babylon, Persia,
The monuments by the Nile ;
Cuneiform cylinder, graven stone,
And the prehistoric tile.
Hieroglyph of Egypt,
The Chippeway’s ear and tongue;
Avesta, Bible and Veda
Have all attested and sung :
That the firestick god of primordial days
Knew the people’s hearts and the people’s ways.
He dwelt on the hearth where the children roll,
The light of the home and its life and soul.
He fought the negation wet and cold;
And people trusted the god of old.
But we’ve built a god that has crushed us down
With the weight of a grand ideal;
An abstract god for the gods alone,
But to mankind all unreal.
And the ruins are tumbled over,
But never a spark they find
Of the firestiok god that the ancients knew
As a balm for the troubled mind.
Then give them their idols back again,
Tho’ they be but wind and wood ;
They express the peoples’ hopes and joys,
And have been for the peoples good.
Tho’ the tomples be many and many,
We’ve never a god in them all,
And the human heart is yearning again
For that god to hear its call.
W.A.
TMRAY.
To Rebelling Woman.
You are a goddess ! There we all agree.
But you complain, my dear, that our devotion
Is flavored with a homely, pagan notion
About the treatment of the deity ?
Well, I confess, our worship may appear
Irreverent and coarse ; much like the scrimmage
Between a Mongol and his brazen image
That keeps its answers somewhat in arrear.
But, after all, the heathen’s heart is stout
And unremitting in its sacrifices;
Although the fact remains that his devices
Are arbitrary—still he is devout.
As for the Christian with his higher bent,
His grand conception of a spirit-being
Beyond the range of hearing and of seeing—
Is he not just a bit indifferent ?
Say, would you rather be a silhouette
Upon the Vast, ethereal, transcendent;
That in its abstract chilliness resplendent
Man, carnal man, shall worship—and forget ;
Or, dear, an idol, to whose throbbing flanks
He fondly clings, with constancy, to rally
His hope and strength—and that occasionally
He takes from off the pedestal and spanks ?
VON KOTZE.
My Love.
Thy soul is a deep, deep valley, mournful and
pale with tears ;
And I throb through the tremulous twilight
hung over the harvest of fears,
Like a star at the weird hour of evening, when
light breaks the gloom of years.
Oh, thy voice is the voice of a bird, Love—a voice
that I heard long ago,
Who sang but one note in the greenwood, but
burnt my soul with the glow ;
And I choked in the darkness of silence, and
wept on the earth for woe.
Around thee the clear winds of heaven breathe
softly, dear love, for delight;
Around thee the moonbeams of heaven weave
vestures, dear love, of pure white.
For thou art a love-guarded lily, a flower in the
garden of night.
J. E. S. HENERIE.

HISTORY or
YANKEE DOODLE.
SEE
DIXSON’S TOBACCO HOARDINGS.
+
Iq Africa.
ID AOstralia.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
General, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
| heat was so intense that some refused to
: fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ehb, a strange sight
aroused them. Marching with drum
and fife was an old man, Jeremiah Wyn-
ton, and his son and grandson. The
old father, with silvery flowing locks,
sturdily strode through the field. The
son with bandaged head fifing lustily,
the youth, catching the spirit of his
gi’andsire, rolled out with him the soul-
inspiring rattle of the drum. Father, son,
and grandson had accompanied the
troops as volunteers, and were ever in
the forefront of the fight. The fierce
light of battle was in their eyes, they
knew no fear, and many a wild cheer
arose, as on that fateful day Yankee
Doodle was heard as never was heard
before or since.
YANKEE Doodle as a Trade Mark
is admitted by all to have no equal
on this side of the World, and, as
a Tobacco, Dixson and Sons determined
that it should enjoy an equal reputation,
consequently the resources of their great
establishment, and patient trials and ex-
periments (involving several visits to
America in search of suitable leaf with
which to produce the tobacco sought
after) resulted in a Dark and Bright
Tobacco, which to-day CHALLENGES
THE WHOLE WORLD IN COMPE-
TITION, and that it is appreciated may
be gathered from the fact that the sales
for New South Wales alone now reach
Tons per Day.
The objection of many smokers to
Dark Tobacco is its strength. Dixson’s
have overcome this, and Dark Yankee
Doodle, while possessing a delightful
flavor, is not strong. Then again, Light
or Bright Tobacco, although mild, burns
the tongue. Bright Yankee Doodle is
equally mild, richer than other tobaccos
in aroma, and DOES NOT PARCH
THE TONGUE. Every smoker should
TRY FOR HIMSELF.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 14, 189‘J.
On Various Kinds of Grog.
The writer has before him a copy of the
Australian Brewers’ Journal, dated 20th
September, 1898. The Brewers’ Journal
is a weighty organ, published in Melbourne
and Sydney, and understood to be of great
mana in beer and whisky circles. It is de-
voted to the interests of every fluid which
makes a man see two dogs where there is
only one, and one dog where there isn’t any,
and which makes him arrive home late on
all-fours and try to blow out his boots before
going to bed. One page of it is devoted
to advertising a number of books which can
be procured on application to the Melbourne
office of the Brewers’ Journal, and the
advt starts with the announcement that the
books in question “ are directly interesting
and of value to brewers, cordial-makers and
vignerons in the different branches of the
business, and will be found of great assist-
ance in the manufacture and preparation of
the various beverages,” &c. Then it goes
on as follows :
“The French Wine and Liquor Manufacturer.” A practi-
tical guide and receipt book for the American liquor mer-
chant. Tim work embraces a clear and comprehensive
treatise on the manufacture and imitation of brandy,
rum, };in, arid whisky; with rules for the fermentation,
mixing, and management of all kinds of wine, cham-
pagne, &c. ; for preparin’? cordials, liqueurs, punch,
essences, bitters, and syrups ; for finin’?, flavoring, filter-
in’?, and coloring wines and liquors; for restorin'? and
keeping ale and cider, and for the manufacture of vinegar
by the quick method. It furnishes rules and tables for
reducing and raising the strength of alcohol, explains the
use of the hydrometer, and contains practical articles on
distillation,"maceration, &e. By John Rack. Illustrated
with descriptive diagrams and engravings, 12mo. Cloth,
21s.
“ The Manufacture of Liquors, Wines and Cordials with-
out the ajd of Distillation.” This work also includes the
best receipts for the manufacture of effervescing beverages
and syrups, vinegar, and bitters. It also tells how to
make cider without apples, to convert cider into all kinds
of wi ,es, to make strong vinegar in 24 hours, to distin-
guish imported from domestic liquors, to make fine
colorings for wines an 1 liquors, to make neutral spirits,
to rectify whisky, to make old barrels look new, to make
new barrels look old, to detect poisonous liquors, to
become a successful liquor merchant, to be an expert bar-
tender, to make wholesome liquors and wines at very low
cost and large profit, and a good deal more useful infor-
ination for the trade. By Pierre Lacour ; 12ino. Cloth,
21a.
“ The Bordeaux Wine and Liquor Dealer’s Guide.” A
treatise on the manufacture and imitation of liquors.
This work teaches how wines and liquors, from the
choicest to the commonest, can be imitated to perfection,
without the use of a single article in the smallest decree
approximatin'? to a poison, and yet stand the closest
scrutiny of the best judges and the severest chemical
tests. Each imitation is in every respect the twin of the
real, possessing the same qualities, performing the same
duties, and yielding the same results. The receipts are
given with exact proportions and specific instructions,
insuring complete success. Illustrated with diagrams ;
12ino. Cloth, 21s.
* • * *
Australia is pre-eminently a land of bad
liquor and bogus liquor, of kerosene and
potato spirit and sudden death, of muddy
brown beer, and thick, gruesome rum with
plug tobacco in it, and illicit distillation,
and d.t. A good part of the copious
drunkenness of Australia undoubtedly arises
through the bad quality of the drink sup-
plied. And a great deal of the badness of
Australian liquor arises out of the fact that
this country doesn’t take its adulteration
laws seriously. As a matter of fact it takes
very few things seriously, except the
necessity for floating another big loan and
the desirability of gaoling any man on the
bare assertion of any depraved and imagina-
tive woman. According to the letter of
the law, in some of the Australian provinces
anyhow, the selling of sham liquor is
just as truly an illegal act as the selling of
sham bank-notes or half-crowns. But the
selling of sham bank-notes is an offence
against Property as represented by the
banks—if the sham bank-note looks good
enough the bank whose name is on it has to
give cash for it to its own loss. The selling
of sham liquor is most notably an offence
against the Person—primarily against the
stomach of the Person—and Australian law,
except in a very few instances, takes little
note of offences against the Person as
compared with offences against Pro-
perty. So Australia doesn’t take its
adulteration law seriously or attempt to
make adulteration a serious offence. The
sale of bogus liquor is illegal, though in a
mild sort of way, but an allegedly respectable
paper can advertise with impunity books
giving instructions for the manufacture of
imitation alcohol, said to be so perfect an
imitation that it can be sold as the real thing
with absolute safety. If the same paper
advertised a book giving instructions how to
make equally perfect imitations of bank-
notes or half-crowns well, what would
happen ?
* • * •
Yet why the law should take so much more
cognisance of the adulterated or imitation
half-crown than of the adulterated or imita-
tion grog is a mystery. The bogus half-
crown injures no one provided it isn’t
detected. If it is sufficiently artistic to pass
•muster till it wears out, then the community
has been just as rich all the time as if the
half-crown was real. The bogus liquor is
hurtful whether the community discovers its
bogus character or not. If the community
habitually swallowed its half-crown, and
imitation half-crowns were unhealthy and
undermining to the constitution, as well as
being of less value than genuine ones as
articles of merchandise, the two offences
would be on a par. Bug the community
doesn't swallow half-crowns, and the two
offences are not on a par.
* * * *
There are a multitude of reasons why
Australia is pre-eminently a land of bad
liquor. The manufacture of illicit spirit is
one of them. Cheap liquor is another.
The tremendous multiplicity of public-
houses in towns and cities is a third. The
comparatively small profit made out of three-
penny drinks would pay without adultera-
tion if the publican sold enough three-
penny drinks, but where thoro are so many
public-houses that most of them can’t sell
enough threepenny drinks to make the busi-
ness pay on an honest basis they naturally
adulterate. Some of them would probably
do it in any case, but some others certainly
do it under pressure. The iniquitous “tied”
house system is a fourth reason. The
supremely idiotic law—in practice, if not in
theory—holds the retail publican alone re-
sponsible for the quality of his liquors, and if
they are adulterated it regards him as the
adulterator, no matter what evidence he may
bring to prove that he sold them just as they
were received from the wholesale dealer.
Therefore the wholesale man can adulterate
practically as he pleases, and compel the
miserable tenant of his “tied ” houses to sell
his liquors, no matter how bad they may be,
and to take upon himself all risks incidental
to their sale. The fact that the special organ
of the wholesale liquor trade deliberately
advertises for sale books of instructions re
the manufacture of imitation liquor is a very
interesting feature just here.
♦ * * *
There is one short, decisive, effectual, and
eminently profitable way out of the whole
adulteration question, if any of the Aus-
tralian provinces had energy enough to adopt
it —by the establishment of a State Drink
Monopoly. If it was done on the most com-
plete scale, it would include the abolition of
all private brewing and distilling, and of all
private sale of liquor. The main reason why
Australia’s wines are not. yet equal to the best
in the world is that the demand is not large
enough (owing to foreign imaortations) to in-
duce the applicition of sufficient capital and
intelligence to the business. This country can
make its own spirits and liqueurs and beer—
and thereby supply a considerable amount of
employment to its own people, and prevent
a large amount of money leaving the country.
It can hire the best available talent in the
world to supervise the manufacture, and give
a State guarantee of purity. Then, if it
abolishes the private grog-retailer, and makes
the man behind the bar a civil-servant who
is not paid by results, he will have no object
in making the public any drunker than the
public really requires to be. Also, he will
have nothing to gain by adulterating
the liquor, and he will have a great
deal to lose, inasmuch as detection
will mean the loss of his billet and
salary and future pension, and the final
closing of his career as a liquor-vendor in
that province, anyhow. There is no more
I'eal difficulty in making the liquor trade a
State monopoly than in doing the same with
the Post and Telegraph Department or the
Railways, and both these difficulties have
been overcome. And it w'ould put an end to
a whole multitude of evils —the demand for
the Bordeaux Wine and Liquor-Dealers’
Guide among the rest. Also, incidentally,
it would cut off the market afforded by the
private drink-retailer to the illicit manufac-
turer, and in this direction alone it w'ould
save the State an enormous loss of revenue.
The dimensions of the trade in liquor which
pays no duty are beyond all reckoning, but,
as a casual suggestion on this point, it was
recently stated in the course of a police-court
prosecution that the sale of alleged Wolfe’s
schnapps in New South Wales is seven
times larger than the amount of real schnapps
imported can account for. That may not
mean that 84' per cent, of the alleged
schnapps sold pays no duty—but it cer-
tainly looks like it.
The Land-Tax Superstition.
The Bulletin receives from Maoriland a loud
printed screech on “ Taxation, Direct and In-
direct.” It is written liy H. G. Ell, and cir-
culated by the “ Progressive Liberal Associa-
tion,” and its burden is a bitter curse on the
Seddon Ministry for not raising more of tho
State revenue by land-tax, and for depending so
extensively on customs. In 1893, according to
the figures of the Progressive Association, the
Maoriland land-tax yielded £297,000 ; it now
yields only £207,000. The customs revenue, in
1893, was £1,642,000 ; in 1898 it was £1,935,000.
And the inference of the pamphlet is that, as
direct taxation is a democratic idea and in-
direct taxation isn’t, therefore the Seddon
Government, which depends more and more on
indirect and less and less on direct taxation, is
an undemocratic Government and one to be
viewed with suspicion. Further, the Progres-
sive Liberal Association offers these suggestions
among a lot of others :
The total unimproved value ot the land, accord in if to
the last valuation, is £‘84,401 ,‘224, but of course this is not
the taxable value, as there are exemptions under the
Land Tax Assessment Act, included in which is the ex-
emption of £5OO, which applies to all holdings the unim-
proved value of which does not exceed £l5OO. After that
it commences to decrease, and finally disappears when
£2500 is reached, lint some idea of the enormous value
of land exempted under it may be gathered from the fact,
according to the Official Year Book, that while there are
over 90,000 land-owners in the colony only about 13,000
pay any land-tax. As is well understood, population and
the facilities for transit by rail and road are the chief fac-
tors in creating land.values. In bringing people to this
country we have incurred a liability of over £2,000,000,
and our railways have cost just on £10,000,000 ; total,
£18,000,000. Then there are the millions to be added
which have been spent on roads, etc., all tending to create
the land values, which contribute so small a'share of the
interest on our public debt, while so much is left to be
paid by the people in enormous duties on the necessaries
of life. . . . The next step we should take is to do away
with the £5OO exemption, and with the revenue thus pro-
vided take a step or two in the direction of a free break-
fast table. To gather some idea as to the amount of
revenue that would be provided were the £5OO exemption
removed, 1 have been through the only available returns,
those of 1802, and, taking the holdings up to £lOOO in
value, I find that the exemptions, with the percentage of
increase in value which has taken place added, would
amount to about thirteen and a half millions. This, at
the penny in the £ land-tax, would yield something over
£56,000. Then, of course, to this must be added the fax
on the deductions between £lOOO, and £2OOO and £2500,
the sum of which I could not ascertain ; also the value of
the numerous small subdivisions of properties which have
taken place since 1891. Altogether, I should think the
value of the land now exempted would not yield much
less than £BO,OOO.
* * * »
And yet, after all, there is no special demo-
cratic virtue in direct taxation simply because
it is direct. And the land-tax, simply as a
land-tax, isn’t one whit more democratic than a
tax on imported wheat and boots and calico.
The land-tax shouldn’t be an end—only a
means to an end. The trouble with the class
which proudly describes itself as the land-tax
parly is that it regards its tax as a particularly
blessed object to attain—not simply as a means
to the attainment of an object. It is in the
position of the sun-worshipper who started by
reverencing the sun as a symbol of its pre-
sumed creator, and presently degenerated into
reverencing the sun itself and forgetting both
the creator and the symbolism. In itself, the
land-tax is only a cumbrous but necessary
expedient, and a most difficult and expensive
means of raising revenue.
• * * *
The curse of private land-ownership is the
tendency towards the accumulation of large
estates and the division of the community into
a small land-owning aristocracy and a huge
mass of landless and more or less servile tenan-
try. But if the land can be brought into a
state of equal or comparatively equal division
if it can be shared out on a reasonably fair
principle among the adult community—then it
doesn’t matter a straw whether there is State
landlordism or private landlordism. When
there are a thousand adults in the community
and £500,000 worth of land, State ownership
means that every adult will own a thousandth
part of the whole; in other words, his un-
divided share will be worth £5OO. In the same
circumstances, under a system of fair division
and private ownership, his share will also be
worth £5OO. The only difference will be that
he will be able to point to a particular spot and
say : “ This is my £5OO worth of the country,”
while the other way his £5OO worth will be no
one spot in particular. If a reasonably fair
division can be arrived at under the system of
private ownership, then the SLate is saved the
trouble of keeping an enormous set of books and
records, and much other worry and labor.
Therefore, unless the State proposes to go in
for the tremendous responsibility of wholesale
and complete resumption, which isn’t a parti-
cularly desirable thing in any case, its object
should be to promote the equitable distribution
of the country’s surface among the people. It
should estimate, first, the value of that surface
and the amount which would go to each adult’s
share under a system of equitable distribution,
and that amount should be wholly exempt.
But every one who owns more than his share
should be heavily taxed on the surplus, and
the larger the surplus the more he should be
taxed, until it becomes to his interest to sell
off his surplus and be content with owning his
fair proportion of the country and no more.
And as it is to the interest of the community
that it should possess its own country—that
the country should be owned by the people who
live upon it, and have an interest in its im-
provement, and who are responsible for
its defence, and that the people should
have homes, and not be merely lodgers, the
absentee should be especially taxed, as a
public danger and a public evil. Maoriland
has kept closer to this ideal than most other
countries. The 77,000 small landowners who
pay no tax are something to be proud of; they
show that the country is getting closer to that
ideal state where every adult owns his fair
share of the soil—may these 77,000 non tax-
paying landowners increase and multiply !
Also, the decrease in the receipts from land-tax
is a source of congratulation to every true
democrat—may these receipts further decrease !
In Maoriland, with its absentee-tax and its
scientifically graduated land-tax, the decline
shows that the race of huge landowners is
steadily diminishing, and that the democratic
object and purpose of the tax is getting nearer
attainment.
* • • •
If that object ever reaches its complete
attainment then there will be no land-tax
revenue at all, for everybody will be at the
exemption limit. The land-tax law will still
remain on the statute-book to prevent any
reversion to the old system of large estates
and stupendous earth-grabbers, but it will be a
precautionary measure only. And then, so far
as the land-tax is concerned, it won’t matter
one lonesome copper whether Maoriland raises
its revenue directly or indirectly—except that
the indirect process by means of customs-duties
is very much easier and cheaper. For, despite
all the shibboleths about the land being the sole
source of wealth, and therefore being in all fair-
ness the main source of revenue, it isn’t the
land that pays the tax, but the owner. And
when once the land is divided on fairly equal
principles among the people, the burden is
exactly the same if the owner pays .£1
a-year and calls it land-tax or 20s. a-year and
calls it customs-duties. Only there is this dif-
ference : In the case of customs-duties, between
2 and 3 per cent, of the amount taken from the
people goes in the cost of collection ; in the
case of land-tax, from 6 to 10 per cent. ; there-
fore the burden on the people is less in the case
of customs-taxation. Also, by raising revenue
through the customs, local industries are
encouraged and work is furnished for the
people ; the land-tax, after it has achieved its
object, or what should be its object, by break-
ing up big estates, has no very great advantage
in this respect.
* * * *
The Bulletin has always advocated a gi adu-
ated land-tax which will grow so heavy in its
upper storeys as to make the holding of bloated
estates practically impossible ; also a specially
heavy tax on the absentee landlord. But it
also believes in leaving the small holder, who
owns no more than his fair share of the country’s
surface, absolutely exempt, and it will be glad
to see the receipts from such a land-tax taper
away to nothing, for then it will know that the
worst evil this country is likely to be threatened
with—the evil of huge land monopolies—has
been practically extinguished. If a ,£IO,OOO
estate is loaded with a heavy tax burden and
a ,£SOO estate carries none, then it is obvious
that the latter will be worth more, acre
for acre, than the former, and mere self-
interest will cause the land to be cut up
into the form in which it is most valuable to
the owner—the form of small holdings. But if
the land is taxed on the same all-round plan,
simply because it is land, then the man with
£IIOO,OOO worth of land, provided he works it
as well as the man with £SOO worth works his
allotment, will have no inducement to cut it up
and sell it. It will be worth no more in small
holdings than in large ones. If he can’t work
it as well, he will have no more inducement to
sell it than to let it, and the letting process,
which creates a race of rack-rented tenants in
place of a nation of small proprietors, is an un-
mitigated evil. To abolish exemptions and
tax the land heavily all round doesn’t neces-
sarily touch the evil of big estates. It may
compel the big landlord whose property is
lying idle to hire a lot of men at low wages to
cultivate it for 14 hours a day, or it may make
him let it to a hard-driven tenantry, but it
doesn’t of necessity compel him to go out of
the land monopoly business. It certainly
doesn’t in England; there the local taxation
makes the land-burdens very heavy, but there
is no exemption to the small holder, so land-
lordism remains just about where it was. And
it is a significant fact that every Tory believes
(if a land-tax is inevitable) in a land-tax with-
out exemptions.
* * * *
The trouble is that so many people who once
advocated the land-tax as a means to a vir-
tuous end, have forgotten the end and regard
the land-tax as a virtue in itself. And it isn’t
one. There is no special virtue in taxing our
own wheat —for when a tax is laid on wheat-
growing land it must needs be paid out of the
wheat grown thereon, and it is really an excise
of so much a hushel—so that we may be able to
let the foreigner’s wheat come in free. And
taxing our own wheat isn’t a step in the direc-
tion of any free breakfast-table. There is no
merit, either, in talking of land as if it was a
curse and an evil; it is a blessing, and a most
essential thing to stand upon, and the source of
all scenery. There is no more merit in State
ownership of land than in private ownership
provided the private ownership is fairly divided;
if 1000 men own £SOO worth of land each it is


just the same in the end as if the State, c °' , j
posed of these 1000 men, owned the who e
£500,000 worth-exoept that where no man
can identify his particular woith he 1 J
have less incentive to improve it. ■
above all, a tax on land, unless it » so
adjusted as to promote its equal dist
bution and to discourage the large lai
grabber out of existence, isn t necessan y
a whit more democratic than a tax on lmpoi e
boots. Indeed, it may be a great deal ess so.
A tax that fell equally on all land would be a
mere excise on the products thereof ; it i wa
coupled with Freetrade it w'ould be merely
taxing our own potatoes while the foreigner s
imported potatoes came in untaxed, and it
would tend to starve the local potato-grower
out of employment. And a tax on ini pot ec
boots would at least tend to give the local man
employment at bootmaking.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Way Out.
Melbourne Labor organ, the Tocsin, calls upon
the people in large type and tones of bitter con-
demnation to remember that the Turner
Government “ abandoned One Man One V ote
at (he dictation of the Legislative Council”—
which, so far as the past session is concerned,
is a true bill, and not disputed by anybody.
But the Tocsin doesn’t say what the Turner
Government ought to have done instead ot the
aforesaid abandonment. It pushed the One
Man One Vote Bill through the Assembly, but
the Council flatly refused to pass it except
with alterations which utterly destroyed its
purport and defeated the object in view. 1 he
Turner Government couldn’t dissolve the
Council because it isn’t dissolvable—it is a per-
petual body. It couldn’t swamp it by appoint-
ing new members, as the Reid Government
could in dST.S. YVV, for the Victorian M’s.L.C.
are elected, not nominated. It couldn’t slop
the Council’s salary, for it hasn’t any. It can t
reform the Council unless tbe Council passes
the Reform Bill, which it won’t do, unless some-
one can invent a strong inducement or a,
method of absolute compulsion. It can’t
make the people elect more democratic
Councillors ; even the Labor-party never lakes
the trouble to put up a democratic Council-
can didate in its own stronghold—Melbourne.
It there is any place and any organisation
which could propel a few decent democrats into
the House of 48 Rich Landlords, Melbourne is
the place and the Labor-party is the organisa-
tion. Yet it never makes tbe attempt possibly
because there is no salary attached to the billet
of M.L.C. —and Job Ham and Robert Reid,
and tbe rest of the old, dreary cattle go back,
election after election, unopposed. The Tocsin
says as distinctly as possible that 1 URNER
shouldn’t have abandoned the One Man-One-
Vote Bill at the dictation of the Council.
Well, what should he have done ? What would
the Tocsin have done if it had been Turner?
It is no good saying vaguely that it would have
fought the matter out ; the question is exactly
how it would have fought the matter out.
And it is no earthly use saying vaguely
that Turner should fight his Upper House
as Kingston is doing in S.A. In S.A. the
Councillors are paid, and there is no property
qualification for members, so Kingston can
find abundance of democratic candidates to run
against the present set of fossils when once lie
has a rousing battle-cry to go to the country
with. Also, the S.A. Labor-party always does
its best to storm the Legislative Council. If the
Tocsin knows exactly how Turner should have
brought the awful Vic. House of Obstruction
to reason it should explain tbe modus operandi
for the good of the country at large. Victoria
is groaning for some information as to what
should he done with its 48 Snags, and valuable
knowledge like that shouldn’t be bidden. It it
doesn’t know, then it is a mean thing to blame
Turner for not knowing either. The Bulge-
tin can see three possible ways of making the
Council reform itself. One is to pass a Bill
through the Assembly making the Council a paid
body with no property qualification for either
members or electors ; to send it up to the
Council ; and to start a first-class iusuiiac-
tion and intimate that unless the Bill
passes all its stages in one day the Coun-
cil’s 48 beads will go down the street on long
poles. But it is 1000 chances to 1 that the
people wouldn’t insurreot worth a straw, and
Turner probably doesn’t want to be hanged
just yet. Another idea is to raise a great
Democratic Association and a campaign fund of
at least .£lOO,OOO, and find a candidate willing
to vote for the Reform Bill for every Council
seat, and get him in by sheer force of organisa-
tion, and keep him square after he is in by pay-
ing him a handsome salary till the object is
accomplished. But tbe IoCSIN party makes
not a semblance of a move to provide either the
fund or tbe association. A third way—pro-
bably tbe cheapest in the end—would be to
send up the Reform Bill with a proviso that
the present M’s.L.C. would receive, on going
out, a life pension of .£lOOO a year each from
their grateful but exasperated country. Id
other words, tbe cheapest way would probably
be to pay the prosenL Council to abolish itself.
There is no short and easy way out of the great
permanent and political deadlock by which
Victoria is afflicted. But it is always easy to
be that kind of cheap democrat who curses the
other fellow for not finding a convenient way
out.

Tlie Austrian Influx.
The sudden resolve of the Maoriland Govern-
ment to shut down ou the influx of Austrian
gum-diggers led—naturally—to a vigorous
raising of Cain, he being the weary person who
is generally raised at such moments. Gum-
digging i s a peculiar industry. Ou certain
dreary flats of the northern part of the North
Island the old men, and wrecks, and drunks,
and scum, and remittance-men, and other
strange derelicts, potter about jabbing pikes
into the earth in search of kauri gum, and (Hid-
ing it occasionally. In addition to the wrecks
and the scum a considerable number of healthy
and reasonably moral people make their pre-
carious crust and cheese at this same indu try.
A few years ago some Stray Austrians—mostly
from the southern part of the great mongrel
Holy Roman Empire—drifted that way and
found gum, and wrote to their relatives
as to the advantages of the gurn-faelds
as a place where a big healthy citizen
with a pike could make a living—as much
of a living as an Austrian peasant wants. And
as a consequence the Austrian influx became
a steady invasion. The new aliens, as a
rule, were good hunidii raw niateiial; as
law-abiding as other people ; clean in moder-
ation ; sober in ditto ; as honest as anybody
else ; industrious : and the sort of men who, it
Maoriland is ever invaded, will probably make
as good a show with a bayonet as any other
citizens. But there were too many of them,
and they were too sudden ; they were all poor,
and mostly ignorant, and the necessity for so
many men getting immediate work at any
wages lowered the wage-rate and tilled the
labor-market with unemployed. It was a vio-
lent inrush of employes with no corresponding
inrush of employers, and Maoriland naturally
began to kick. Then the Government did the
right thing in the wrong way—it made the
foolish mistake of shutting its ports without
notice and refusing to let the latest consign-
ment of Austrians come ashore, and, as a result,
these belated citizens of the Holy Roman Em-
pire are being carried about from port to
port and know not what to do next.
Also, there are furious appeals _ by Aus-
trian Consuls for the British Em pile to
come out and squash Maoriland, and for the
Austrian empire to prod the British empire
from behind and keep it up to the nuuk. As
to the legal aspect of the question, there is
hardly any doubt that the Maoriland Govern-
ment is in the right. There is no law which
exactly fits the case, but the right of any stale
to prohibit the entrance of any persons whom
it regards as undesirable rests on such very old
custom that there is uo absolute need for a law.
Yet the situation is a grievous muddle all the
same. Cheap Austrian, or cheap alien 01 any
kind, is certainly undesirable, hut instead or
shutting the door in a sensational fashion with-
out notice the Maoriland Government should
have sent along an intimation three months
ago that the front portal was going to bang on
a certain date. It knew of the influx thou just
as much as it does now, and it has known of it
for two or three years past; there is no sudden
emergency just now which has to be met on the
instant. By giving notice it would have shut
out the present cargo just the same, and made
less fuss and less international row. As it is,
the present crowd has a genuine grievance. It
thought the door was open because it had
always been open and no notice of closure bad
been issued. And now it is a crowd of vagrants
on the high seas and doesn’t know what to do
next.
Che Sanclgroper’s Late Repentance.
The Westralien press, which mostly crowed
itself hoarse about the glorious influx of foreign
capital while the boom was on, now begins to
agree with The Bulletin in its contention that
foreign capital is, in the end, a delusion and a
curse. The “ influx” is all right provided the
owners of the money come with it and settle in
the country, for, in that case, the capital ceases
to lie foreign ; but the investment of absentees
cash is only an influx of debt and the
importation of liabilities. In Westralia the
importation of foreign capital has piactically
ceased, and the principal has vanished out of
Westralia as completely as if it had never been,
but the interest goetli on for ever. So the
Boulder Star talks in this fashion :
When the gold-production of Western Australia was
little more than 200,0000 z. a year there was infinitely
more prosperity than now, when it is over a million
ounces. It is paradoxical, hut true, much truer perhaps
than even the surface shows. The reason is not far to
seek All the mines are owned by foreign people, who
are careless of the welfare of the place whence they draw
their dividends.
And the Coolgardie Miner
The substantial and rapidly increasing production of
gold in this colony is conferring, and will confer, no
benefit whatever upon the State other than that directly
derived from the wages of the men engaged in mining it.
The protection afforded to the propel ty of the companies
owning such valuable sources of wealth as the Lake \ lew
Consols, Ivan hoe, and other mines is granted without the
slightest acknowledgment on the part of those to whom
the service is rendered, and instead of the countiy
receiving any practical good from the raising of the gold,
by the circulation of its value in the community, the
Whole of the metal, except the moiety required to cover
the cost of producing it, goes to pay dividends to share-
holders who are residing in Great Britain, America, and
elsewhere, and to swell revenues of those countries.
And even the painfully-respectable and ortho-
dox Western Mail (Perth) breaks out in this
awful manner :—•
West Australians will not be satisfied to be shut out of
all the profitable ventures in the colony. While the pre-
sent system of having no local branch-office obtains, the
distance of local shareholders from headquarters will
make their wishes go practically unconsidered, while it
will effectually block local purchases to any extent. Yet
the latter should be fostered in every way possible. The
history of other Australian goldfields shows how superior
are locally-owned ventures, both in their effect on the
fields and on individuals, over those in which the owners
and their lease are a hemisphere apart, and united only by
the thin stream of dividend gold.
Pity is that Westralia didn’t think of this
earlier. The “influx of foreign capital” was
a fraud from the beginning. As a rule, a
stranger came to Westralia and found a mine.
He went to London and sold it for ,£150,000 —
which was the alleged influx in question—and
he went away somewhere else with the money.
The only foreign capital which really came
to Westralia was the imported machinery
which was put on the claim, and a few
thousands for current expenses; and the gold
won from the mine goes to England for ever
afterwards. Six years ago W.A. should have
put on an absentee tax big enough to quash the
influx of foreign capital in the bud, and now
even W.A. begins to realise tlie fact.

The City Railway.
“Engineer,” on the Sydney city-railway
question :
D ar Bulletin. — It is to be hoped that Premier Reid,
wobbled he there or not, will maintain his pre.-.it atti-
tude of antagonism to the scheme for grabbing Lyle Park
for a railway-station. So far, no other seems tolu '• re-
ceived even decent consideration ; that only at the hands
of a party of gentlemen obviously incompetent to form an
opinion of any value thereon. Hence the continuous all-
r.vind barracking ffir ir is curious, at least-not to say
sinister. Yet, puu.ug aside the sentimental aspect,
which has its due value, and the hygienic, which is all-
important, the scheme is, bluntly, an abortion on purely
en ,r inefcrinsf grounds. Wlioin would it benefit ! Appar-
em'lv only" a small section of the suburban passenger
traffic, whose occupation is located within coo-ee of the
Park, and the encircling property-owners—country pas-
sengers must tram,’bus, or cab it to their hnal destina-
tion. Goods at the Park seem an absurdity. The great
mass of the present city seems almost as close to Redfern
as to Hyde Park—what about the city of the future l
Surely Redfern is its inevitable central nucleus. Apart
from cliques, Sydney wants a line railway, not a butler-
stop. It wants a frequent service of fast running trains,
and a dead-end under these conditions is full of fatal
defects. The cost of safety alone would be enormous.
It wants, ai'ain, its circulation to reach all its vital parts,
not be congested at any one spot. The H.P. scheme is
simply shifting the Redfern death-trap one mile further
north and removing a few fangs. A glance at the fre-
quently-published maps fails to reveal any considerable
increase to platform accommodation. Their length is
somewhat greater, but the number remains the same.
The gradual absorption of the whole park would be in-
evitable. No amount of explanation can shift that fact,
How, then, is Sydney to get what it wants' u liter
merely suggests. Take a loop, roughly pear-shaped,
apex at Redfern, axis along Pitt-street, head at Circular
Quay, sides at Sussex-street about, and College-street
about. System underground as far as possible,
giving free scope for curves and gradients, and
minimising resumptions. Two tracks, trains travel-
ling in reverse directions to suburban terminals—
wayside stations, small but frequent, say half-a-mile
apart. Motive power—electricity, avoiding the disagree-
able contingencies of steam, supplying light to cars and
stations, manipulating all signalling and safety appli-
ances. (Some points : Electricity is now displacing steam
in all departments of industry. The time is inevitable, it
is not even remote, when the whole Suburban traffic must
he electrically driven. The Public Works Committee
hardly considered this question at all.) To conclude, no
scheme, in the final settlement, can cost less than a mil-
lion. For £20,000. the best expert in the world in city-
railway engineering should be available for, say, six
months -one man’s head is better than a dozen. Would
it not be a good investment? Meanwhile, let us barrack
against the H.P. scheme, at least uutil the next Redfern
accident.
The Land-Grant Railway Woe.
Thk eternal Midland land-gntnt railway trouble
in Maori land is as fresh as ever, even now. It
lias been settled several times, and breaks out
in a fresh place every time. According to tho
original agreement an Kuglish syndicate was to
build and own tho Midland railway, and to re-
ceive a stupendous land-grant. It never built
more than a fraction of the railway for various
reasons. The shareholders put up very little
capital to begin with, and what they did put
up was almost wholly spent in pomp and
official magnificence in London. Their little hit
of railway was built with borrowed money, and
when they defaulted on their interest payments,
and refused flatly to put up any more capital to
meet their creditors’ claims, their credit natu-
rally gave out, and they couldn’t borrow any
more. Then the Midland railway stopped
short, and, when the term of the concession ex-
pired, the Government took possession, as pro-
vided for in the agreement. It ilidu t get much
of a bargain even then, having already paid in
land as much as the unfinished hit of railway
was worth ; and now it begins to he doubtful
if the Government has really got its Midland
railway, after all. The agreement which the
STOOT-VoGEf. Ministry made with the shindy-
kit at the beginning of the disastrous specula-
tion provided that, if tho eo. didn t finish the
railway in a certain period, the Stale could
take possession and finish it at the eo. s
expense, and, if the eo. didn t meet the
said expense, the line was absolutely forfeited
to the State. The same clumsy agreement
provided that the eo.’s debenture-holders had a
first charge on the line. If they have a first
charge then their right to the line comes hefote
the State’s right of forfeiture and the State
can’t forfeit the line. If the State can forfeit
the line then the debenture-holders haven’t a
first charge on it. As matters stand it seems
that the State, having already paid the full
value of the railway in land, may have to pay
its full value over again by settling up with the
debenture-holders ; in other words, that after a
dozen years of worry it will get its Midland
railway at about twice what it could have built
it for in the first place. Or, perhaps, if the
debenture people establish -their claim at law,
it may he cheaper to give them the sorrowful
hit of railway and start a new one.

Last year the N.S.YV. coal exports were 53,900
tons more than in 1807- Y icioria alone took
28,000 tons more than it did in 1897, so it
accounts for more than half the increase. Y ic-
toria is the biggest coal customer N.S.YV. has
got ; it took 650,000 tons last year—more than
a quarter of the whole export and nearly twice
as much as was taken by the next biggest cus-
tomer. The N.S.YV. coal export to places out-
side Australasia is steadily diminishing ; the
other provinces, and Victoria above all of them,
keep Newcastle and the other N.S.YV. coal
centres from such a slump as would shake the
province to its foundations. And this is just
where the moral comes in. Queensland and
Tasmania have already put a heavy duty on
N.S.YV. coal and these two markets are practi-
cally lost. The only way they can be recovered
is by Federation, which will abolish the duty.
Victoria has just discovered that it has
abundance of coal of its own, and unless
N.S.YV. hustles along with Federation, which
will abolish interprovincial duties, it will some
day put a duty on N.S.YV. coal also, and shut
it out, and by that means so develop its own
mines that that market will be lost just as
Queensland has been. Westralia, too, is
beginning to open up coal-mines in the Collie
district, and it is also liable to put on a duty,
and thus destroy another market, unless Fede-
ration is moved along a good deal faster than it
has been. A huge N.S.YV. industry hangs by
a precarious hair, and is liable to be very neatly
wrecked unless Federation is at tained. Though
Victoria has never taxed imported coal up to
date, it is liable to do so at any moment while
the provincial system lasts. And if a quarter
of the export trade vanished in one act, what
kind of a struggle would there he for the trade
thao was left, and what kind of a drop in prices
and wages as a consequence of that struggle?
The condition of affairs at Norfolk Island
makes it the duty of N.S.YV . Parliament either
to cut the island adrift or, without delay to
determine and define its style and method of
government. Col. Spalding, who now notifies
his retirement, went to Norfolk Island as
Resident Magistrate without the authority,
direct or indirect, of N.S.YV. Government or
Parliament. The appointment was an Imperial
one, through the N.S.YV. Governor, and,
though the Reid Govt, may have been cogni-
sant of it, it was not asked for its <dh ial
approval. The Imperial authorities appointed
the Resident Magistrate one day, and on the
next handed over the island to the stepmotherly
care of N.S.VV. This bad beg.uning, so far as
intelligible control by N.S. W. is concerned, has
made itself worse by go ugon, until the question
of holding the island properly or culling its
moorings from N. 8. Wales has become
importunate. Two and a-half years ago,
on the occasion of Railway-Commissioner
Oliver’s official visit there, an ex-magis-
trute proposed “That it be represented lo the
Governor that it is not desirable for Norfolk
Island to be annexed to N.S. W.” Mr. Oliver
declined to accept the proposition, but found
as the result of his inquiries that maladminis-
tration of justice was rampant and crime of all
sorts in excess. Under the best of circum-
stances N.S. W. would find the proper control
and guidance of these islituders more thciu ditii*
cult” The uncertainty of the islanders at pre-
sent as to the due limits of N.S.W. authority
over them and the repugnance of many to
acknowledge that authority at all, as well as the
unenlightened view N.S.W. Parliament takes
of its duties and responsibilities in the matter,
make the current attempt at Government a
sham and a shame.

In Southern California, not long ago, ono Eubanks was
haii"r<l for the murder of his daughter. It would seem

she charged with having assaulted her. On her testimony
tiie unfortunate man was sentenced to 12 years m tne

facts have been brought-to light because Miss Eubanks
iias just threatened to send another man to the peniten-

are males carefully to eschew Miss Eubanks' society. A

whenever a girl chargod a man with bang the author 0/

“ Jack Siiay ” on au industrial scandal: —
In Sydney, during last live years, about 20 men in the
painting trade alone have been killed or hopelessly injured
through scaffold accidents. In this trade competitive
nriees forbid the use of any but the most make-shift scaf-
fold appliances, and that accidents are not even com-
moner is more due to the care of the m.en than the fore-
thought of the masters. A visit to the yards of most
Sydney master-painters would show some scaffolding that
ought to procure its owners six months “ hard.” trestles
rotten and loose in all the joints ; steps with a mass ot
bits of additional wood nailed on to keep them together ;
planks full of splits, knots and saw cuts. While, as for run-
ning tackle-blocks worn out and fallout asunder ; ropes
the hearts of which are ground to powder with long and
heavy usage. Then the scaffold is as often as no* a
played-out ladder with a few boards on it ; and when it
breaks, as it did in two lamentable recent instances—
well “ it’s the man’s own fault for getting on it. the
man however, has the*choice between that and knocking
off to starve. Badl.v-needed is a scaffolding-inspector
with power to smash-up any faulty gear ; and, until such
is appointed, sensation-mongers can always rely on a
periodical “ Serious Scaffolding Accident. One Sydney
linn (A Dkan and Sons) tests all its scaffolding before
use and vet even its shop lias not been free of accidents.
Let me here protest, by-the-way, against the fire-
brigades notion that sailors are the fittest men for scaffold
and ladder work. Personal experience has shown me the
error of this. Painters more especially have to hang out
higher than the mast of any ship, not for short spells with
soft-falling places underneath, but for days on end, over
flagstones and roofs.
“Banshee” to The Bulletin:—
Why are not lawyers obliged to study Medical Juris-
prudence? Surprising, in view of frequent miscarriages
of justice in criminal cases involving questions of forensic
medicine, that the matter hasn’t attracted more atten-
tion A medical practitioner lias to pass in Legal Medi-
cine • why should it he otherwise with the lawyer. Ido
not mean to assert that he ought to know, for instance
the tests for poisons, but why should he not know in that
connection what is expected of medical men in cases of
poisoning? Such lawyers as lam personally acquainted
with are of opinion that Forensic Medicine should be a
part of the legal curriculum. Look at the pitfalls, e.g.,
that await a lawyer defending a man for his life on a
charge (say) of rape ? Picture a Crown Prosecutor striv-
ing to get “life” for a prisoner vvhose innocence might
be easily established by defending counsel vvith a
smattering of legal medicine assisted by a medical
witness who has previously coached his examiner?
In Reg v. Kelly, tried in Dublin in the CO’s, Butt
succeeded in snatching a verdict for prisoner in face
of most damaging evidence, much of which, though
quite inadmissible from a medico-legal point of view,
was accepted owing to forensic medical ignorance of
prosecutin'' counsel. Butt knew his work, hut he did
not rest at that,. He had himself coached in anatomy
and surgery till lie was able in another case to worst W.
Stokes (now Sir \V. Stokes, Surgeon to the Queen in
Ireland). Butt to Stokes : “ Will you kindly tell the jury,
Mr Stokes, where runs the arteria princeps cervicis f ”
Stokes :“I am afraid I forget.” Butt to jury : “Gentle-
men here is a great surgeon who admits that he does not
know where runs the principal neck artery. (Said
artery is only a superficial branch of a larger t rijnk, and
cot its name through some ancient anatomical tom-
foolery. But the jury didn’t know that.) Also, in Butt-
nbr’s case at Sydney the medical evidence would have
been burst up by counsel possessed of medico-legal know-
ledge Mt Rennie case, ditto Suppiei.d case, ditto Dean
case:’ no committal, even, should have taken place,
because the material tested by Govt, analyst was not
properly sealed up when delivered to him. Lisson s case,
again, should never have gone to a jury before experts in
insanity had given an opinion about his mental state.
But that is English, not Botany Bay law.
Outside the cities the management; of most
Australian fire brigades is partly a farce and
most of the other~part a scandal. It is the
same in all the Australias ; the only one that
has made any sober attempt to put tilings right
is Victoria, and the effort there is a doubtful
success. In N.S.W. a local Edre Brigades Board
is supposed to rule its local concerns, hut the
three parties represented—Govt., local muni-
cipal council, and insurance co.’s —continually
squabble over contributions. In Broken Hill,
where nine-tenths of the premises seem to have
been designed and built with the special object
of being burnt, the insurance co.’s struck, and
the board cheerfully replied with a notice (now
three months old, and still in force) that the
brigades were not to turn out to any insured
premises which might he blazing. Also, in
South Australia, at Mt. Gambier, ructions of a
similar kind have been followed by the locking-
up of the fire-station. And now there is trouble
at Newcastle.
