“ Maritana ” was the second production of
the opera season at Sydney Lyceum, and in most
respects the tale of that gentle Spanish beggar-
maid was a very fine production all through. The
present writer didn’t go to see it on the opening
night, being mindful of the way “Carmen,” on its
first appearance, jolted in a springless fashion over
the rough roads of old Spain ; but on Saturday he
discovered “Maritana” to be a show which it
would have been a pleasure to meet anywhere.
An interested party of the most unreliable charac-
ter also assured him that it had been quite as good
all along. Farley, with old age coming upon him,
can still make Up to look 25 with ease —lie could
look 20 if he wanted to, for Farley in his war-
paint defies the progress of years ; and his Don
Jose is a very fine piece of work. He is a hand-
some and gorgeously-clothed Spaniard, and his
deep voice rolls almost as impressively as it did a
dozen years ago. Leumane, as Don Caesar, is a
pleasant off-hand swashbuckler, a good actor, and
looks like what any Spanish nobleman might be
when he had been sleeping out in the Domain. A
certain break in his top-note also suggested on
Saturday that Don Caesar had been exposed to the
inclemency of the weather. As for Miss Lilian
Tree, she sings the part of Maritana very artistic-
ally, and acts it exceedingly well, and looks it a
little—just a little. Her stoutness is against her
for one thing, and her persistent wearing of a
gorgeous up-to-date frock in the first act, instead
of the cheap orthodox gipsy costume, is incon-
gruous. Still, the three principals are all artists,
and so is Bert Bailey in his little part; and even
though King Charles 111. is a trifle weak and
Lazarillo is shadowy, they don’t matter very
much. It is a finely-mounted and brightly-
painted opera which goes smoothly all through,
and the chorus and orchestra don’t fall needlessly
over their music ; and, taking one or more con-
siderations with another, it is the bast version of
“Maritana” that Sydney has seen for some
time. If The Bulletin were asked to point out
with a gun the person who is most responsible
for the success it fancies it would fire that weapon
immediately at Leumane.
* s* -;K- *
The forty gentlemen who came into un-
lawful possession of property at Bagdad, away in
the back blocks of history, tramp in their dazzling
procession every night at Her Majesty’s, just as
they did when last reported upon. As the nights
go by Little Gulliver asserts himself more and
more as the genuine funny man of the show. He
isn’t the boss professional funny man ; in a panto-
mime that individual is mostly got u p laboriously
in woman’s clothes, and the end of his bony nose
has a bloom upon it, and he wears a short, crumpled
skirt, and stockings that are down at heel. The
man in woman’s clothes is apt to he a labored
piece of mirth, and his constant resemblance
to himself grows a trifle wearisome. But
Gulliver looks like a bright, fresh spontaneous
speck ; he always suggests that his two brief legs
have run away with him spontaneously, and that
he is an unpremeditated sera]) of the wildest des-
cription. Next to Gulliver and the donkey, the
least stereotyped thing in the panto, is the “ series
of patriotic tableaux, ” in which Williamson whoops
in great pictorial style about the greatness of the
Anglo-Saxon race. Few things appeal to an Aus-
tralian so much as a few remarks about the great-
ness of the Anglo-Saxon race. It always reminds
him of the time when he went to the Soudan on
borrowed money, and came back as soon as pos-
sible bringing a goat.
** * *
On Friday night Alfred Dumpier does
“Hamlet” at the Criterion for one consecutive
night, and “His Natural Life ” goes up on Satur-
day. In the old days, Dampier’s Shakespearean
Friday nights were one of the institutions of
Sydney, and were a boon to people who liked their
Bard in small quantities. Presumably the pre-
sent revival is an experiment, and if it proves that
there are still enough people who like a little
Bard now and then the old practice will be
resumed. And if, on the other hand, the Bard
has slumped, there will be no particular harm
done.
** * *
The business at Sydney Royal seems to
get still better as the hot, insect-laden nights drift
by, so it is probable that Charles Holloway will
keep the present effort going for a considerable
time yet. Charles is undoubtedly an able stage-
manager, and his tale of painted Sin goes
smoothly, like the work of a true artist. Why it
should be called “ When London Sleeps ” no. man
really knows, for nobody except pretty Eugenie
Duggan seriously attempts even to go to bed in it,
and she doesn’t go there to any great extent lest
the beautiful night dress with the frills on it
should get crumpled ; she mostly stands at the
window with the limelight on her, until Charles
Holloway comes in and sets fire to the house by
way of forcing her to return his affection. But
presumably the title is meant to imply that Lon-
don goes to bed indifferently every night with its
midnight lobster on its chest, and cares nothing
though wicked oaptings like C. Holloway are
leading innocent girls astray in the gloom-
some hours. And if London wakes now
and then, and puts its tasselled night-cap
out of the window, and sees Amy Gour-
lay looking for the rich and chirpy youth in the
streets below, it doesn’t take much notice. This
Eaper’s feelings towards the policeman who gives
eavy fatherly advice to the girls that are out late
is still unfrjendly, but that weighty cop has a very
E leasing billet all the same. For he “ moves on ”
liss Gourlay in a severe Christian tone of voice,
and that little midnight pirate looks so pleasing
in her red hat and her red gamp that to move her
on is a privilege and to run her in would be a great
joy. Miss Gourlay, of course, is understood to he
Lost, and so are a dozen or more other girls in the
glittersome hall of vice, but none of them lose
themselves in public, or do anything worse than
have a little light refreshment at the expense of
the fleeting Johnny, and laugh a good deal, and
wave their jewelled arms in the atmosphere. The
halls of vice on the stage are very harmless, as a
rule. In fact, the opprobrium is so carefully kept
out of sight in a back room that it is difficult at
times to feel quite sure that there is any oppro-
brium at all.
** * *
Ajax, the gentleman who has pawned his
bones and mislaid the ticket, and the other people
alluded to last week, are piling up many shekels
for Harry Rickards at the Tivoli. At the Palace
the soft, persuasive coon melody wails gently in the
evening air, and the Jubilee Singers call on the
Lord to deliver Daniel—which is needless, for
Daniel was delivered before the first coon song was
invented, and ask irrelevant questions in song such
as “ Did not old Pharaoh get lost ? ” —just as if the
whole race of Pharaohs wasn’t lost 2500 years ago.
“ The Strategist ” hasn’t been removed out of the
Alhambra bill so far, and the Royal Standard co.
is taking a turn at “ My Partner.”

The prime joke of the Sampson fiasco, at
Melb. Opera House, was in the fact that 14 able-
bodied stevedores could seriously believe the
“ Strong (show) Man ” capable of attempting to
beat them in a tug of-war. They might have ex-
pected that the “challenger” would change his
tune when cornered. Instead of a tug-of-war
Sampson proposed to do with a couple of iron
pipes something very like what the “ Georgia
Magnet ” does with a billiard-cue. The 14 burly
stevedores were to push or pull at the pipes on
either side with a view to shifting Sampson, the
while that he worked the “ force-direction ” trick,
one supposes. Nothing came of his proposal, so
the true inwardness of the Sainpsonian scheme re-
mains shrouded in mystery.
* * * *
Sampson shows himself unwise in his
revolving - platform business —• so obviously a
“ fake ” that it irritates the audience. The break-
ing of two chains with his fist is another preten-
tious display, which weakens on acquaintance.
He doesn’t take up a strong position for dealing a
mighty blow, and his careful manner of taking
aim at two particular links in the chains suggests
that they are especially vulnerable. The best of
Sampson’s work is the duinb-bell-lifting. Cynics
may take a discount off the alleged weight of the
implement, and they may also assume that the
length of the steel bar gives the lifter a better
chance than he would have with a more orthodox
bell, but there remains the dead certainty that
some of Sampson’s muscles are quite out of the
common even among athletes.

A recent St. Paul’s has a fine portrait of Daisy Holly,
now dancing with much success in India. Daisy is the
much-photographed woman of Australia. Nearly- every
photographer in Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide’has hail
her before the camera, and English and American publi-
cations frequently reproduce her pretty face. She is a
native of Victoria, nearly 20 years of age, and distinctly
one of Australia’s beauties. Miss Holly, about the
cleverest danseuse produced by Australia, has scored
heavily in India, where she was taken by Tommy
Hudson with his latest company. Calcutta Critic speaks
of her as “ A perfect dream, both as a woman and a
dancer,” and other notices are equally glowing. Another
instance of an Australian finding abroad the appre-
ciation denied at home.
Hear Bulletin. —A mummer “ Admirer” says “ Wilson
Barrett is sft. Bin. Anyone who calls him sft. 4in. has
the jaundice. The profession recognises that his stage
figure lias been the model of symmetry.” Well, it was a
London costumier who spoke of Wilson Barrett to a
newspaper interview-er as “an undersized man of about
sft. 4in.” Can’t say whether the costumier was jaun-
diced, never having seen Barrett out of his delusive
stage-boots. As for the profession’s recognition of the
actor's symmetrical figure in the past, I would like to
know whether a man’s head is included in his figure, and,
if so, whether the almighty profession doesn’t think Bar-
rett's head and ueck a little too big for his body ? Also,
can the profession kindly enlighten ignorant playgoers
as to whether Barrett was a model of symmetry in his
stockings, for, in that case, one would suppose that the
artificial lengthening of his legs tended to spoil the
model. Anyway, Australia cares nothing about the
figure of Barrett as it appeared—hoots or no boots—long
time before he came to this country. The Bulletin's
par. in “Pov. Point” dealt with the Barrett of last year,
whose stage proportions were manifestly “out of draw-
ing.”—Non-Admirer.
Amy Roselle would have been an unrivalled Madame
Sans-Gdne. No scraggy actress, however clever, could he
as satisfactory as Mrs. Brough in the part. She is good
enough. But think of Amy Roselle Daere's qualifications
—buxom, magnetic, powerful, jolly when so disposed,
and a head-mistress of her art.
A pretty little silent effect is introduced into “The
Geisha” every evening by Miss Beatrice Perry. Two
Perry sisters were imported—Miss Florence for the name-
part, and the charming 18-year-old Beatrice, who was
presumably a family stipulation in the contract. On the
stage they are much the same-looking girl, by the way.
When the Geisha sister is about to make her first, appear-
ance, Miss Beatrice has to toddle on with a cushion which
she places in readiness for Miss Florence, and her drop-
ping of the cushion is always the signal for some applause,
whereat she modestly shakes her head to disclaim the
distinction of being the more important Miss Perry.
There’s pathos in that head-shake. It seems to say,
“ Please don't take any notice of me. I’m nobody.”
The joke in “ Madame Sans-GCne ” that goes down best
with the audience is that when the ennobled washer-
woman is retorting on those who taunt her with her
laundry antecedents. “It is true that I washed
chemises,” says she ; “but that was in the days when
the Emperor’s sisters had no chemises to wash.” The
joke is a new version of one that is told concerning a
wealthy soap-boiler’s grand daughter in England. “ Her
grandfather made his money out of soap,” sneered a
peer’s daughter. “ When yours had not learned the use
of it,” was the quick reply of the girl, who overheard the
remark.

Dear Bulletin. — l witnessed “Face’s”
s.s. Brighton incident. The girl was too impatient
to take the gangway. She climbed the wharf
rails, jumped for the top of the paddle-box, and
caught the hands of a man who failed to hold her,
so down she fell. An elderly man instantly
jumped in “all standing,” and was promptly
followed by a straw-hatted young fellow. Then
the crowd made a mad rush for that side of the
vessel and canted her over so that if any more
rescuers were wanted they would have had to
shove a mass of gaping women and young male
females into the water to get there.- Manly.
The precocious Australian female has already
got in her fine work regarding the Gatton tragedy.
This time, the imaginative lady was only nine
years old, and lived at Greenmount, Q. She
rushed home and told her flabbergasted parents
that she had barely escaped from a ferocious
maniac, who made strange noises, and desired to
do at Greenmount as he had done at Gatton.
And she gave full details from the depth of her
nine-year-old imagination. The man was old,
with a long grey beard ; he w'as covered with
blood and bruises, and carried a very long knife;
he was dressed in so-and-so, and said such-and-
such horrible things that the shud-
dered to repeat them. The Clifton Mounted In-
fantry was immediately called out and scoured
the country; three policemen were quartered at
the farmhouse ; and the neighborhood was put in
a state of siege. When the district had been
searched without results, it occurred to somebody
to ask whether the nine-year-old hadn’t invented
the whole yarn ; and the nine-year-old ultimately
admitted she had—she wanted to be a heroine of
“a tragedy.” Then the Mounted Infantry were
solemnly disbanded ; the police force cursed and
rode home ; and the show burst-up. But it did
the nine-year-old great credit while it lasted.

South Australia just now is suffering from
copper booms. As a result of the advance in prices all
the old familiar silver bounders have turned up in King
William-street with splendid specimens of bine carbonates
and green oxides taken from some wonderful show * up
North,” and all the shop-windows are full of beautiful
lumps’ of ore certitled as having assayed 60 per cent,
copper, balance consisting of gold and silver. Lots of old
abandoned silver-mines arc being resuscitated as copper-
shows, under new names, ready for exploitation.

The Ballarat Woollen Mills lately called for
competitive designs and tenders from builders of steam
engines, that of the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
South Melbourne, being accepted. These engines are of
the compound surface condensing type, with cylinders
17” and 38" by 60" stroke, and will indicate 700 h.p. on a
consumption of 151 b. steam per hour. The design is a
handsome and massive one, and they are probably the
finest example of engine-building yet produced in the
colony.*

Adelaide, and Perth, the sole agent in Australia for J. and
E. Hall's patent carbonic anhydride refrigerating and

summer season. The cost of refrigerators to produce

tons of ice. The patent carbonio anhydride system has
been adopted by the Victorian and other Australian
Governments ; also by all the leading steamship com-
panies, meat companies, dairies, and butchers of the
world.*

Up to 31st Dec., 1898, the revenue of the six
Australian provinces showed as follows :
Increase over cor-
responding period
of 1897.

Decrease as com-

Westralia (6 months) .. £149,803
The N.S.W. revenue for the six months was
£4,676,981, and the expenditure £4,909,650, so the deficit
thus far is £232,669. This is better titan the situation on
31st Dec., ’97, when the deficit was £430,801, still it isn’t a
very cheering situation. And the expenditure of loan
money is growing to he an awful item. During the half-
year N.S.W. got tit rough £953,841 of borrowed cash, as
compared with £767,821 in the corresponding half of 1807
— an increase of £186,0201 And as about 1400,000 of this
went to pay for ordinary expenses, which should be
chargeable to revenue, the half-year’s deficit under a
decently honest system of book-keeping would be over
£600,000. So long as a Treasurer is allowed to charge any-
thing he pleases to loans no Treasurer need wind up the
year with a shortage—if only he is unscrupulous enough.
And since the Reid Govt, came into otlice he generally has
been unscrupulous enough.
Victoria went back considerably during Decem-
ber, 1898, the revenue for that month being 1.90,148 less
than for Dec., 1897. But Dec., 1897, was a tremendous
occasion, when a sudden inrush of probate duty through
the judicious and commendable death of many rich
citizens made the revenue take an unexpected rise of
£139,000. And that pleasing accident could hardly hap-
pen two Decembers running. Still the half-year’s receij Is
are £68,048 above Turner’s estimate, so tilings look well.
South Australia lias started to improve suddenly
and rather unexpectedly. The .1 uly-Soptember quarter of
1898 was only £4035 better than the corresponding quarter
of 1897. But the October-Docember quarter is £42,072
better than the October-December quarter of the previous
year.
Tasmania closed its financial year on December
31 with a net revenue of £908,006. Treasurer Fysh’S
estimate was £913,275, but Parliament knocked off £12,000
of taxation, so Fysh comes out wonderuilly close to bis
expectations. This is the best year’s revenue Tasmania
ever had, being £62,936 better than last, year, and £24,807
better than the next best year, 1891. If Fysh has managed
to keep his expenditure down to the estimate (which is
doubtful) he will have £109,000 surplus to reduce the old
deficit with.
Westralia’s revenue for December, 1898, was
£211,178, being £27,766 less than for December, 1807.
The six months’ revenue (£1,200,672) is £260,000 below
Premier Forrest’s estimate.
• * » *
Queensland finance can show one good feature,
anyhow—though it is a very lonesome feature indeed—
about as lonesome as George Washington in heaven.
Under the Audit Amendment Act of ’95, when the
Treasurer announces that he has a surplus he lias to hand
it over in actual cash to the trustees of the Public Debt
Reduction Fund, who use it to extinguish some of the
public liabilities. If there is a delcit, however, the
Treasurer keeps that. In N.S.W., under the Reid regime,
they do things differently—the Treasurer hands over bis
deficit and keeps the surplus. For instance, in the first
half of '95 Reid had a shortage of nearly £460,000, and
he handed that over to the nation as a present, and it is
part of the public debt to this day, and then he started
fair with his alleged Gash Bas s. Next year he claimed to
have a surplus of some j. 330,000, hut lie didn’t hand that
over by any means.
* * * *
South Australia has passed a new Loan Bill for
£1,733,000, which isn’t bad for the country with the
Second Biggest Debt on Earth, and with a population
which, all put together, wouldn’t make a second-class
city. The Kingston Ministry lias many good points, but
its finance is a disgrace to any honest community—if
there ever was any honest community.
* * * * *
Last year Sydney Municipal Council contrived
to cut down its deficit to £l3OO, whereas, in 1897, the
shortage reached the magnificent figure of £36,520. And
the vigorous barracking 1 of Alderman John Norton is
mainly responsible for the improvement.
* * • •
Why are not wharfage-dues imposed at New-
castle and Wollongong, as at other N.S.W. ports? Vast
sums have been spent on these ports to facilitate the coal-
trade, and everywhere else the interest on such expendi-
ture is charged against the port and covered, in part at
least, by wharfage-dues. But in these two cases alone
the interest is charged against the community at large,
and thus the N.S.W. coal-trade is subsidised and pro-
tected by a bounty equal to 6d. per ton. In other words,
the people of N.S.W. are taxed to “coddle” an export
industry which, seemingly, can’t stand on its own feet.
And as the “coddling” of industries by bounties or
duties is directly opposed to the boasted Freetrade policy
of N.S.W., when does the Reid Government propose
putting this matter right ? #

ney by the San Francisco mail-boats ; in 1896, £1,677,742 ;
in 1897, £2,075,476 ; in 1898, '5,015,000. The policy of
jolly and fat is steadily incre is ng the drain of gold, and
t he stock in the hanks has now got down lower than it was

there wasn’t enough coin to meet the demand.

St. Joseph’s Building Society (Sydney) has made
a still further re-arrangement with its depositors. These
individuals agreed in 1895 to temporarily accept 3 per
cent, interest, and thev now agree to extend the 3 per
cent, arrangement for five years longer. Also any
attempt at fixing a definite date for repayment is to be
abandoned, but the depositors are to get instalments of
Is. in the £ as the money becomes available. The St.
Joseph’s cheerfully acknowledged in its last balance-sheet
that all its assets’wouldn’t fetch enough just now to pay
the depositors, so probably this is about the best they
can do.
Despite pessimistic prophecies, S.A. farmers
will reap a good harvest this season. The State Statistician
reckons that of 2,150,000 acres sown, 300,000 were cut for
hay. The other 1,850,000 he expects to yield 6 bushels to
the acre, or 11,100,000 bushels, worth, at 2s. 9d.,
£l 526 250. Food requirements will be 1,800,000 bus., and
seed next season will take 1,500,000 bus., and thus
7 800,000 bus. (worth, at 2s. 9d., £1,072,500) will
be available for export. But for the Sept, and Oct.
drought quite 20 million bushels would have been gar-
nered.
« * * *
Fraser’s mine, which was the John-the-Baptist
of the VV.A. boom, being not only the first to be taken
over by a company but also the first to start crushing,
has gone back £2039 during the year—chiefly through the
heavy cost of developing the Fraser’s South lease.
£33,940 worth of gold has been won in the 12 months ;
the cost per ton of ore was £1 10s. 10d., the earnings £1
10s. 6d. The position is, therefore, not regarded as past
redemption ; and there is more hope than usual for a
company which as carefully itemises its accounts as the
Fraser’s does.

field and a S.A. mine which, despite early promise, have

he has taken up, and that is a good sign.

N.S.W. Mines Minister Cook, who was once a
miner, said there is no such thing as luck. And yet
The Lady .Margaret, out Menzies way, struck a body of
fresh water early in its career ; hut, as tin* liuid wasn’t
wanted and gold was, the former was hauled up and
tipped on to the surface. Presently they struck a reef —
pretty poor, but good enough to mill, seeing they had
“whips of water.” They bought a tn.ll then, and had
just about put it up when the water gave out. The shaft
was sunk another 7l)ft. ; hut the water wouldn’t come,
and drives put in east ami west were equally resultless.
And now there is another battery hung up indefinitely.
Tito Kalgoorlie Goldfields Electromotive Power
Co., to establish a central power station for the Cool .cardie
fields, is incubating. Capital. £500,000, of which the
mines subscribe half, and the London public the balance.
The co. will be the biggest of its sort in the world, and
promises to reduce the cost of working the mines by 25
per cent.

They don’t lose as much time tackling the
telluride and other complex-ore problems in the West as
they did the sulphide ditto in Broken Hill. Already no
less'than live oxidising furnaces are being erected or are
planned—Brown’s straight-line (Consuls), ltopp (Aus-
tralia), Shaft (Main Reef), Konemami (Boulder), and
Angall (Perseverance). But, then, the greatest mistake
the Broken Hill go's— Day Dream, Pinnacles, South
British, and others - made was in building furnaces
almost simultaneously without waiting to see how the
thing would pan out at the very few mines that really
could stand the heavy racket.

Homeward Bound, N.S.W., 1347 tons, L34oz.
Two dwts. to the ton—nothing in it at that —and this

Another move in Lake George shares, which
doubled in value in two days last week and then went
back a bit. Production since end of Nov., when smelting
was resumed, reported to be 92 tons of copper, 5730 z.
gold and 15,9030 z. silver from 6351 tons of crude ore—a
little better average than when the mine closed down.
Everything said to be running well, and total cost, cover-
ing mining, smelting, development and general expenses
at the mine, declared by the management to be a shade
under 14s. per ton of crude ore. This, if correct, is sub-
stantially lower than the previous manager’s records.
The realisation charges altogether should not make total
cost more than £1 a ton of crude ore, and with that, even
with the low-grade ore represented by this production,
the mine should give a profit of 6s. or 7s. a ton, which is
something ou a big output. In the last report issued
from London by the directors serious charges are made
against the man who managed this mine up to some six
months ago, and his friends have been waiting to know
what action he intends taking in he matter.^
As matters stand, the public had better leave
Lake George Mines shares severely alone. These scrip
are the subject of constant mysterious rumors and
market-rigs. The information vouchsafed from the com-
pany’s office is so scant as to warn off all but the blindest
gamblers. . , . .
Cobar Notes : Cobar sadly lacks water; rainfall
for ’9B smallest for years—not loin. Even the Great
Cobar Copper mine is none too well off this year.
Cobar Gold Mines (Fort Bourke) making good
arrangements, but only slowly. Company reconstructed
last April, getting over £40,000 new capital, but next
April will not see it much more than half-ready lor the
start. Hoped that when crushing does begin more
activity will be shown. _ ,
The Great Western, with its dreadful past, has
a hideous future ahead failing a change of management.
Stopped dead the other day with shaft down _oUft., not a
man working on the place. Little money.no pay ore in
sight or to be opened up without great expenditure.
With the few hundreds to be gained from the 3d. call,
it might as well attempt to buy up Mount Morgan as try
to right itself.
Chesney, as good-looking as ever, but still search-
ing for iron to flux its ore. Plenty of good, treatable ore,
but the big step ahead is to decide on the inode of treat-
ment.
Occidental has opened up and had a splendid
crushing from its lower levels. Cyanide-plant been in
full swing, with good results. So far, all goes to prove
truth of prediction here made weeks back. At present,
out of water, like most other local shows, but directorate
wisely devoting money to further developing in mine,
and a better scheme of water-conservation.
Rumored that the Great Cobar Copper Syf*di‘
cate is anxious to get hold of everything on the field.
Certain it is that it is quietly acquiring interests in
most of the mines.
The Overflow preserves a deathly silence after
all the commotion about “ the nice lot of amalgam and
“87 per cent, extraction ” by cyanidation. If the
management doesn’t soon show a bar of gold, it will be best
for its leading lights to mind their sheep and let a compe-
tent manager have a free hand.

Broken Hill mining co. divs. last year, 15.
Broken Hill fatal mining accidents last year, 20. Previous

* * * • •
Still another system of mining at Broken Hill.
Manager Patton brought over from the Comstock the
square-set timbering. The B.H. lodes were too heavy,
ami solid bulkheads of Oregon superseded the sets. Then
fires came as well as creeps ; and mullock was packed in
all spaces available. Still there were creeps. The
quarries (open-cuts) were started by manager Howell to
relieve the pressure—which it did, nut also increased the
danger of tire. Ilalf-a-dozen other methods have been
tried underground, especially in the big sulphide work-
ings. Nothing which involves much timbering close up
to the face is economical, because the heavy blasting
smashes the beams to splinters. Finally Inclined slopes
are being largely adopted—apparently an entirely new
idea, and one of whioh the present manager, Stewart, has
great hopes. _ .

sales are fraudulent transactions, “ 18 carat” rings and
trinkets being frequently 9 carat with a surface wash on
them. •• It is a well-known fact that shopkeepers order
their work to bo stamped far above its quality, ancl the
manufacturer who refuses to do it loses the order. Ange-
lina in general will ho grieved to hear that her 255. wed-
ding-ring is probably not worth more than about four
“ bob ” for pawning purposes when Edwin holts to Wes-
tralia, leaving her with nothing in the house except a
baby. A Melb. vendor of “18 carat” marriage tokens is
even less scrupulous than the immortal Hebrew silvcij
smith who, on finding that he couldn’t get the lion brand
on some electro plated forks and spoons, besought the
manufacturer to work In a leedle dawg which would
answer the purpose of a leedle lion for any customer who
didn’t know much about animals.

If ever a country should be sick of syndicates
and their shuffling, that country is certainly Tasmania.
But the calmness with which the cheerful Tassy suffers
the backing and filling of the Great Western Gridiron
Co., and the readiness with which he finds excuses for
the delav in the flotation of that magnificent grab, is too
astonishing for mere words. Unless, indeed (and it seems
the only explanation possible), the Tassies have them-
selves ho faith in the alleged enormous value of their
West Coast mineral fields. In the absence of any vigorous
local demand for the immediate construction of the line,
is it to be wondered at that Bull looks askance at the
BymlU-Baker crowd's tempting offer?
In the matter of gold-production Queensland
fortes steadily ahead, not bounding forward at an alarm-
ing rate or suffering a serious relapse now and again. In
’56, Victoria produced nearly 3,000,0000 z. ; in '77, only
809 6530 z. Maoriland has varied from a yearly output of
200,0000 z. to 800,0000 z., and back again to 250,0000 a.
Queensland started with about lOOOoz.—two years after
Separation. In *Bl, she produced 270,9480 z. ; in ’9l,
673,4390 z. ; in ’95, 626,2990 z. ; in '97, 807,9280 z. ; in ’9B,
918,1060 z. And there are huge areas patiently awaiting
the arrival of capital and scientific operations.

The old - established Westralian district of
Northampton, outside Geraldton, is busy saying nasty
things about the Westralian Smelting Co., the latter
having declared that the district is quite unable to supply
the lead needed in treating gold tellurides, &c. For years
past vague stories have been told of the immense quanti-
ties of lead which, in former times, were taken to Europe
as stiffening for wool-ships. The co. now affirms that the
“ immense quantities,” so far as it can make out, did not
exceed 4000 tons; worse still, the deposits are patchy and
uncertain. This, after general-manager Kohler had in-
spected. ( ( ,
“ K.” : Re recent Bulletin query as to reason
for different shapes of picks on various goldfields. Miners
here (W.A.) say the shape depends generally on the
blacksmiths’ fancy, though sometimes individual, miners
have fads on the point. Also, in some localities, the
nature of the country operated on controls the shape—
notably the alluvial. A Hungarian tells ine that, in
Europe, the shape of the pick is as diverse as the nation-
ality of the users. On the question of handles, an old
Thames man says that manuka is there preferred to
hickory, because of the ill effect of the loeal mineralised
water bn the latter. In the South Island of Maoriland,
hickory is preferred. At Clunes (Vic.), years ago, only
handles of locally-grown woods would satisfy the miners.

Comments in Melb. papers represent the
appointment of cx-Gov. Hopetoun to the post of
Lord Chamberlain, as a “promotion,” and a
stepping-stone from the provincial Governorship.
Could any idea be more degrading? The Lord
Chamberlain is purely a Court flunkey. His
functions are to walk backwards on State occasions
with a white stick in his hand, and to act as a kind
of official Master of the Ceremonies. No sort of
ability is required for the office ; all the real work
is done by permanent officials at St. James’
Palace. A mere fool could be a good Lord -Cham-
berlain ; and most Lord Chamberlains have been
mere fools, for the simple reason that a man of
genuuie ability would hardly demean himself by
taking such an office. Yet, so low is the idea of a
colonial Governor that it is regarded as a “ pro-
motion ” to be made Lord Chamberlain after
having been a Governor 1
* * m *
W.A. Governor “ Jerry ” Smith has a holy
horror of Coolgardie society. The reason is said
to be that at a Coolgardie ball a well-known
“leading” lady seized H.E. by the waist, and,
twisting him round the room, said, “You dear
old giraffe, I do so love to waltz with you.”
Whereupon the ball was at once deprived of
gubernatorial presence and patronage.

When Excellency Brassey landed suddenly
the other day .at Stanley, Tas., the local folk
hadn’t time to draw up.and deliver an address of
welcome and protestation of loyalty, and the
warden and his council have been kicking them-
selves ever since. The only Stanleyite satisfied
with himself is the local correspondent of Laun-
ceston Telegraph, who had the chance of a life-
time and made the most of it. Thus :
From the landing-stage the party proceeded direct to
the post-and-telegraph offices, after inspecting which his
Excellency was pleased to express his highest approval of
the buildings and efficient arrangements for the trans-
action of business. For some time Lord Brassey con-
versed most affably with the officials, making minute in-
quiries concerning the resources and productions of the
district, lie seemed much pleased with the verdant
appearance of the green hills, and expressed the genuine
pleasure experienced in finding one spot on earth which
apparently enjoyed an immunity from irrosquitoes and
other insect pests.
It almost recalls S.M. Herald’s immortal
“ splurge” over the great monkey-soap wedding.
Is there any valid reason why, in the in-
evitable interregnum between the resignation of
Gov. Hampden and the appointment of bis suc-
cessor, the N.S.W. Chief Justice should be paid,
as on similar occasions, at the rate of £10,500 a

year? South Australia increased Chief Justice
Wav’s salary to .£2500 in like circumstances, in
effect making a considerable saving while the

Waxellency Madden has at last got his
little bit of blue and red ribbon with an enamelled
star at the end of it. He is now a K.C.M.G. He
lias certainly waited long for the showy
decoration without which a knighthood is
like a highland costume sans a philabeg. It
was known to some in Melbourne a month
ago that “the. Michael an dSt. George”
would be given to Madden as a New Year’s
honor. Ind eed, the insignia came out from
the Colonial Office then, and are in Brassey’s
possession.
The Victorian police are taking heroic
measures to identify the lady of the boot-box
mystery. All the Melb. police are now required
to go and look hard at the head (preserved in
spirits) and see if they can locate it. Country
police are to be brought down in batches to have
the head shown to them. (Postmen are being
brought in by relays to study the gruesome dead
bottled countenance on the off-chance that some
of them may remember delivering letters to it when
it was attached to a clothed human body. Also it
is proposed to send photos, of the relic to house-
agents, railway and steamboat officials, hash-
houses— where the morning letter-carrier will
deliver it just in time for breakfast; and to
scatter it over Australia generally.
One great difficulty, hitherto, has arisen out of
the cussedness of girls supposed to be missing.
When the police heard that some Mary Ann had
disappeared from her usual haunts they would
start advertising and investigating, but in several
instances Mary Ann, having some reason for
lying low, refused to answer, and let them waste
days pursuing blank side tracks that led nowhere.
But the police have got mad and aggravated now
over their weary boot-box mystery and are stand-
ing no nonsense. The last lot of missing girls
who refused to show up were brought to light
promptly by a simple little advt. which implied
that a large fortune had been left to each of them.
This worked so well that they all turned up at a
run the very same week. And when they found
that the promised “ news of something to their
advantage ” was simply the glad intelligence that
none of them was the dead woman in the boot-
box—which they knew already—they were so
wild that they were almost ready to smash the
gla<ss jar with the head in it with their umbrellas.
One in particular grew so angry that she spoke
quite disrespectfully about the head.
• • * *
Blackmailing has of late been ca-rried on
with much success in Albert Park, Melb., but the
other night two men who tried to bounce a couple
on a secluded seat made a grievous error. They
said they were police, and talked of outrageous
charges if cash was not forthcoming. The
young woman on the seat pleaded for a time in a
thin, small voice, and wept bitter tears, but,
tiring suddenly, jumped up, threw off her bonnet,
and smote the leading blackmailer a demoralising
blow on the jaw. At the same moment her com-
panion started to “deal it out” to the other
stranger. The young woman vvas all over the
enemy in a brace of shakes, and in the second
round picked his weakness, and left him tucked
up on the grass, counting the stars, while she
helped her mate to knock skin and corners off the
second man. Then the two battered prowlers
were towed to the lagoon, and pitched in where
the mud was deepest. The supposed female then
kicked off the skirt, and went home rejoicing,
having got even over a deal with the foxers on a
previous evening.

You can’t impose on the Jap. Up North
way a certain Jap. inspector was entrusted
with the transmission of the men’s wages to Japan.
His countrymen, on receipt of certain letters from
Japan, accused him of having detained some <£lso
of the wages which should have been sent home.
A strike was declared, and the inspector found it
prudent to hide himself till the money matter
was put straight. Not a man would work till
things were square, and as to “ scabbing ” —such
a thought never struck a solitary alien. No, the
Jap is not an ideal serf, by any means.

Seven Salvationists were sent to prison
the other day at Bairnsdale (Vie) for failing to
pay fines inflicted for parading the streets without
the local council’s permission. This is, no doubt,
all for the glory of the Lord and the good of the
Booth cause, but it looks tolerably mean for tho
Salvation authorities to allow the rank-and-file to
go to gaol for doing what they themselves coun-
tenance and support, and practically order the
underlings to do.

A tale of mean streets. A respectable
but unmarried chimney-sweep in a Southern
city, noted for his good humor and bow-legs,
recently woke up in his bachelor quarters to
find a strange and month-old infant sleeping
peacefully on his dining-table. A korcro of
neighbors was called, and a weather-beaten
chum of the astonished and protesting sweep
was elected Solomon for the occasion. His
judgment was to the effect that if, on exami-
nation, the infant proved to be bow-legged
it should be allowed to remain as an honored
guest ; if not, it must be at once handed over
to the police. The judgment was approved
of by all present, except the proprietor of the
cottage ; but, rather than have any dispute
about the matter, he accepted the unanimous
ruling of the neighbors, and the police were
not sent for. The Solomon, being a family
man, was, of course, considered a reliable
authority on the shape of month-old babies’
legs.
Bill was a shift-boss in a Broken Hill
mine, and the insurance drummer had
been talking eloquently and persua-
sively to him for half-an-hour. Then
Jim came up from his bachelor’s
camp and chipped in : “ What’s t’
doing?” “Aw, tbinkin’ of in-

you ! Goin’ to give t’*old ooman sum-
mut to chuck to nawther man, are
ee?” “Aw! Say, boss, how much
fer two-fifty ? . . . An’ how much
for five honderd? Aw! then fill ’er up
for five honderd. If t’ old ooman have
five honderd she’ll look round bit; if she
haven’t, with five kids squealin’ on t’ her,
she’ll be takin’ any bloomin’ ijut, like Jim here,
ev a stone com’ down on me. See!” And the
five honderd was booked.
On its Proclamation Day, South Australia
still goes down in a body and broils itself on the
white sand at Glenelg, near which place the pro-
vince was first officially established. The old
arched gum-tree beneath which the proclamation
was read keeps as erect as an average gum-tree
can, though long since it became lifeless. Some
years ago a fence was put round it, else by this
time it would have been chipped away—not so
much by people wanting “ relics ” (for it is visited
by few indeed, especially on Proclamation Day) as
by people wanting to boil the billy. By the way,
S.A. might as well decide definitely what to call
its day-out. At present it is printed Proclama-
tion, Foundation, Commemoration, and Anni-
versary Day; the crowd just calls it “ ther
twenty-aith ” every time.
This year, at S.A.’s Proclamation Day shivoo,
the chair was backed by the flags of Austi-alia,
Britain and the States entwined ; at which
several speakers rejoiced. Afterwards the ’Mur-
kan Consul was formally begged to let M'Kinley
know of the “most enthusiastic manner ” in which
the “ influential and representative assembly ”
showed its “ attachment to your flag and
country.” And the consular agent—which his
name is Murphy—replied that it would afford
him the greatest honor ever conferred on
him as the representative of the U-nited States to
convey the sentiments to M'Kinley before-men-
tioned. Wonder if the White House will answer
that.
A comedy ending in a tragedy was enacted
near an Australian city during December. A
young man, of good birth and education, but
addicted to drink, fell in love with the daughter
of a buxom widow. The mother, however,
frowned on his suit and ukased that he must keep
sober for at least six months before he even spoke
about marriage. By a great effort he complied
with this condition, and at last got reluctant
maternal consent to the union. On the day before
that fixed for the wedding the bridegroom went to
the residence of his lady-love, and was so hospit-
ably entertained that he lost all consciousness.
When he came to his senses he found him-
self alongside—the widow. She had carried him
off and made him her husband while be was dis-
guised in drink and oblivious to her identity. The
spouse, on grasping the situation, arose, left the
house and committed suicide.
Many Melb. tradesmen this year blossomed
into verse and reaped therefrom most substantial
reward. A poetic dustman in West Melbourne
cleared £3B in Xmas-boxes, and all the newsboys
seem to have made a good thing of it. Below are
original “ verses ” by a Melb. suburban paper-boy
“ out after dollars ” :
The Melbourne spielers have dropped the
“ rich old uncle in Fiji ” in favor of “ My friend,
Mr. Tyson,” and when the prospective victim
jocularly asks if it is any relative of “ .Timmy,” he
is gravely told—“ Yes, a nephew.” After which
the game proceeds on the lines of tho deceased
millionaire’s having bequeathed .£5OO to every
honest man who can produce *£so in gold, as an
evidence of thrift.

“ Melb.” : Strolling up Bourke-stroet last
evening, 1 was accosted by a venerable, wh.ite-
xnoustaclied, aquiline-featured stranger, • who
effusively shook hands. He looked like a broken-
down Hibernian nob'eman, and was palpably the
worse for liquor. “My dear fellow, it’s a thou-
sand pities 1 didn’t meet ye foive min-utes ago.
A friend who knew me in betther days gave me a
bottle of claret. I didn’t know what to do with
it. There it was bulging under ino waistcoat. I
have a good wife at home, with a horror of liquor.
So I went up the lane there, knocked off the top
wid a shtone, and drunk the lot. But what is
claret without company? ” Then (sadly) “Good-
bye ! good-bye ! ”
Two Cornish John Langdons in Adelaide,
though not now related, are to be made family con-
nections shortly. The charming daughter of editor
John Lungdon Bonython, of S. A. Advertiser, is
about to marry the eldest son of John Larigdon
Parsons, ex-Govt. Resident of the Northern Terri-
tory. The bridegroom-elect is a law partner of
Federal Conventionist Paddy Glynn and a great
grandson of George Fife Angas, founder of S.A.,
from whose daughter he has inherited .£30,000.-
“ Another Dominie ” to The Bulletin :
Re “school-ma’am's” letter, 24,12/’9B. Undoubtedly
the dominie (as “Schoolma'ani ” avers) would he perfectly
safe with 99 girls out of a hundred in a bush-school. But
it's just that one female leper who does the mischief, and
there’s always one in the crowd—and sometimes more.
“.Schoolma’ani ” is apparently of opinion that the 99 pure
maidens can rehabilitate the hapless pedagogue’s reputa-
tion if it has come a cropper over the wild statements of
some budding Messalina. As far as writer’s experience
and observation are concerned, “Dominie” is right-
right up to the hilt when he advises his brethren of the
ferule to avoid being alone with any female pupil.
Further, “ Schoolma’ani” would only appoint girl-teachers
to mixed schools where but one teacher is required.
A pretty prospect for the girl-teachers ! Why, there are
districts in the West where a girl-teacher could only live
in a disreputable bush-pub, half-shanty, half-brothel.
There are many localities where the unfortunate woman
would always lie compelled to lodge with some wretched
cockatoo family whose ideas of partitions and bed-rooms
never got beyond a few old sacks and scraps of canvas.
Writer knows extensive tracks of country, dotted with
little schools, where Japs., Kanakas, Syrians, Chinamen,
and Hindoo hawkers run riot, and—well, God help the
hapless lone woman they come across.

to pursue their ghastly
trade without display.
“Another way” would
be to utterly squelch the
private undertaker and in-
stitute in Australia the
Parisian Pompes Funebres
(municipal) system, under
which the rich have
funerals of whatever degree
of glory they like to pay
for, the very poor being
buried for nothing.

Rc the Gatton tragedy, Harry Stockdale
writes i
As vou will remember, previous to the fact beiiur made
public, I suggested that. Murphy had been shot. My sug-
gestion proved correct, My further impressions are that
Michael Murphy was killed before he reached the slip-
rails and that in all probability there never was any
struggle on the ground that would leave tracks of dis-
turbance. Probably Murphy was accosted on the road by
a man on foot or ‘on horseback. If on foot the man
would ask or perhaps be offered a lift, would get up from
behind, then, standing behind or partially alongside,
would shoot Murphv, and being higher than the sitting
man the bullet would take a downward course. Hie
sisters would naturally scream, and the man would im-
mediately stun both in the cart. Or, telling them that no
harm would come to them, would bind and gag them,
then drive on to the rails where the other man let them
in. During the after horrors I take it that the brother
moved or gave some sign of life, and was then smashed
about the head to make doubly sure.
Further, I believe whoever perpetrated this outrage was
naked. The sound heard by a neighbor of splashing or of
someone washing clothes was the criminal cleansing nun-
self in the water. Thus there would be no stained
clothing to afford a clue. I believe also that the P i tr P ] e "
trators had considerable bush knowledge, and probably
some knowledge of aboriginals, because they used fire-
arms as little as possible, preferring to smash the heads
with wooden weapons. I think that next these men re-
turned through the rails and along the road to the town,
judging soundly that as soon as the news spread all
tracks would be destroyed by the crowd trooping to the
spot. If this is not so the tracks should have been picked
up a little outside of the scene of the tragedy, always
supposing the much-lauded trackers are worth their salt,
or are handled effectively. The perpetrators evidently
anticipated little or no resistance, and one of the most
remarkable features in the whole affair is the fact that no
attempt appears to have been made either to hide or
destroy the bodies. All the care seems to have been
devoted to leaving no connecting link between the perpe-
trators and their victims, and this points to a well-con-
sidered and thoroughly matured plan of procedure.

The fool-suggestors who are abroad over
the Gatton business, as over every other
“ tragedy,” don’t seem aware that for any distinct
photographic image strong light is required,
whereas the Murphys wero done to death in the
dark. Seems, in any case, to bo nothing in the
eye-photograph notion, beyond the fact that tho
retina of a rabbit, promptly killed after staring at
a brightly-lightefl window, will retain, for a very
short time, the image thereof.

“ Reminiscences of N.S. W. and Victoria.’ As
any reasonable person will see who reads Judge
Therry’s account, Lynch was mad ; but that made
no difference in his doom, any more than it does
in such oases to-day. For Deeming was mad as a

“ H.” : The Gatton affair recalls Poe’s
ape. Can a madman have done it? While the
thing looks unfeasible as a one-man job, it is hard
to imagine how two can ever have been brought to
such a concatenation. Were the girls, or either
of them, about to be married ? The crime is
unique.
• • * *
Sydney Centenary Hall was crowded to
suffocation last Sunday when Parson Taylor held
forth on a fascinating subject—the double rape
and treble midnight murder at Gatton. The good
shepherd was particularly strong on the necessity
for immediately raising the “age of consent.”
How the raising of the age of consent would have
helped poor Nora Murphy (aged 27) and her sister,
when they were clubbed at midnight on a lonely
road, the Lord only knows, but the average parson
somehow loves to mouth over that useless and
unsavory subject. Also the Centenary preacher
wanted to know “why our young men didnt
marry in the proportion that they should marry,'
and he said that the answer to this conundrum
was a very painful one. So it is. The precarious*
ness of a young man’s billet in a hoot-factory now-
adays is painful. Still with all that, in 1897, 274
men in N.S. W. married before they were 21, which
looks as if they were doing their duty pretty well.
And in that year far more young men married
before they were 21 than were ever married at
that early age in N.S. W. before—not only more in
actual numbers, but more in proportion to the
total number of marriages—so the young man
seems to be rushing into matrimony as wildly as
can be expected. The good old reverend says the
Australian young man isn’t doing his duty in
this respect now, because “around us of late
years there has been a loosening of the tone of
moral restraint,” which only shows that tho
reverend knows nothing about it. The “ tone of
moral restraint ” may have loosened, hut, the
young man is marrying more recklessly and idioti-
cally than ever, and on a smaller salary.

By committing suicide, detected criminals
make the only atonement in their power to
society—by saving society from the expense, of
prosecuting and formally killing them. By standing
trial, they add incalculably to tho loss their crime
has already caused. There is no moral lesson in tho
trial and conviction. Tho only gainers are tho
lawyers and a few newspapers. 1 n the early days of
Sydney, coach-owners came in for a trifle. Addi-
tional coaches started three times a. day for
Woolloomooloo court-house, now Dnrlinglinrst.
during the days of John Knatchbull’s trial, and
half-fares extra were charged.

That sprightly and dapper attorney, Mr.
Baxter-Bruoo, long the idol of the women and the
envy of the men in North and South, has re-
entered matrimony, the lady being Miss Mary
Joyce, of Claremont College, Randwiok, “than
whom,” says a daily paper, “ it would be hard to
find a more dainty, beautiful bride.” Also, it will
be universally conceded that it would be hard to
find a more cheerful and beautiful bridegroom
than Mr. Baxter Bruce.

On New Year’s Eve last, a suburban mid-
night service was interrupted by a wandering
drunk, who happened in casually, attired in a
dilapidated tablecloth, wearing on his head a tea-
cosy, and carrying a bottle of “stringy.” Stalk-
ing up the aisle, he announced himself to he “a
blanky Russian chief: ” and promptly subsided,
diffusing through the sacred atmosphere a sooth-
ing aroma of stale tanglefoot.

A flippant S.M. Herald reporter gravely
asserted in his report on the Salvation Army camp
at Manly that the up-to-date salutation of the
Salvationist is, “ Hosanna ! How-de-do-dee,” to
which the accosted one replies, “ Hallelujah !
Fancy meeting you!”—and the grave Curnow
passed it. Or did the journalistic instinct triumph
over the Methody teaching ?

Melb. people do want a Sydney harbor,
badly, but they have the advantage of illimitable
beach for holiday use. Last Boxing Day, tho
whole foreshore from Frankston to WiHiamstown
was literally lined with people—about 30 miles of
solid men, women and children paddling their
hoofs in the briny.
* * * *
One J. H. Giddons, a Melb. weather pro-
phet, rushed into print, the other day, with an
offer to expose the awful ignorance of Govt,
meteorologists. Giddons, it seemed, had a system,
of his own by which he could map out the state of
the weather for at least two years in advance.
Having airily predicted how things would go
during next autumn, winter, and spring, Giddons
condescended to deal with the immediate future,
and stated that Melbourne would get a nice, cool
change two days later, on Dec. 30. Unfortunately
the 30th was another blazing-hot day, and poor
Giddons went out into the garden and perspired
■ bitterly. The shock to his system was almost
more than he could bear.

1 “A.S.”: Fremantle (W.A.) <mol was re-
* cently in mutiny, and no wonder. Prayers every
1 morning—twice on Wednesday, and three times
> on Sunday. At dinner, the prisoners stand in the
f main corridor, and their food, coarse and vile, is
placed at their feet. Officers range up. Chaplain
enters. “ Attention ! ” and every head is bowed
as the chaplain snuffles out thanks “ for this food
Then hast given to nourish our bodies.” Then,
“Right about face! To your cells!” The
prisoners retire to devour tough mutton and rotten
spuds, and thank “ Gawd.”
DEAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Silent, silent, when the dawn
Through the ashen room is drawn ;
And it lingers on thy face,
Counterfeits a vanished grace
As the shadows slip away
To the meadow of the day
Does not thy persistent heart
Yearn to all its wonted part?
All the fond, vibrating bars
From the flame of viewless stars
Will not ope the fretted lid
Where thy lovely soul was hid.
Now thou liest there so still,
God has shown thee all His will,
And His universe is whole
Unto thy expanding soul.
Thou hast fled from love and moan ;
I.ittle children here alone
Stumble for the lamp of love
Thou didst bring them from above.
HUBERT CHURCH.
WHERE ART THOU, ROMEO?
In dreary ages past, Fellow Spirit, you and I
Doubtless hovered close together, in Sympathy’s sweet tie,
Maybe we were as Moths, or the echoes of a sigh,
Yet drawn by force magnetic, to live, to love, to die.
I called thee, Fellow Spirit, thy loved form I fain would
see,
A longing seized upon my Soul, to meet, to live with thee.
I care not what, invisibly, our Spirits used to be,
If flame in air, or brute in lair, it matters not to me.
I lost thee, Fellow Spirit, nor have I found thee yet.
Thou promised in lost Ages that when again we met
Your kindred Soul would search me out, your Sympathy
abet,
So come 1 thou long-lost Romeo and claim thy Juliet!
E.I.W.

There was at least one good point about
this New Year’s Day : it happened on Sunday,
and so the straw-hatted stupids, who paraded the
streets blowing trumpets, had perforce to cease
their idiocy at midnight for fear of being run-in
for Sunday desecration.
The man who seems to have committed an
unnatural offence upon the boy Hill before mur-
dering him, at Goodna, Q., will, despite all the
press clatter, be found to have had nothing to do
with the Gatton outrage. Crimes so different in
character are never committed by the same person.
On Christmas Day certain allowances are
made in N.S.W, gaols—prisoners are allowed to
mingle; in fact, pay each other visits. Vocal
music is permitted at Darlinghurst. Last Christ-
mas Day some wag started “ There’s no place like
home,” and the song was taken up by all the
yards.
Edmund Barton, Q.C., will complete his
first half-century on Jan. 18. Every Federa-
tionist in Australia hopes that before his next
birthday comes the wish of his heart will be
accomplished and that the Constitution will have
been generally adopted. Treas. Holder (S.A.)
prophesies that Union Day will be Ist Jan., 1900).
Hanging for rape is in Australia the law
of the land. It is a law which signs the death-
warrant of every ravished woman. “As well hang
for a sheep as for a lamb ” ; and “ dead women tell
no tales.” The prospect of hanging deters no
ravisher ; the sort of man who can commit deli-
berate rape is never deterred by anything.

In the approaching Q. elections the Pro-
gressive party stands a fair chance of increasing
its representation, but very little chance of cap-
turing the country. In favor of the Opposition
there stands the Govt.’s loss of popular Premier
Byrnes ; also the occupancy of the Premiership
by Dawdling Jimmy Dickson ; the absence of
popular men (Philp excluded) on the Ministerial
benches ; and the damnosa heredilas of cronk
finance and cronker legislation that sits on
Ministerial shoulders. On the other hand, the
Progressives are heavily handicapped by the
divisions between the Drakeites and the Labor
men, and the absence of any prospect of alliance
between them. Further, the Labor Opposition,
like the Government, lacks a man of pronounced
character, ability, and individuality to give it
political tone. What is worse, the public at large
believes that the party is badly managed and
badly led—when it is led. The old “Socialism in
our time ” bogie is about played out, but Q. elec-
tors at large have not yet come to the conclusion
that a pure, undiluted Labor Administration will
be a pure, undiluted blessing—and, therefore, the
allotment-pegs, kanakophiles, land-alienators, and
alien-eoddlers will probably rule the roast a little
longer. From six to ten seats will probably be
the utmost gain of Liberalism in the ’99 elections.
Book Arcadian Cole, after much wrestling
with the Federal capital question, has produced a
book called “ Greater Melbourne and the Federal
Capital.” The book throws new, if not dazzling,
light upon the subject, and brings out sundry
edifying and humorous points of difference be-
tween Sydney and Melbourne.

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ Sydney, January 9, 1899.
My Dear Myee—
The Honorable Jack Want and his bride were
passengers by the Orotava for Ismailia on Saturday.
The marriage came off five months ago, but the
passenger-list was the first public information, and
Sydney spent Sunday at the telephone trying to
ring up details. The new Mrs. Jack is tall, fair-
haired, and attractive—she was previously a
widow. Another testimony for the always-wanted
widow.
It was rather a cold water douche to the com-
mittee of the Consumptive Home bazaar when
Lady Hampden timidly announced that she would
not be here for the fair. As the preliminary meet-
ings were all held at Govt. House under Her Ex.’s
immediate scrutiny, the blow fell heavily. Put-
ting two and two together on the quiet, it was
deduced that Govt. House had known all along
that it couldn’t be here for the bazaar. Collapse
threatened until Lady Hampden very sweetly and
meekly remarked that she did feel that she had
been “acting” a little in the matter, but really
couldn’t help it. A few particular people wanted
to sidle out with vice-regality, but the committee
got its muscle up and pluekily determined to face
the possibility of a bazaar without the Y.R. pat-
ronage. The latest development is the proposed
Press Bazaar, to be run in with the rest of the
Fair. All the papers are invited to express ideas
on this point by Mrs. Thring, lion. sec.
Everyone sympathises deeply with Mr. Dan
O Connor on the death of his son, a young civil
servant with a promising future before him, and
a brilliant college past. Another sad bereave-
ment was the death of young Mr. Funckh, the
only son of the well - known foreign wool mer-
chant. While diving at Balmoral he struck his
head against a snag, and died the same night.
Whether the account of the heat-waves in Mel-
bourne and Adelaide has convinced Sydney folk
that down south is no place for them, or whether
it is just a craving for a new sensation, is not cer-
tain, but with the exception of Sutton Forest and
other parts in the Hampden vicinity;, the northern
line is being more largely patronised than the
other this summer. Quite a number of regular
holiday-makers have pitched their tents at Beecroft
and write in glowing terms of the charms of the
spot. The Hawkesbury, too, has been the scene
of many encampments, and house-boat parties are
well to the fore.
Wedding-goers were rather disappointed last
Wednesday. (There are many girls in Sydney
who go to every fashionable wedding as lookers-on,
just as religiously as to comic opera and cricket-
matches.) It was understood that Miss Holds-
worth’s wedding was to be a naval one, as she was
to be married to the surgeon of H.M.S. Dart, and
crowds of interested women assembled at St.
John’s, expecting to see the carriage drawn by
seamen, the bridal path over arched with anchors,
and various other uncommon sights. As a matter
of fact, only two full-dress uniforms were in evi-
dence—those of the bridegroom and his best man,
and only one floral anchor figured in the decora-
tions. Dr. Bobardt (a native of Melbourne) is
very tall and well-proportioned, with a fine,
soldierly (or sailorly) carriage, and, as in the case
of cricketer Maclaren, quite robbed his bride of
the position of central figure in the show.. To the
bridesmaids, two of whom were dainty little Kate
Greenaway figures, Dr. Bobardt gave gold life-
buoy bangles, and the same to the bride.
It is becoming fashionable for bridesmaids’
presents to follow the bride’s, and if you want to
acquire a handsome piece of jewellery without
going in for the wear-and-tear of an engagement
you might as well take up the bridesmaid line as
any other. At a recent wedding the bridegroom, a
gilded widower, presented three bridesmaids, two
of whom were mere children, with gold necklets,
set with diamonds and sapphires. The bride re-
ceived a similar present, the only difference being
that her necklet had also a pearl pendant.
. One of the prettiest weddings on record was that
celebrated between Prof. Wood and Miss Whit-
field on Dec. 27 th. The bride’s parents are opposed
to anything in the form of hollow show, and the
ceremony was fixed to take place in the house—so
quietly that you could almost hear a pin drop.
The little girl who was to have been bridesmaid
contracted typhoid fever, so that the wedding was
even less ostentatious than had been intended.
The bashful young curate will have to look to
his laurels. Judging by recent events he does not
seem to be “in it ” compared with his elders and
betters. No less than three widowed C. of E.
parsons annexed three sweet young things as life
partners during the past month. The first to take
the plunge (for the second time) was a beardless
youth of 40, the second was a well-set-up young
man of 60, while the third was in the prime of life
—that is to say between 70 and 80. In each case
the bride was a youthful spinster. Rev. Cecil
Wilson is not very old, nor is he a widower, still it
is as a Bishop that he will enter into matrimony
next month : therefore look to it, ye curates.
Some time back there was a reference here to a
man-milliner who had opened trade at Grenfell,
N.S.W. Ever since the beginning of business,
this tradesman has been the piece de resistance of
the district. “ Figure to yourself, ” then, the ex-
citement attendant upon this Bunthorne’s nup-
tials, which were celebrated last week at the same
bush town. When a wedding is a man’s wedding
it causes even more ado than when it is a woman’s,
and on this occasion red carpet was laid down for
the hero to tread, every flower was collected from
the parched district to adorn the church, and
no one for miles around could think of anything
but the wedding. Quite a secondary excitement
was the arrival of the new Sydney doctor with his
pretty and popular bride, though this event, too,
gave great satisfaction.
Most people count their words out like pearls
when they send a cable. But an ex-Sydney
beauty who wedded a gilded Englishman, and
now lives in tip-top style in fashionable London,
sent out an enormous message to her family the
other day. She began by announcing the birth
of the heir, and ended up with the statement that
the proud father had presented her that morning
with a diamond necklace.
One can be “ chic ” or dowdy in a bathing-gown
as well as in a ball or tailor-made gown, and, at
present, cotton is “in” for bathing-gowns. Be-
ginning with the humble garibaldi, and working
upwards through turkey-red and other striped
prints, fashion finds its culmination in the white
zephyrous garments lately forwarded by a Parisian
firm to its haughty Sydney customers. The gowns
are cream-color with dark-blue designs—starfish,
anchors, and flags—covering them at regular in-
tervals. They are close-fitting, and of scanty pro-
portions, and bathers who have not imported their
gowns from Paris assure the wearers of these
creations that they, from a short distance, merely
look like tattooed human beings.
The star of the wash-lady is high up in the
firmament this year. A terrific rage for white has
set its mark across the summer, and, as every one
knows, one white dress needs at least two others.
On Saturday, the stands at the Cricket Match
stretched out like sheets in places where twenty or
thirty stiffened white robes sat next to each other.
I stopped counting after the first thousand, and
took to studying all the different shades one
laundress can evolve from this so-called absence
of all color. _ Grey-white, yellow-white, blue-
white, dead-white, dirty-white, brown-white, until
it requires a little imagination to know when a
dress is white.
An artless little laundry bill for a family of two
daughters and a mother was shown to me the
other day. It told the legend of—
GO colers - - - 5/-
20 shert - - -' 10/-
10 whit dresis - - 15/-
And still the Family Herald clothes the Penniless
Heroine in spotless white without a crease, fresh
from the hands of the laundress.
How to live and look as if you liked it on seven-
pence-halfpenny a day, has cropped up again with
variations. First of all, Miss May Manning
sees that there will be very slight need for divorce
courts when her potato-peel system is brought into
force. (Here the ladies at the lecture laughed,
says the D.T. male reporter). If only women
could realise the sublime sacredness of potting all
water that has ever boiled anything, and stowing
all the carrot-tops and fish-bones, men would
never want to part with their wives ! A neat
argument, for the rattle-boned husband brought
up on sevenpence-halfpenny a day, including soap
and candles, would hardly have the strength to
drag his weary framework up the King-street hill
to the Divorce Court.
And now the Sunday Times has appealed to the
country with a variation of martyrdom that guaran-
tees to house the vital spark in any frail tenement
for fourpence-halfpenny a day. I notice that
Mrs. Kirby, the lady undertaker, who is the inevi-
table corollary of such an item as one shilling a
week fish-bill for seven people, is not included in
the expenditure, and doctors’ bills are also cun-
ningly kept out of sight. “Holly Berry,” who
feeds, clothes, and furnishes for eight people on
thirty-three shillings a week, puts down a ivell-
sounding item. She allots Mr. Holly Berry half-
a-crown a week for tobacco, and allows three
shillings for meat for him and herself and her six
children. The paper that can set forth such plea-
sant reading as two-and-sixpence per week for
hubby’s tobacco, and three shillings for a big
family’s meat-bill, would flourish greenly as a
matrimonial-agent, from a man’s point of view.
The things that are left out and can’t be done
without are the most striking features of these
little dissertations. For instance, no mention is
made of brooms, scrubbing-brushes, crockery, and
the little things that will wear out. And what
about the doctor’s bills for those who don’t belong
to the Lodge? All goes well until Sissy gets
croup, and mother has varicose veins, and the
three younger children indulge in measles, and
somebody dies of diphtheria. A bill of twenty
pounds hanging over the family of eight with an
income of two pounds five is quite as black a
nightmare as the two thousand hanging over a
“comfortable ” citizen.
According to their promise to provide open-air
amusement for this sunburnt city, the committee
of the electric-light carnivals let off a few packets
of Pain’s fireworks last week at the Association
Ground. It seems that Sydney really yearns for
a night out (of doors) now and then, and the
patronage ran into many thousands. The visitors
came chiefly from the suburbs where the few
couldn’t possibly live, don’t you know. Still, the
crowd felt itself licensed to be jolly, and clapped
genially when the coruscations came out with a
strong family likeness to Tommy’s boneless birth-
day crackers. So far, the bicycle sports have been
the best entertainment of these evenings, for the
fireworks had a melancholy way of turning back
as soon as they started. The set pieces made the
most ambitious attempts at illumination, and next
to the fiery ships and the light in Reid’s eyeglass
was the glittering buckle worn by the heroine of a
cause cdlebrc. This was made of cut-steel, a good
half-yard long, and quarter wide, and covered the
back of the lady from right ribs to left. To air it
when the night was full of dazzle was a highly
ingenious idea, as it reflected rockets like a set of
mirrors.
At the Art Gallery there has been an extra at-
tendance of country folk lately, doing pictures in
their own subtle, silently-admiring way, which is
a great improvement on’the metropolitan “Isn’t
it lovely ? ” That old Dutch fruit-and-flower pic-
ture that nobody ever looks at nowadays has been
gazed on long and lovingly by big and little
bushies, who know real roses when they see them
in a picture. Lady Godiva and Savonarola,
whom Sydney used to be taken to see in its school-
days, and now passes aggressively, have also re-
vived under bursts of admiration from way-backers.
But in vain do you try to discover somebody look-
ing twice at the Australian artists’ pictures. At
the same time, nobody takes any notice whatever
of the Frame and Queen of Sheba, which may be
some alleviation to local talent. One day lately,
I watched six little bush children and their mother
and father hanging round that adorable water-
color, “Snowdrop and the Seven Little Men.” I
wanted to get there myself, and loitered about
for half-an-hour, but finally gave up, and left
them to the joy of seeing a fairy - tale
realised so perfectly. Anyone who ever cared for
Grimm ought to have a look at this charming bit
of interior, painted in Rossetti reds and browns
and blues, with a beautiful pallid Snowdrop in a
dull green frock lying asleep on the goblins’ little
beds. The goblins swarming round her are de-
lightful things in bright red suits,- with crooked
mouths and noses ; entirely hideous, and yet in-
fested with an .artful suggestion of harmlessness
that goes straight to any child’s senses.
The next world has not been asserting itself
publicly in this city for some time, but I hear that
a wonderful book of “experiences,” by a well-
known Sydney penlady, is coming out soon and
strong. Apropos of which, the daughter of a
lately-deceased Hebrew saw her father standing
by her bedside, the other night, looking the image
of his last photograph. Next day she told her
mother positively that she had been visited by her
father’s spook. Her mother declared she had
dreamt it. “ No,” said the daughter, “he was as
plain as life, and was wearing that black-and-gray
suit that he wore in his photograph.” “ That
proves it,” cried the mother ; “didn’t I say it was
only a dream ? I sold that suit to Sol. Isaacs a
month ago.” Oh, shade of Aristotle!
Lilian Tree is singing and acting so delightfully
m “ Maritana ” that you can even forgive her for
dressing a gipsy in a nineteenth-century yellow
satin evening dress, cut off at the knees. She
advances a long way sartorially when she gets
into a blue silk with a long train, made with
sleeves that start half-way down the arm, and end
at the wrist in the most modish manner. The
opera goes briskly, and is holding good houses,
that thrill nightly whenever there is a hint that
“Let Me Like a Soldier Fall,” or “Sainted
Mother,” or “Scenes that are Brightest” is
coming round the corner. Farley is a little
hardened at the knee, perhaps, but sings robustly,
and wears a handsome suit of tawny velvet toned
up with turquoise-blue tabs. Leumane wears his
fair hair cut straight across his forehead, and
speaks like Cecil Ward playing a “drunk.” The
pretty page of Miss Lena Morton appeals directly
to the gods, who applaud all her utterances with
that wicked, ragged cross between a syren and a
shriek and a whistle. And Rivers Allpress, with
his lank hair tossing in the air, works harder than
anyone with his much-improved little orchestra.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Sassiety journalism loves to dwell upon the
happiness of those households where hubby and
wife seldom gather together. If one or other
wanderer returns to the nest after several months’
absence in distant places, it is beautiful to read of
the family rejoicings. A few weeks of bliss, and
then the two leading performers in the comedy
agree to go on loving apart as before. Very likely,
theirs is a happy union. ‘ Lord and Lady Algy, ’
for instance, were regular turtle-doves as long as
they kept up separate establishments ; so the fact
that certain people in real life do get along pretty
smoothly is nothing to brag about considering
that they make it a point of religion to inhabit
different latitudes. The Kilkenny cats themselves
might have been on good terms to this day if only
they could have kept lots of space between their
respective goings-on.
“ Two Melb. girls dropped into a furniture-shop
to hire chairs for a mission treat. When they got
inside they found it was a coffin mart too, and
before they could escape they were pounced upon by
the proprietor, with eyes cast down and face
clouded in sickening semblance of sympathy,
though he evidently sorrowed in earnest when
disappointed of a funeral. As for the chairs, the
girls wanted stuffed upholstery, as more hospitable
of aspect than bentwood. However, the lender
explainedJ;hat better-class furniture was never let
out for fear of damage. He was an oily foreigner,
by the way, and grew quite facetious when he’d
recovered from his disappointment re the ‘remains.’
‘Yes, my dear young ladies,’ he simpered, in
answer to persuasions, ‘ but ven you and de young
mans begins to play cat and mouses, vat vill
pecome of my poor plush suite ? ’
_ “ The same ghoul, in the course of a professional
visit, said, consolingly, to the daughter of deceased :
‘ You are not so dick (thick) as ven I came last
time ’ (former bereavement), ‘butnefer mind, de
fat live not always longest. ’ . Then, turning to a
sympathetic old lady neighbor who stood by, the
bird of ill-omen scanned her up and down in a
slow, appraising sort of way and remarked, ‘ Now,
dis good lady might live eight or nine years yet,
she looks so quide und beaceful.’
“Mark Twain, extolling Zulu complexions, pro-
nounces black skin more ornamental than all but a
few of the choicest samples of white. So far as
black babies are concerned, Mark is not singular
in his preference. Whether Australian piccanin-
nies, kanakas or niggers, dusky midgets are twice
as pretty as colorless European coevals. Davy, in
his ‘ Cuba Past and Present, ’ praises tiny niggers
as ‘the sweetest little rascals on earth,’ and sym-
pathises with an enthusiastic lady who exclaimed,
‘Oh, why can’t we have black babies, who turn
white when they grow up?’ When blacks lie
dead in decent surroundings, as on the mission-
stations, you’d swear they were asleep. None of
the waxen ghastliness so depressing to beholders
of white-skinned corpses.
“All this is apropos of that chubby Javanese
urchin who rides a pert pony in Fitzgerald’s
Circus. Such a brown satin skin as he’s got, flat
nose and ‘ smile that is childlike and bland.’ His
proprietors know how to set off his points, too.
Sometimes he looks like a jewelled toad in
shimmering green, slashed all over to show
spangled glimpses of underlying gay colors, here
and there and everywhere. On melting afternoons,
his sleek bronziness is shown up by cool butter-
color, with sash and shoulder-knots of sheeny
orange satin.
“In ‘New Babylon,’ Frances Ross’s stage
plumage provokes heart-felt praise. The material
of her house-frock follows the design of the newest
muslins—realistic roses, pale pink and blue respec-
tively, closely covering its groundwork. A plastron
inserted right down the frock-front carries alter-
nate pink and blue frills of non-flowered silk. At
Goodwood, the Queen wouldn’t be ashamed to
call the dressy heiress * cousin, ’ so elegant does the
latter look in faint grey silk, plentifully appliqued
with cream and black baby-ribbon, artistic lisse
sash, filmy cream and black millinery, with
ostrich-feather drooping over the rear brim, and
just enough brightness supplied by a twist of vivid
rose at the waist and knot of the same on the
handle of the belle’s white parasol. Then there’s
that white flower of a blameless its
pointed over-skirt all fairy-white frills, with black
elfin edges.
“ Elizabeth Watson, in her r6le of unauthorised
man-catcher, wears stereotyped adventuress uni-
form—black gown sloping off her shoulders. She
reforms in the end : the criminal conspirators "et
off with life-sentences, while the only culprit
who dies hard, instead of being more-or-less
happy ever afterwards, is the disqualified jockey
