“ By the way, this dying dupe’s repentant un-
doer constantly smooths her own raven bandeaux
with both hands as she bends over her victim’s
death-bed. An experienced nurse testifies that
distracted friends seldom finger their own thatch
at such times, though apt, if not forbidden, to
stroke back the moribund’s hair from his damn
forehead. P
“In that picture at the Gallery, * The Crisis,’
the solitary watcher holds one hand tightly over
his mouth to deaden the sound of breathing. Hos-
pital nurses ‘doing’ the Gallery generally find
fault audibly with this picture, because, they say,
in an illness where there is a ‘crisis,’ the patient
is not propped high up in bed. In lung and
breathing troubles they scout the possibility of a
definite ‘ crisis. ’
The Geisha still goes on her way rejoicing.
Dorothy Vane never looked such a winsome little
creature as in her final .Tap frock of white satin
heavily embroidered with golden storks. Her
short hair, parted innocently, flies back as she
dances—not like a fuzzy mop, but in a shower of
flaxen fluff. Pauli, too, fast gains ground in
feminine favor. At first, like “ Pretty Ja-ha-ane, ”
admirers recognised his need for the request “ Pray
do not be so shy.” Now he extends his arm's
yearningly to spots in the dress circle, where
skirts are thickest, at the end of every verse of
“Mollie Mine,” and girls in those favored regions
compare notes on the thrillsome effects of his
glances.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Of the N.S.W. Legislature of 1843—12
nominated and 24 elected—only one member now
lives —Dr. Nicholson, Bart.
Melb. papers on Jan. 3 obituarised James
Williams, journalist, and on Jan. 4 James Wil-
liams, hospital secretary. Old acquaintances.
A recent little Union Club dinner is inter-
preted by some over-suspicious people as pointing
to the selection of G. Republican Dibbsas N.S.W.
Agent-General.
N.S.W. has but one surviving ex-Premier
—Chicago Dibbs. Victoria boasts seven—Berry,
Gillies, Munro, O’Loghlen, Shiels, Service, and
Gavan Duffy.
Joseph Russell, the first finder of tin at
Stanthorpe, is now farming at Pine Mountain (Q.)
in his 85th year. His tin-days are still his golden
ones. He has been 82 years in Queensland.
Gov. Buxton never—ah-um—liked racing,
but he enjoys immensely a “ big shoot.” So says a
London correspondent. 700 pheasants in six
hours is the death bill of this W.C.T.U. magnate,
who never—ah-hum.
The sale of Will Ogilvie’s book of verses,
“ Fair Girls and Gray Horses,” issued by The
Bulletin in November, has been so rapid that
another edition, of 2000 copies, is now in the
press and will be published in a week or two.
REMARKS IN THE CROWD.
“What's the row here?"
“Dunno! But there’s a woman at the bottom of it.”
“Poor dear! Why don't they take her to the
hospital?”
Over 31) years ago W. J. Lyne, leader of
N.S.W. Opposition, owned a cattle-station in the
Gulf country. He was among the first white men
who made a track from the Gulf to Bowen —
over 1000 miles - through practically unexplored
country.
Gov. Brassey is keeping up the “ falling-
off ” tradition. Having fallen off his bike, his
horse and his yacht, there was little left in that
line, so he has (he says) “ fallen off in weight,”
owing to the hot weather. He has, as it were,
fallen off himself.
N.S.W.’s five Chief-Justices were all born
outside’ Australia. Martin, whose parents came
here in the employ of Gov. Brisbane, arrived in
Sydney when but a year old. The rest—Forbes,
Dowling, Stephen and Darley—achieved man’s
estate before touching Australian soil.
One constable at Russell-street (Melb.)
police-barracks was, prior to joining the force, a
p.uson in a Melb. suburban tabernacle. As a set-
off, Moonee Ponds (Vic.) possesses a cleric in the
shape of Rev. Jack Good, who was formerly a
Melb. constable, and did duty in Bourke-street.
Died, Dec. 18, at Deloraine, Tas., James
Bennet, aged 84, who arrived in the island 1835.
Currier, constable, bom face, merchant, and ulti-
mately one of Tasmania’s largest property-holders.
He was the last of the firm of Block, Bennet
and Barber, known as the “three B’s.,” and was
thrice married.
Premier Turner has gone on tour in E.
Gippsland “ incog.” as he did not wish to be
deputationised. There was no need for elaborate
precautions. Turner may be seen strolling on
the crowded St. Kilda pier any Sunday, and not
six people turn to look at him. His normal ap-
pearance is an incog.
Young Dan. O’Connor (son of the N.S.W.
ex-P. M.G.), who died last week at the early age
of 2.’ bade fair in his school days to outshine his
father’s Demosthenic outbreaks —with the super-
latives clipped a little, of course. By the way,
the Sydney Jesuits now have in hand two youth-
ful Gavan Duffys, both far ahead of their fellows
in college oratory.
W. S. Fleming, the Scotch Adelaidean
lately killed while missionising in China, was not
quite well-educated enouuh or backed by enough
influence to get taken on in the regular saloon and
easy deck-chair position. He had to work his
passage across—which, however, wasn’t so very
rough an experience, as in his unregenerate days
he had roved much.
The well-advertised Father Long has been
transferred from Kanowna to Perth; and the
Sacred Nugget will, it is hoped, be now forgotten.
His reverence was shifted, too, just on the eve of
the opening of the new church, towards the build-
ing of which that bazaar was held to which the
whole district flocked in anticipation of seeing the
sacred myth on view.
Australia is threatened with a visit from
that princess of poseuses, Lady Florence Dixie, who
has been explorer and war-correspondent, is athlete
and Woman-suffragist, but will be best remem-
bered as a romancist, in connection with the
alleged assault upon her by three Fenians (in
buckram) of whom no trace was ever discovered.

death of Mesmerist Kennedy in the States, of
• Bright’s disease. A Melbourne paper of same date
had his picture above a patent-medicine advt., in
which was contained a testimonial of his cure by
the nostrum eulogised. Life’s little ironies.
Dailey, by the way, gave somebody a testimonial

The great Ebenezer Ward, M.L.C., of Ade-
laide, is the son of a Baptist parson. He first
struck the Holy City as a member of G. V.
Brooke’s dramatic co., having previously been
associated with Collins Levey on Melb. Herald.
The Muoh-Travelled Smythe, who was then sub-
editor of Adelaide Advertiser, persuaded Ebby
to take his place as he (Smythe) desired to return
to Melbourne, so Ebenezer became a S.A. institu-
tion.
Thus a correspondent—
Victorian Premier Turner feels keenly the stories that
he is personally a mean man, consequent upon the re-
trenchment policy he has had to carry out. Those who
have known him for many years say that he is neither
particularly mean nor particularly generous, but just an
ordinary careful person, good-natured enough, but with
an ingrained habit of living within his income. It is easy
to be generous with other people’s money; and it ought
to be remembered that Turner’s reputation for meanness
depends, not upon his spending of his own earnings but
upon his administration of publio funds, wherein to be
“ mean ” is to strike “ the golden mean.”
Captain Gother Kerr Mann, R.A., who
died lately at North Sydney in his 91st year, was
present at the first hoisting of the British flag at
what is now Auckland. He was schoolfellow with
Lord Napier of Magdala, and was himself all
through his life a bit of a roarer. He raised a
battery of artillery for the Crimean War and
talked military tactics to no end. It was in the
peaceful solitude of Cockatoo Island, however, lie
did his best work—the construction of the dry dock
there. The veteran celebrated, about a year ago,
his diamond wedding amidst a patriarchal as-
semblage of descendants to the fourth generation.
Capt. Mann had lived through 12 years of George
11 l.’s reign, the whole of those of George IV. and
William IV., and 02 of Victoria’s.
Dr. John Logan Campbell, the pioneer
merchant of Auckland, M.L., has been in business
there since the early forties. About 30 years ago,
having acquired a colossal fortune, he visited Scot-
land with the intention of settling down thereasa
laird, and would no doubt have filled the bill
creditably, as he is a near relative to the ducal head
of (-lan Campbell. But a few months’experience of
Caledonia satisfied him that it was much too
“stern and wild ” for a climate-pampered Maori-
lander, and he returned to M.L. and devoted him-
self to the expansion of his business and the en-
couragement of art. Twelve years ago the late
W. J. Hurst, M.H.R., told the present writer
he had reason to believe that Dr. Campbell's will
devised a quarter-of-a-million sterling for the en-
couragement of art and other public purposes in
Auckland.

Bishop Cecil Wilson, whose proposed close
season for kanakas attracted notice a little while
ago, and who is now on his way to Maoriland to
espouse a daughter of Bishop Julius, will make
Norfolk Island his headquarters. But, as Dr.
Wilson claims that his episcopal authority over his
subjects continues, even when they have settled
on Queensland sugar-plantations, his life is bound
to be a roving one.
A week or so before the New Fear
“ honors ” came out, Queen Victoria sent a letter
congratulating Henniker Heaton upon the partial
accomplishment of Imperial penny-postage. Hen-
niker seemed a cocksure certainty for a baronetcy
at least, but it didn’t eventuate, and there is, pre-
sumably, weeping in the house of Bennett. A
bauble and an autograph at the same time would
be unwonted generosity for Her Gracious, anyway.

Letter received by a would-be contributor
to an alleged Australian weekly :
Memorandum from business manager, Tki.korai’H
Newspaper Company, Limited. Offices: Queen-street,
Brisbane. November 18, 1898. Dear Sir, —Replying to
your memo., we do not accept Australian stories for pub-
lication in either the Tki,*ohapii or the Wkkk. Wo
obtain our supply of story-matter direct from England,
and only publish stories by the best and most popular
authors. We have our stock of Christinas story-matter
already in type.—Yours faithfully, E. L. M'Oonkky,
Manager.
“Guy Boothby has just suffered a shock-
ing bereavement. Not many weeks ago lie bought
the famous bull dog, Black Watch, for .£3OO, thus
giving the highest price ever paid for any speci-
men of that breed in England. Black Watch was
entered for the National Show at Birmingham,
but fell ill, and, in spite of veterinary attention,
gave up the ghost after lingering a couple of
days.” The bull-dog, though truculent-looking,
is a sensitive animal. Black Watch probably
found out what sort of books were written by the
man who had bought him. To be owned by Guy
Boothby was too much for the amour propre of
any well-bred pup.
Mr. John Rive, one of those rare, smiling,
placid men whose pink cheeks privilege them to
wear red neckties in their old age, had hundreds
of congratulations on celebrating his BGth birthday
last Saturday. Mr. Rao, who hails from Scotland,
was the first town-clerk of Sydney, where he
arrived in 1839, and, in 1858, on the town council
being abolished, he was appointed one of the three
commissioners for administering its affairs. In
1857 Mr. Rao became “ secretary to the Railway ”
and in 1881 was appointed Commissioner, and
was Under-secretary for Public Works until his
retirement from the service. Mr. Rae’s vigorous
Aberdeen constitution and his keen artistic and
literary interests make him happy in the winter
of his age. Fortunate is the man who when near-
ing ninety can enjoy his pipe and bury himself in
Horace or Cicero.
THE SOUTH AUSTRALIAN CRICKET TEAM.
Of the three members of N.S.W. Public
Service Board, two (Barling and Coghlan) are
members of no club, and the third (Wilson) an
irregular member of one. A good deal of wrath
has been wasted over alleged club-influences in
the management of the civil-service in N.S.W.
What if the vials, when next filled, be emptied on
the heads of departments who, whether clubmen
or not, are pillars of Masonic and other secret
societies ?
Melbourne Age’s epitaph for millionaire
Tyson :
He died the owner of vast opulence, which he never
attempted to enjoy in any other sense than that of pos-
session. His name is unassociated with any great act of
philanthropy, for which his constant excuse was that he
never had time. And now that he has gone there remains
not a man or woman or child in the whole world to say
they feel his loss ; and he leaves scores of people whom
he cared not one jot about scrambling for the wealth
which he had not a heart big enough to distribute even
by a will.
“ Charley ” Tyson, now deceased, when
managing his millionaire uncle’s Hey-
field estate, in Gippsland, usually
kept a racehorse in training. Old
man Tyson had a horror of the
racecourse and its belongings.
One day he arrived, as
usual unexpectedly, at
Heyfield, asked where
Charley was, and was
told that he had gone
into town. The ancient
prowled around, and, to
the horror of the groom,
looked into the box where
the racer (with a feed
muzzle on) was located.
“ What in the name of for-
tune have you got there ?”
asked the gilded veteran.
“ He’s a bit wild, sir,” was
the i-eply, “and we always
muzzle him in case he should
bite somebody.” “Well,”
said Tyson, “it seems strange
that he can’t get a quiet horse
to ride out of the lot we’ve gob
here.” Had the old man known
enough to recognise a racehorse when he
saw it Charles would probably have been pur-
sued off the place that night by his dreary relative.
John Higginson, who visited Sydney the
other day, and confesses himself deeply interested
in the questionable Illawarra Harbor scheme,
settled in Noumea over 50 years ago. Although
an Englishman born he is French by naturalisa-
tion. On his visits to Australia, and he has
been dropping in off and on for nearly 40 years, he
invariably intimates that he is getting ready to
abandon convict labor in his mining operations,
in favor of Australian free labor.
What prico Henniker Heaton ? If he
live he may beoome a baronet; nay, set a coronet
upon his narrow but noble brow. All this shows
wnat an easy game it is to play; and that it needs
not brains, nor great merit, nor heroic deed to
walk up the ladder. Hooley was a fool, indeed,
who failed with such splendid opportunities.
Grooer Lipton will rise. But Henniker, whom
we ere now saw scudding for safety into the
Liverpool Arms before the vengeance of Walter
Cooper ; Henniker. who could not please even old
Ruin-and-Onions of the Western Post— he mixeth
with the high and the mighty, and hath doubtless
framed and glazed that missive from Her
Gracious, which shall hereafter be the cherished
heirloom of a race of nobles. Yes, it is coming—
Henniker Heaton will yet be made a peer “as a
compliment to Australia.”
Aid. Lowry, of Townsville (Q-), who died
the other week, was a character in his way. When
in Brisbane, representing the Seamen’s Union, he
one day drifted along to the entrance of Govern-
ment House and grounds. He wanted to go in
and have a look round, but the sentinel at the
gate refused him admission, and an altercation
ensued. Anyway, “ Ned ” Lowry didn’t get in,
but he wrote a long letter to the Governor de-
manding as a free’n’ndependent citizen his right
of access to Her Majesty’s representative at any
reasonable time. The Gov. replied in an evasive
way—not as Carington “ Smith ” would.
“ Smith ” would have invited him to come as
often as he liked and bring his pals, too —with the
result, perhaps, that after all the aggressive
Lowry would have been “nobbled.”
At the recent Sydney Highland Gathering
feed Gov. Hampden drew attention to the fact
that his “seat” on a horse had been adversely
oriticised in a Sydney paper, and made mention of
the new style introduced by Sloan, whereupon
Premier Reid said Sloan’s was a style he had dis-

lutely safe as when he held a horse by the neck.
George was never much of a horseman, bub this
scribe can answer for his pluck. Over a quarter-
of-a-century ago writer formed with the present
N.S.W. Premier portion of a riding party to
Bungarribee, on Penrith road. They put George
on a brute of a horse, which gave him about six
spills on the road up. One of the party, a good
horseman, tried to persuade G.H.R. to change
mounts, but the same old George declined. “ I’ll
finish the trip on him if he breaks my neck,” said
George; ‘I wouldn’t give the beast the satisfac-
tion of thinking he’d bested me.”
Amusing to hear angular, dry-as-dust theo-
logian Professor Rentoul remarking that “ the
verses and other writings of Mr. Marshall-Hall
ate, in literary quality, contemptible.” Where
are Rentoul’s pretensions to do anything so grace-
ful and readable as “On the Long-beach, Mor-
dialloc ” ?
As from lips of a child softly sleeping,
’Mid dreams of the morrow’s deliirht,
The light-rippled laughter pomes leaping
As leaps some glad star through the night—
So happy, so free, and so guileless, the tinkle of waves
and the sight.
No wreath here, no wreath for the dreamer,
No chaplet of sea-scented air,
No meadow-sweet, flower-kissed streamer
Entwined about temples and hair,
But stillness that is as a Presence, unearthly, and awful,
and fair.
Hall’s conventional Swinburnian verse can hold
its own against Rentoul’s conventional lectures.

Aboriginalities.
“Alpha”: In a New England farming-
district were two half-time schools. As a rule,
the Department of Public Instruction sends
small teachers, fresh from the training-room, to
gain experience at such places. One of the
schools adjoined a house, where lived the parents
of two girl-pupils, and whenever the teacher
caned or attempted to cane either of them, she
would lift her voice in an anguished shriek for
“ pa.” Then the warlike progenitor would rush in
and “go for” the teacher. This went on very well
for two or three years, until the department senff
along a boxer and a man of brawn. The very
first day of his regime the elder girl rose in violent
insurrection, and was officially swished, and, as
usual, she filled the atmosphere with shrieks of

in and cast himself bodily on the teacher. The
teacher cast him off again, and he fell headlong

with a steady rain of tables, chairs, forms, etc.,

“ Yarrum ” : Some years ago, when school-
mastering way back, had occasion to call on Mrs.
Casey to see why her Bridget (pet-name, “Bride”)
hadn’t been to school for a few days. Knocked at
the door, which was opened by a tidy-looking female
with a lot of smiles and no teeth. “ Book agent ? ”
said she. Told her gently that I was the new
teacher. Then she smiled some more, and asked
me to “come in.” She excused herself for a
moment, went out at the back door and had a loud
and animated conversation with somebody in a
detached kitchen. Casey then entered ; and,
without a word, reached for my throat, which he
got. We rolled over a lot of times on the mud
floor, and I verily believe that, had I not known a
“ holt ”or two, I’d have been cold meat; he had
murder in his eye. With my knee in his stomach,
I demanded an explanation. He told me that he
had been a “ Men of Mark ” victim, and that he
had vowed to thrash the first book-fellow that
called. When I had satisfied him of his mistake,
he said, “ Well, you look like a bookman, and will
have to be mighty careful getting about.” Mrs.
Casey, the lady with the toothless smile, was as
deaf as a beetle; hence the trouble. Left the
place shortly afterwards with a sort of a piebald
present: to wit, a silver watch aud gold albert.
Casey headed the list of subscribers. I made him
do it under threat of prosecution for attempted
butchery of a pedagogue.
* * * *
“ H.S.” : I know an aboriginal who has
been hamstrung and travels on all fours like a
horse or dog. He can cover a great distance in a
day and keep pace with any ordinary walker.
His action is remarkable, though not pleasant to
look at. This man occasionally travels 30 miles
in one day.
“Boomerang” is a term unknown in
South W.A. The local name is “kylie,” and the
implement is manufactured from the jam-tree,
which also furnishes the wood for the “ womera ”
or throwing-stick. Black-boy resin contributes
the knob fora hand-grip on the “ womera.”
* * * *
A favorite bird in the golden West is a
little songster known as the “shepherd’s friend.”
Dark back, breast white, and longish comically-
tilted tail. Travels for miles with sheep, flitting
from back to back and getting his living on
“tick.” His tune is peculiarly cheering at day-
break. Two prolonged notes, in a minor key,
followed by six sharp notes in a hail-fellow-well-
met-style, the whole conveying a suggestion of
“ O-o, O-o, why don’t you be cheer ful, why don’t
you be cheerinl ? ”
“ Yarrum ” : Was standing, the
other evening, in the bails of a milk-
ing-yard, when a man engaged at the
place as pig-feeder and generally useful
came along and stuck his head into a
bucket of milk to have a drink. Then
somebody threw a junk of “masti-
cated grass ” and knocked his hat in.
Now, it might not have mattered
much to the milk had the man been
fairly clean. He was one of the
dirtiest-looking individuals ever I set
eyes on, and had something wrong
with his nose into the bargain. There
was scarcely a tooth in his head, and
he had other drawbacks. His hat was
dirty, and began to move away after
it came from the bucket. Watched
this milk being put into the regular
can, and it was afterwards “ cooled ”
and sent aw'ay by train. Dairy-
men’s wives and daughters never
use milk in any form they see
too much of it. Like the Chow’s cab-
bage, it is good enough for others. The
farmers complain that the health-
inspectors are too exacting, and put
them to unnecessary expense. Just
now they are having those unsightly
rags, which were formerly used under
the lids of cans to stop the milk from
splashing out, done away with, and it
is about time. Some of the old women
will tear up any discarded article of
clothing for “milk rags.” Once had
a good look into a separator after about
80 gallons of cow-juice had passed
through, and I haven’t taken my juice
(neat) since. It is right enough with
rum, but let the rum predominate.
The “pig-feeder” says “it’s a foine
thing for a cowld, as it puts a coating
in the shtomach.”

“C.H.R.”: One way to warm
your toes whilst riding not yet men-
tioned. Take your feet out of the irons
and let them hang. Never fails.

The Pastures Board, Balranald (N.S.W.),
offers a bonus of 3d. a dozen for eggs of crows, and
Is. a dozen for eggs of eagle-hawks. If theP.B.
hopes to check the increase of crows and eagles
by this means, it will be disappointed. All col-
lectors of birds’ eggs, with personal experience,
know that when a nest is robbed the despoiled
birds simply build again and lay another clutch.
Small birds, except the very shy ones, build at
once in a tree adjacent to the one from which
HORSE PHILOSOPHY.
THE LADY'S HACK : “ That
boss of yours wouldn’t
say so many sweet things
to my mistress if he had
to carry her, and had the
sore back I’ve got.”
THE GENTLEMANIA HACK: “Never
mind wait till he marries her.”
their nest was robbed. If only the eggs are
taken, many birds will use the old nest material
in building the new one. Writer has known one
pair of birds (rhipidura albiscapa) to be robbed 11
times in one season, all the nests being taken
within a radius of 200 yards. Cruelty? School-
boys don’t think so ! Hawk-tribe frequently lay
in same nest from which eggs are removed.
Writer has taken five pairs of eggs in eight weeks
from same nest of common brown hawk (hiera-
cidea orientalis), and then the pair laid in a crow’s
nest near at hand, and reared their young. Crows,
if nest is stolen, take to a hawk’s or magpie’s nest
to lay in. Probably too lazy or knowing to build
again. Eagle-hawks go away miles when robbed,
but lay again for a certainty. Common chooky
bears out point. Remove her egg from day to
day, and she may lay 100. Otherwise she sits on
the first 15 or so. It is no use Pastures Board
buying eggs—only an inducement to wary seller
to let eagles and crows live. A smart hand might
work up quite a nice connection by keeping a keen
eye on each pair of crows located.

“Tomahawk” on ancient Clarence River
aboriginal wooing :
When a young gin thought she was old enough, she
wound a piece of kurrajong fibre tightly round the little
finger of her right hand, at the second joint, and started
out a-sweethearting. An admirer would, after due pre-
liminaries, yabber, “My word you bin makit budgery
feller milkie. You yan longa me ?” (Milkie is Clarence
River for wife.) If his offer was accepted they would flit
at nightfall, and, when caught, the dusky Romeo would
have to enter into single-handed combat with his Juliet’s
brother or other champion. If the husband retained the
prize, the kurrajong ring was kept on until the finger
dropped off. Not the case, as has been supposed, that
the finger was chopped off. Another custom fast becom-
ing obsolete is the self-mutilation of the gins after death
of a man. The wailing, also, which used to be kept up
while the moon lasted is rarely practised now.
• * » •
“ Long-Un ” : He was the sorriest of raw-
boned crooks when we got him, but plenty of feed
soon developed his kicking abilities. Ordinary
swingles, fool’s play—iron ditto he bent double at
first go. As a hack, apparently knocked out aft 20
miles, and a boss groaner. Yet, shifting camp
across sandy desert in terrific heat, he put in a
mild 75 miles (5 a.m. until 3 next morning) with-
out feed or water, and with 22 stone on his back
(two men) for 10 miles of the distance. Another
time, cantering over sandhills with him. Rabbit-
burrow, horse ploughing up sand with nose, rider
slips off near side, dragging off-side stirrup. Hob-
nailed boots; usual result: Kicking, tugging and
swearing - match. Rider’s shoulder in sand ;
gripping rein both hands; right toe stuck in
stirrup on top of saddle. Shoeless hoofs land
him on chest and arms. Tears sole off left boot—
no time for prayers; then horse standing few
yards off shivering. Two days later, driving
through burnt scrub—whizz go hind legs, land
mallee root in driver’s eye. That’s years ago.
Horse-sale in city, t’other day; recognised my
dear old friend dragging tired legs out of yard in
milk-cart. Cost milkman £3 inside 2min. Frag-
ments of cart here, battered milk-tins there,
alleged milk everywhere, and badly-damaged
milkman looking round for the earthquake.

Chinaman crossing Manning River in
vehicle-punt worked by native boy, who keeps
“poking borak ”at John. River very wide and tide
running out strong. John says nothing till punt
arrives at wharf ; then blandly observes, “ Me
likee water —you jus’ pullee me back! ” The
nigger had to do it, too, and as the fare is only Id.
return, the Chow kept him at it for two mortal
hours, with the temperature llOdeg. in shade.
Then Ah Lun drove off smiling, and a tame nigger,
too collapsed to poke borak even at a worm,
dropped in a heap under a tree.

“Rake”: Be tree-grubs. Have eaten
them with relish often ; would like to find a good
patch now and spend Xmas among ’em. When a
certain racing-man of olden times stayed at the
Hamilton Hotel, outside Brisbane, he frequently
breakfasted, dined, supped, and banqueted upon
them, much to the disgust of Jack Walsh, the
publican. An old gum-tree just outside the house
yielded a plentiful harvest at all times.

Q. cockroaches are aggressive. Six years
ago the printed notice, “ Hang up your
boots,” in a fashionable Brisbane hotel
quite amused me. I didn’t know
then that cockroaches ate all the
un-hung boots. Left a nobbier
of whisky by my bed one night,
and next morning found about 20
giant cockroaches on the chair, brawling and push-
ing each other. Old chap, evidently leader, kept
backing, his eyes fixed straight ahead, till he
backed off the chair. Put it down to D.T.’s, and
possibly the sight of a snake. Anyway, the fall
sobered him.

“ H.M.T.” : Though corroborrees are not,
as a rule, musical, the writer met an exception in
W.A. The crowd, which hailed from the Black-
wood, was composed of bucks and gins in fair
proportion. Affair began with ordinary inhar-
monious chant with a saltatory background, each
theme concluding with the weird whoop that is
usually the climax of the entertainment. Then,
to the surprise of the civilised audience, came a
finale in the shape of a minor chant in perfect
harmony, concluding with a kind of invocation to
the four quarters of the heavens with arms up-
raised and then gradually brought to the horizon-
tal. The final chord would have done credit to a
Liedertafel.
• * * •
Tamar-shooting is popular in W.A. The
t.amar is a sort of nephew of the kangaroo, slightly
larger than the wallaby, and frequents brushwood
thickets which are perfectly impassible to anything
but himself and his smaller relatives. Blacks are
the beaters, and the marksmen place themselves at
suitable breaks and pick off the animals as they
travel along the races which traverse the under-
growth in all directions.

staggered resident of N.S. W. backblocks town :
Quirindi, December 19, 1898. Mr. Sir,—
I hereby Causin you if you ever repeat the Word to my
son that he has been in Gale Again as you
make it a practices I shall prosicute you to the full 'ex-
stent of Law or any othe person from'this day allso if you
dare hit any off my Children again I will do so you must
bee verry Igrient or you wood not dare do such a thing
my Child did his punishment and it is more than you or
any other person dare throw it up to him he did 2 houers
and it is no disgrace on my Child you and no dought, the
person who put him theire have committed a lot grater
Crime in your life so I shall put a stop to you and all
other skums and Larractons off Quirindi which clearer
shows you are one or you would not be so Igrient as to
repeat the word even once to him & I promise you when
his father arrives home next week you must think he as a
mother that do care for them so you rent her well if ever
you or anyone Else repeats the Word or dare hit one of
them again I will make things hot for you or them and
then it may bee the bitter bite you may do a little longer
than him if I am short of money I am not short off sence
or Brain which I fear you are verry lack of Both I remain
yours Obident Servant

When drinks were reduced from Is. to fid.
at Burketown, on the Gulf of Carpentaria, an old
Gulf hand w ent on an unusual burst—he was a
man of many bursts, but this one was of such
royal dimensions that a friend asked the reason.
The tanked one looked up in surprise. “They’ve
redooced drinks to fid.,”he said. “Well?” “Well,
they mayn’t keep redooced. An’ if they went up
to Is. again and a cove remembered that he hadn’t
been drunk when they was redooced, w’y he’d go
and scrag hisself, wouldn’t he? ”

The present “good season ” in Victoria
has been mainly due to the mildness of the sun-
shine, thusfar. The rainfall for ’1)8 didn’t equal
the record for the three previous years, hut 1895-0-7
were warmer and drouthier when the crops were
coming on. There is no rule for good seasons. The
“ necessary ” amount is a very variable (juantity,
and the country that looks drought-stricken on
paper may be pretty green in fact.
* * • •
When Jack Billson, then boss middle-
weight of Goulburn district, was working on the
Goulburn-Peelwood road, he and his mates had
a loud, aggressive way about them, and used to
court-martial passing swaggies. Once a swaggie
appeared, tumbled under the shade, and kept up
a pitch till Bupper-tiilie. After meal, swaggie
tried before Billson, seated on the camp-bucket,
charged with loitering on H.M. thoroughfare, and
with devouring the hard-earned grub of the camp.
Found guilty, and sentenced to shout for party
at roadside pub., or take six rounds out of Billson.
Swaggie liked daylight to fight in, and was allowed
to camp till morning. Morning came, with result
that swaggie, Billson’s Sunday boots, and Mick
Hogan’s best pants were missing. After that, the
camp took no risks, and offenders were duly
sentenced and executed at the shortest notice. It
went on all right till, one day, Billson shattered
the pub., and then the pub.-keeper readied up a
special swagman. “ I’ll try a grain of ye’r tay,
mates,” remarked a stranger, as he threw down
his Matilda at our camp one day, and proceeded
to help himself generously to tea, damper and
beef. Billson whispered to Paddy Ryan, “ I’m
shiverin’ to be at him,” and went on with his
munching. Usual trial and usual sentence.
Stranger explained “he hadn’t the possible for
beer, and didn’t care about fightin’, as his
mother had always said fightin' was most de-
gradin’.” The camp was determined on beer or
blood. “ Wouldn’t you chaps wait till niornin’ ? ”
“No fear ; inornin’ business off since last
bloke,” and the champion rose to prepare fu"
the fray. “Well, if you chaps must have it to
night, I s’poso yer must.” They fought two
rounds without anyone being hit, owing to the
stranger’s artistic dodging. Third round : furious
onslaught, much lashing at place where stranger
ought to be, and much loss of wind. Suddenly
something heavy dropped on the camp judge’s
nose, and a flash of sparks fled from one eye. The
champion reeled, looked up with his sound eye
full of astonishment and once more rushed at the
foe; another crash, and the air was full of Bill-
son’s teeth. There were no fourth, fifth, or sixth
rounds. The stranger stayed in our camp and ate
our provisions all Sunday, and we put up the beer-
money ourselves on Monday morning. And when
lie was gone Billson briefly reported: “ Look
here, mates, if you’re havin’ any more of this trial
and sentence business, you’re goin’ to carry out the
sentence of the jumpin’ court on your own.”
PESSIMIST AND OPTIMIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The world ain’t worth a damn,” he tiredly said,
And felt his food-ban's greasy emptiness.
“ No house to cadge at—sun a fiery red,
And hell and flames for one long day ahead.
A cove might just as blanky well be dead
As poking on in this infernal mess.”
Then clear and thrilling came a magpie’s call,
A rush and ripple swelled to joyous sound :
“ Grand world and glorious sunlight! Greetings all.
Dry air ! Cool shadows, where the far faint fall
Of waters whispers Hope to souls in thrall 1
Hear! hear the music as the world spins round."
The man gaped at the magpie then a smite
Convulsed his face and made the wrinkles squirm.
“ The world's alright to yon there ain’t no guile,
And all God's wonders buzz along in sty le.
But—just you hump this bluey twenty mile—
And pass me down that blanky alright worm.”
Vic.
L.M.D.
Workers and Wasters.
Norah Murphy, aged 27.
THE GATTON (Q.) TRAGEDY.
Ellen Murphy, aged 18,
Michael Murphy, aged 29.
“ C.C.” writes on a well-worn subject:—
Ask any old shepherd who has been forced by the
spread of civilisation into “ travelling for Riley Brothers,”
why “ things are only middlin’,” and he will answer, with
a wicked glare in his rheumy eyes, that “ wire-fencin’
ruined the lurid country "—and he believes it. l’ut the
tame query to a bullock-puncher, and he will lavish the
choicest and most complicated curses lie was saving for a
“ bit o’ black soil ahead ” in his efforts to convince you
that the railways have sent the country to perdition—and
he certainly believes it. Enquire from a bush-worker
looking for a job, and he will reply briefly, yet forcibly,
“ blanky cockles’’ and, from the expression in his face,
you may fancy he lias no doubt about it. Ask the cockv
himself, and lie will straighten his buck a moment, and,
after wiping his head and consulting the lining of his old
hat, he'll say : “ Well, I dunno, mister. Some ses it’s all
on account o’ these er bloomin'squatters ; tint 1 dunno
as the country’s changed much since I fust knowed it.
Plenty o’ 'ard graft an’ little for it, arter the storekeeper’s
’ad ’is cut out o’ the crop.” That is the old cocky’s
opinion. His son, who passes his time flashing about on
Hie colt the old man saved five guineas for tho “get of,”
scorns the insinuation that anything is wrong with his
world. “ Things ain’t too had,” tie'll tell you. “ 1 took the
‘ ten-mile' push down in a half-mile flutter for a couple o’
notes last week at the pub. Plenty o' sport an’ darnoin',
wot more does a cove want ?” lie smokes cigarettes, this
degenerate son of the soil (he is too tired to smoke a
pipe), and as he leans against the rails at the annual
jockey-club meeting, his pure ambition is to he mistaken
for one of the “ cunning " push from Sydney. But the
talent size-him up as a “ fly-fiat,” and their easiest victim
in a little game of nont.e. According to “6x8,” this is
the man who cuts down wages, hut the present writer’s
“DRIFTING."—A BARCOO RIVER SKETCH .
opinion is that the young cocky would scorn to run even
a blackfellow out of a job, much less “ 6xB’s ” hard-work-
ing hero with the beery halo. The “ semi-drunk-but-
anti-hosh ” scribe gets nearer to the spot when he shifts
the charge of wage-cutting on to the man with the halo,
who, when offered a job at a low wage, hasn’t a fiver in
his pocket to shout “ No ” for him.
* * * *
A lot of twaddle has been written about the noble
instincts of the bush dipsomaniac presumably by
writers who have had few opportunities of ob-
serving his eccentricities. Here is one of the
present scribe's experiences out of a bundle. Boozer
took a contract for ring-barking ; low price, of course.
Engaged a passing swaggie to heip him. Swaggie walked
16 miles to the camp. Boozer hadn’t a spare axe, so
swaggie, eager for the job, tramped back to town and got
one. Boozer had mate at work for six weeks literally
running from tree to tree. Time, midsummer.
Contract finished and passed. Boozer told his
mate that he had taken another block to ring
and would have to go in for more stores. Went
to the head station, drew his cheque, and struck a
crow-line for the nearest pub. Swaggie waited
patiently at the camp until the tucker gave out, and then
went to the station to enquire for his boss. Manager told
him that there was no other work. Couldn’t recognise
swaggie’s interest in the cheque paid over; man a
drunkard; ought to have come with him to draw the
money. Very sorry; hard case ; might get something if
he hurried into town ; but feared it was too late. Swaggie
did the journey in record time only to learn that his late
employer was already “ stiff ” and doing a fortnight in
the local jug for “ drunk and obscene.” Then the store-
keeper added the last straw to swaggie’s bundle by stick-
ing him up with a bill for the axe he had left at the camp,
and a pair of boots he had worn out on the job. Swaggie’s
language unprintable, culminating in a violent assault.
Result—“ Twenty-one days.” (The storekeeper was a
J.P.). Swaggie entered the lock-up with blood in his eye
and begged to be put in the “ same cell as his mate—just
for company like ; ” hut the man with the beery halo of
improvidence objected vociferously to such thrifty com-
pany and thanked his gods that he would have a
week’s start of such a mean wage-cutler. A month
later the writer met the beery one earnestly seek-
ing a job, for his swag consisted solely of two corn-sacks
and a jam-tin.
* • * •
But with all his ingenuity the bush-boozer is not
quite such a wage-wrooker as the individual who
grafts like a working bullock and prides himself
upon being a “ ringer "—either in the shearing-shed
or at any other bush labor. Take, for instance,
the man who shears 800 odd sheep per diem—
the man we read of but seldom meet in a shed. Tho
record appears all over Hie province. “ Oh, oh !" ex-
claims the ready-reckoning manager of a big pastoral co.
—absentees mostly—“ 800 a day means t‘lB u week earned
by a common working man at. what is practically unskilled
labor.” Then, either Ignorant of or ignoring the very
favorable conditions necessary for such a feat, he stands
agitating for a combination to reduce shearing rates.
The fools who cut such startling tallies ure blinded by
their vanity to the obvious fact that they are being used
for the special purpose of drawing attention to tho
abnormal wages it is possible for a shearer to earn, ho that
when a reduction of the standard 20s. per 100 forces a
strike, the public sympathy will bo with the squatters.
These record ringers know very well that the “ lioss over
the hoard ” is not too particular in havinga close, level cut
when a record tally is being attempted for publication,
and they are also well aware that the man who cuts lot)
for a reoord doesn’t get much chance of repeat-
ing the performance. When he has to catch and
carry his own sheep out of the pen, and when they
happen to be hip, hard-cutting wethers instead of
soft, record-breaking ewes brought to his hand, he is
a good man if he puts out a hundred a day and doesn’t
have to spend the next day in the hut with a “ knocked-
up hand.”
* • • *
In all contract work the tendency is to fix the
price by the earnings of a few exceptional bullockers who,
inspired by vanity and greed, are beguiled into extra-
ordinary exertions which cannot be maintained for any
lengthy period, and by falling into the trap laid for them
the average workers are deprived of a living wage, while
the men who set the pace can only gain it under a strain
that must end in break-down. The same wage-cutting
influence is at work in other trades than bush work, and
it is worth noting in this connection that the introduc-
tion of the linotype-machines into the composing-room of
the plutocratic “People’s Paper” was quickly followed
by the publication of a record performance of a comp,
with the new type-setter. Has that performance been
equalled by the majority of comps. ? I don’t- know ; but
I do know the result of a record in post-boring which
bears out. my argument,. A man took a contract to bore
posts for a seven-wire fence at 10s. per 100. This man,
who may be described as a dumpy Hercules with a cast-
iron back, bored 200 iron-bark posts in a “day” of 14
hours in very rough country, and, of course, adjoining
squatters heard of the “ good money ’’ he earned at it.
The next applicant for boring was offered 6s. per 100, and
tried it for a day or two ; but as he couldn’t stick at it
from jackass to jackass and swamped the little he earned
by leaving the business ends of two 2s. 6d. bits in yellow-
jacket posts, he threw up the job with a curse upon the
man who put up the record and brought down the price.

The N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
of Moore Park, Sydney, has placed upon the mar-
ket a splendid Lager Bier called Sydney Lager
Bier. This bier is the choicest thing ever offered
to the people of N.S.W. Analysts praise it most
highly ; doctors recommend it to convalescents,
and connoisseurs at the last Sydney Agricultural
Show gave it first prize against all competitors.
What has long troubled the rational people of
N.S.W. is “what to drink.” There is no fun
in summer weather in drinking fiery alcoholic
liquors that break up the nerves, affect the diges-
tion, heart, liver and kidneys; and on the other
hand there is only an intense vagueness and total
dissatisfaction in drinking wish-wash. Sydney
Lager Bier is the happy mean between these ex-
tremes, and people in the country who desire to
have the newest and best drink going should com-
municate with the N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing
Co., Moore Park, Sydney, for particulars. Al-
though the new lager is so much superior to all
others, it is the most reasonable in cost upon this
market.

From a Maoriland paper :
SADLER. —On October, 1898, George Sadler, aged
82, formerly shipmaster, Brightlingsea, Eastern
Essex, near Great Clacton, brother of W. E. Sadler,
of New Zealand, and intimate friend of the cen-

“ APENTA,”
The Best Natural Purgative Water
as a safe, ordinary and gentle aperient. Sole
exporters: The Apollinaris Company, Limited,
London, W.
Bishop Grimes (R.C.), of Maoriland, which
is supposed to be a riclt and prosperous country,
has gone to beg and pass the miserable, shameless
hat round in poor old Ireland, and decent, self-
respecting M.L. Catholics feel pretty bad when
they read about the cleric’s crawl for foreign
coppers. One of the strongest grievances of this
paper against the R.C. and Anglican church's
is the way their begging representatives wander
periodically to Britain to drag the independence
of this country in the dirt by weeping, in the name
of Australia, for foreign charity. When it con-
siders Bishops Grimes and Stonebroke-Wigg and
the fraternity of mendicant bishops generally it
feels half-persuaded to be a Wesleyan. Grimes,
by the way, is telling some very steep yarns about
the way Holy Church has to toil and hustle in
Moaland. For instance: —
He would mention one or two instances in his own dio-
cese, to which he was appointed ten years ago. One of
his priests was obliged to go 17 miles every Sunday be-
tween two Masses. To visit the whole of his vast parish
he should be six days on horseback and he should bo
absent a whole month from his presbytery. During that
month even the devoted Sisters of Mercy in that district
were obliged to forego the privilege of assisting at the
adorable sacrifice of the Mass even on Sunday. One of
his missionaries went on one occasion nearly four hundred
miles to give the last sacraments to a man who bad not
seen a priest for 40 years.
Just seriously figure out the inwardness of that
yarn ! The first known European settlers in
Maoriland arrived there in 1814—and they were
missionaries. Forty years without the comfort of
seeing a priest would bring even the earliest
colonist up to 1854. In 1854 there were settle-
ments at Wellington, Auckland, Taranaki; Nel-
son, Otago, Canterbury, Hawke’s Bay, and many
other places. The most remote individual in the
whole province could hardly be 300 miles from a
settlement if he tried, even in 1854, let alone 400.
Where did that dying man hang out?
DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS
CURE PERMANENTLY. LIVING WITNESSES SPEAK.
A Woman’s Message.
CONVEYING WORDS OF HOPE TO THE
AFFLICTED. BETWEEN LIFE AND
DEATH. BUT NOW CURED.
Truth, it is said, is sometimes stranger than
fiction, and in no way has this phrase been better
exemplified than in the plain, unvarnished state-
ment given a reporter by Mrs. E. Morey, a lady
who resides at 125 Jones-street, Ultimo, N.S.W.
The story she related is here given in her own
words. She said: “I had not been well for
several weeks, suffering terribly from severe pains
in my right side, in fact, the pains at times vvere
so severe I could neither sleep or rest on my light
side, and drawing my breath was accompanied by
a sharp pain in the side and back. My liver was
in a very bad condition, food I could take but
very little of, because of the distress it caused after
eating. I tried many treatments and medicines,
but all failed, and my condition was so bad I fully
made up my mind death was the only release from
my illness. A few weeks ago I beard of the re-
markable cures being effected by the use of Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills. I was persuaded to
try them. The first few doses gave me immediate
relief, the pains leaving like magic. I continued
using them for a time, and to-day am entirely
cured. I firmly believe Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills have been the means of saving my life. I
have recommended them to many of my friends,
and it gives me great pleasure to make this stale
ment public, as my faith in Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills is very strong. If persons reading my
words are sufferers, a remedy is at hand that will
cure them, viz., Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills.”

Slipping up Death.
AN ACCOUNT OF HOW A MAN
WRESTLED WITH DEATH AND
SECURED FIRST FALL.
We do not know if Mr. W. J. O’Brien, Junee
Reefs is an athlete, but we do know that he
has performed a phenomenal feat with a little
assistance—assistance we might say, small enough
to swallow. He writes: “I have much pleasure
in testifying to the quality of Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills. I was working in the mines and got
leaded, and went to the doctor, who could give
me no relief. I also tried many patent remedies
which also failed, and made sure my time to go
would soon come, and gave myself up as com-
pletely done for. At last a mate of mine said,
‘ Here is something that will cure you, and if they
don’t, nothing will do you any good.’ He gave
me a bottle of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. I
took one dose that night and one next day, and
the result was wonderful. I am happy to say
that I am now quite well and able to work again,
thanks to Indian Root Pills, only for which I do
not think I’d be alive to-day.”
What He Lost,
AND WAS GLAD TO GET RID OP.
There was no need for Mr. S. G. Coulson, tailor,
of Muswellbrook, to scratch his head and look
round for what he had lost, or make inquiries
about the neighbors. He just took his pen in
hand and wrote to us. He says: “It is with
pleasure that I acknowledge the wonderful efficacy
of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills in curing indi-
gestion. They are, indeed, in my estimation, the
best pill in the market. I have tried a good many
patent medicines, but yours I shall always recom-
mend to my friends as a reliable medicine for indi-
gestion, biliousness, pains in the back and stomach.
They are unsurpassed.” Many of you who read
this yearn for such a disappearance which Mr.
Coulson has experienced, so now you know how
to make it. 1
A Brisbane Marvel.
A WELL-KNOWN LADY SUFFERED
TORTURES FOR 15 YEARS, APPAR-
ENTLY NIGH UNTO DEATH, BUT
DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOTS PILLS
EFFECT A COMPLETE CURE.
Mrs. Elizabeth Smith, whose home is in Surrey-
street, Red Hill, Brisbane, Q., has every reason
to be a thankful woman, as she has been actually
rescued at the brink of the grave. Having heard
such reports, it was decided to fully investigate, so
a reporter was sent to obtain the facts, which are
given in Mrs. Smith’s words : “ For the past 14
or 15 years I have suffered severely from my liver
and indigestion, and two months ago I had a very
bad turn ; the sensation was as though there had
been an iron band around my stomach, and I had
great trouble in breathing. In fact, I thought I
was going to die, and was about to make my will.

“Trebless”: Six weeks out. Travelled hun-
dreds of miles. Not a life written. Letter from our life
insurance co. Why no business?” The doctor—new-
chum —desperate. Said he : “ They will stop our advance
next week for a certainty. We will insure ablanky tree !’
Selecting a box sapling, we got to work. Name, John
Tree ; age last birthday, 42, &c., &c. Filled in the pro-
posal for £250 policy, and forwarded it, with a quarter’s
premium, to Sydney. That “ life ” kept our advance
going fer three weeks more.
“A.A.A.”: Curious to note signs by which
veteran stock-buyers indicate a bid at auction. At a
Hunter River saleyard recently I noted following : Nod,
backward jerk of head, wink, turning-up of eyes, lift of
forefinger, twitch of mouth, and crossing of two fingers.
A certain Melb. mine-boomer is so tired of
being upbraided by victims that, to save the victims
a lot of useless talk and himself a lot of time, he has had
a glossary of abusive words printed in small type on
large cards, as, for example—
1. You are an arrant thief.
2. ~ „ a blasted thief.
3. ~ ~ a confounded thief.
4. ~ ~ a damned thief.
And so on, with 500 other epithets alphabetically arranged.
When victim comes in and starts to upbraid, the financier
simply presents him with one of these cards, adding :
“ I’m very busy to-day, my dear sir. Kindly pick out the
expression you think fits the case, mention the number
of your observation, and my clerk will turn up the index.”
* » * *
“Diomus’': N.S.W. should reform the Pawn-
brokers' Act. One way in which the screw is turned on
the poor (I illustrate by an actual incident) : A poverty-
stricken woman pawned some portions of her clothing
for 2s. fid., and lost the ticket. She had to go to the
pawnbroker for particulars of the pledge, prior to getting
the signature of a J.P. so as to obtain a duplicate ticket.
Shy lock charged her Is. 6d. Think what that Is. 6d. must
have meant to her! This is not an isolated case, for
thousands of pawn-tickets are lost yearly. Some pawn-
brokers are, I believe, merciful, and let their clients off
With a charge of fid., but many, as in this case, grind out
as much as Is. fid. Others may go higher, for all I know —
there is, in fact, no reason why they shouldn’t charge £1
or £lOO as the Act says nothing about payment; so Shy-
lock is given a free hand. His customers must go to him
to get the duplicate, and the only alternative appears to
be to let the pledge go. Seems to me that a uniform
charge of 3d. would amply repay the loan-monger for his
trouble.

This letter, from the representative of a wealthy
Sydney house, is the latest thing in debt-collecting
“ Dear Madam,—l noticed you at mass on Sunday last,
and I could not help thinking of your good departed hus-
band ; poor fellow, I often think of him. May the Lord
be merciful to him. I was nearly going up to speak to
you, as I wanted to let you know your late husband owed
my firm a few pounds.’ As you know, according to the
teaching of holy Church, no soul can enter heaven until
the person has paid the last shilling he owes. I feel
certain it must have escaped your memory, or it would
have been settled before, and that is why I now mention
It; not that we are greedy after money, but 1 feel sure
you will agree with me that it is impossible for his soul to
gain rest while the amount is owing. Therefore, in order
to remove the obstacle, you will kindly remit the amount,
£3 7s. 9d.—Yours sincerely, —~
* * * •
The multitudes of reaper and binder agencies
which lately exploited the Vic. mallee district are now
mostly busy finding either the buyers or the machines.
In the absence of roads the agents have now to
depend for guidance on the position of the scores of
abandoned machines. Something like this: “ Want
Smith, eh ? Well, go ’long straight ahead for 300 yards,
and you’ll come across a red binder ; then turn to the
right, and 200 yards farther you’ll see a yellow reaper.
After passing that hill keep to the right till you see what
looks like a small mountain (it’s really a heap of reapers
and binders ready for a bonfire) and you’ll find Smith’s
place a mile further on.” One canvasser told writer
lately that it was dangerous to go out anywhere in the
mallee after dark, as you were almost sure to break your
neck over one abandoned machine or another. He added
that there was such a derned lot of these implements
about that the wheat-crop couldn’t be big—the machines-
left it no room to grow.
* * * *
Notes of the defunct Provincial and Suburban
Bank of Melbourne are now worth 2s. 6d. each, in Aus-
tralian criminal circles, for, having been genuine in their
day, they possess a finish and crispness which not even
the most skilful forger can impart to the “flash
article. The notes got loose through the carelessness of
the bank liquidator, who for many years strong-roomed
them, till, by and by, they began to take up too much
space, and, the estate being quite done with, the boxes
containing the notes were shifted to a shed in the office-
yard. There they lay forgotten, until the boxes more or
less fell to bits, and Fate directed an old, worn-out
vagrant to the yard. Happening across the notes, the
Yarra-banker realised that the tide had come to him
which led to fortune, and, stealthily taking away the
pile by degrees, he “planted” them, and has been
living happily on the proceeds of their sale. For pur-
poses of confidence-tricking and “spieling” these notes-
are invaluable; even experienced tradesmen are occa-
sionally deceived by them. Also, some enterprising person
with a jar of acid has recently taken to altering the name
of the bank to that of a living concern, and many such
notes are in circulation in the West. The trouble is that
the notes are all fives and tens; had that vag. found
where the ones were stowed, he might by now be posing
in London as a representative Australian.

Dear Bulletin.— Be “widow’s coup” (8.,
10/12/’9B), how’s this for a banker’s coup ? Farmer dies,
leaving property to wife, and making her sole executrix,
in which capacity she instructs an auctioneer (widower)
to sell the land. He sells, and places proceeds to credit
of his overdrawn account, but when he wants to pay the
widow the banker hangs on to the money. Auctioneer
asks banker what he is to do—how he is to pay ? Banker
says, “ Don't pay ! Marry the woman.” And he did.—
W.A.
* * » *
Hooley, Bottomley, Whittaker Wright and
others of whom the public has signified that it has had a
little too much, were not in it as company-mongers with a
man in the same line of business 20 years ago, who re-
cently came out of gaol and.died. In ’79 he issued a
“ private and confidential ” prospectus of a co. “ to re-
cover the enormous wealth of gold lost in the Red Sea,
when the waters engulphed I’haraoh and his hosts.” It
was beautifully worded and so cunningly devised that
even now it is Used as an ideal specimen by boomsters.
It attempted to prove the exact spot where the Egyptians
waded in, and that- by some upheaval the depth was
much less than at the time of the catastrophe—in fact,
that on a clear day the bottom could be seen. It was
intended to lift out with a derrick the chariots covered
with gems and having wheels of beaten gold. He only
asked for £lO,OOO, and he collected £7400. Then he went
to gaol for 15 years. No wonder he died when he saw
what a hash others had made of the business.

How is this for good business ? A man had 40
acres of cleared land which he wanted ploughed ; local
price £1 per acre. Did he pay it? No. He offered
prizes for a ploughing-mateh —lst prize, £6 ;2nd, £3 ; 3rd
£2. All the best talent rolled up with teams and ploughs
and wives and families. Judges appointed, the 40 acres
ploughed, the families had a good picnic, and the whole
thing cost Mr. Smarty (say) £l2 for prizes and refresh-
ments.

“Matchbox ” : They’ve syndicated the Westra-
lian timber industry. The men at one big mill (this from
three of them interviewed separately) get Bs. a day, and
are paid in paper—in cash when they’re off on a jam-
boree—once in six months or so. They buy all provisions
at the employing co.’s store at prices ranging from 10 to
20 per cent, above those paid at nearest township. Buy
anywhere else and you’re the proud creator of a vacancy
for a mill-hand, duly advertised in the West Australian.
Beckon up the tax —interest for money held in co.’s
possession, profit on the stores, necessity for attending all
local shivoos got up by manager and friends, church col-
lection, &c. —and you can guess who makes the money—
not king working-man. Add to this that the timber-
leases range up to 100,000 acres, and reckon the chances
•of prosperity ahead of the misbegotten deformity that
•defaces the map of Australia.

“Yambo’ : When I was a young fellow, and
thinking of getting married, I bought a piece of land in
a part of Melbourne called Hotham Hill. This land had
been bought from the Crown at so much an acre and re-
tailed at so much a foot. Among my neighbors was one
MacToole (his name wasn't MacToole), a laborer on the
railways, and an arrant thief. Another neighbor was a
cabman, one O’Callaghan (that wasn’t his name). O’Cal-
laghan was often late (or early) in getting home ; and one
early morning he was stabling his horse when he heard a
sound near MacToole’s place like w T ood thrown down. He
went out quietly to investigate, and saw some one sneak-
ing away, presently returning with a piece of timber
which he had annexed from a building in course of erec-
tion near by. It was very quietly dropped alongside of
other pieces at the back of MacToole's fence. As soon
as Mac had gone away for more (he was in a wholesale
way of business apparently) O’C. slipped along and picked
up as many pieces as he could carry. As fast as Mac-
Toole brought the wood O’Callaghan annexed it and
heaved it over his own fence. After 13 journeys Mac T.
felt about, apparently to see how his stack was growing,
and found only the pieces he had carried last trip. This
seemed to puzzle him, and he felt around some time.
Then he stood up and scratched his head—couldn’t find
any timber there —and remarked (s otto voce) : “ Well I’m
blowed ’’(he didn’t say “blowed”), gave it up as a bad
job, and cleared away inside to bed. MacToole is still
alive ; he is an ancient pick-and-shovel man and an impe-
cunious thief to this day. O’Callaghan is a prosperous
•cab-owner. The man who knows enough to make another
man steal wood for him in the night generally prospers.
He has learned the great secret of success, which is to let
the other fellow take the dead lift and the responsibility
and annex the wood yourself.
♦ * * »
Not long ago a seeker after work, on the repre-
sentation that there was something that would suit him,
was induced to deposit £1 with a Sydney “ registry
office ” shark ; which money, nothing having been forth-
coming during several weeks, he demanded the return of.
His shark then told him that he required a clerk, and
offered to take him on at 15s. per week and live on
premises. But as there was no bed, no food, and no
salary forthcoming, the victim took out a summons, and
got a judgment by default. That was all he did get, for
another shark in the same line of business held a friendly
bill-of-sale over every stick in the establishment. The
concern has since been sold-up by the faithful mortgagee,
and the late owner has departed in search of fresh prey-
ing-grounds, leaving a flock of shorn sheep to besiege his
vacant premises.

Woman’s Weakness.
The woman of to-day is usually ailing ; there is
always something the matter with her. The
world’s doctor-bills for women is over double what
they are for men and children. Of course, the
organism of a woman is much more complex and
delicate, and requires a great deal of attention,
but to suppose that it was originally intended that
women generally should suffer for the greater part
of her life is ridiculous on the face of it. The
great trouble iu this case lies with the woman her-
self ; she disobeys the natural law, fails to observe
regular habits, and seldom takes proper care
of herself, or gives enough attention to her own
bodily welfare. Tight-lacing is one of the many
causes of the alarming predominance of weak
women to-day. The abdomen is compressed into
half its original size, and the digestive organs not
given sufficient room in which to perform their
various duties ; they, in their turn, crowd the
heart and female organs, and the result is palpita-
tion of the heart, irregular function, and a general
weakness of the whole system. It is absolutely
necessary, furthermore, that the waste matter
of the body should be carried out of the system
regularly and systematically, and this can only
be done by keeping the whole digestive organism
in perfect condition.
A woman in an unhealthy condition is entirely
unable to perform her duties as a wife and mother,
and instead of .being a help and blessing to her
husband she will prove a fretful, grumbling bur-
den, whose very existence is a ourse to herself, her
family, and her friends.
Cleanse the system, then, and in future give
more attention to the mechanism whioh forms
your womanhood. Take a few moments from the
perusal of fashion-papers and bargain-advertise-
ments, and spend the time in finding what rules
of nature you are disobeying, and what you can
do to improve your health and make those around
you happy; and remember that Bile Beans will
oleanse the whole body of waste matter, tone up
the system, and give you renewed energy and
vigor, thus enabling you to make a fresh start,
with a clear road to a healthy and happy exist-
ence.

Indigestion.
Indigestion and dyspepsia are very similar, and
in many cases the same treatment will apply to
both. Indigestion is the inability of the stomach
to pass the food given to it, and many are the
reasons thereof. Over-eating causes dyspepsia,
but this is not, as is popularly supposed, the most
common cause ; indeed, there are so many causes
of this common complaint that it is sometimes
hard to conclude what is the real reason. In-
sufficient mastication, or “ bolting ” the food, is
one of the principal, while among others are lack
of exercise, abuse of spirits, which causes inflam-
mation, insufficient food, bad air, worry, or brain-
work, and sedentary habits. The symptoms are
almost as numerous as the causes : distress after
eating, and a heavy feeling on the chest are the
most noticeable ; bad taste in the mouth in the
morning, accompanied by a dull pain in the head,
loss of appetite, general indisposition for work,
and a pale and sallow complexion, are all signs
of, more or less, indigestion. The general ten-
dency is towards constipation, and bringing sour
liquids up into the mouth is a frequent occurrence
light wines may be taken in small quantities. A
regular amount of exercise will be found very
beneficial, but must not be of a violent character.
Bile Beans for Biliousness, taken as directed, will
clear all waste and impure matter from the
system, and entirely do away with any constipa-
tion that may have been contracted. They will
also insure the cure being permanent.
Costiveness-
The most effectual way of overcoming a costive
habit is to take at bedtime half to three Bile
Beans for Biliousness, or enough (o operate
once, and not more than twice, the next
morning. Pursue this course for eight or ten
days, and then omit taking the Beans for
a single night, but, nevertheless, the next morn-
ing make an effort, whether there is any desire
or not, at the same time in the morning that the
Beans operated the day before. If successful, so
again the next morning, and ever after regularly
at the same time of day. It must not be put off
for a single moment beyond the usual time, as the
old habit will return again.

eating, and a terrible feeling in the head, with
pains in the right side and between the shoulders.
I also was subject to a languid, tired feeling, and
felt extremely quarrelsome. My appetite was
quite gone, and I could not eat the most tempting
dishes, while my rest was always disturbed and
irregular. About two years ago I completely
broke down, and had to attend the hospital as an
out-patient. My body was covered with blotches,
which the doctor described as ‘hives.’ I got no
relief while in the hospital, and gave up in despair.
I tried every patent-medicine I could hear of, and
none of them had any effect on my complaint.
As a last resource, I was advised by a friend at Old
Dubbo to try Bile Beans, and he presented me with
a box in May last, but I had no faith in them, having
tried so many medicines. I commenced to take
them, however, to satisfy my friend, following the
instructions given on the leaflet in which they
were enclosed. Before I had finished the first
box I began to feel relief in the stomach, the pains
being less violent, and my appetite improved
slightly. I felt so much encouraged by this that
I purchased five large boxes, and took the Beans
regularly. My health and strength steadily re-
turned, the spots left my body, and my weight
also increased. To make a long story short, I
completely recovered. I am now enjoying far
better health than at any time since I was 24
years of age, and am now 42. In following my
occupation of wood-buyer and general dealer I
am exposed to all weathers, and never feel the
slightest ill effects. When I think of the long
years of suffering I have undergone I can only
describe my cure as miraculous.” “ You certainly
look in the best of health ? ” said the reporter. “ I
am indeed, and feel quite happy. My wife and
friends in Dubbo can hardly realise the great
change in mo since I commenced taking the Bile
Beans. I attribute my cure entirely to their use,
and shall be glad to recommend them to anyone.
I honestly believe that but for the Bile Beans I
should not have lasted another six months after
leaving the hospital.”

The Mayor’s Proclamation.
“Glen Innes, N.S.W., Oct. 11, ’9B.
H. H. Warner and Co., Ltd., Melbourne.
Gentlemen, —1 have often thought it my duty
for the sake of others to write you. It will be five
years next month since my wife was given up by
two doctors, as a patient who could not live the
night through.
She had been treated for heart complaint; but
we afterwards found that it was kidney disease.
On Sunday when the doctors told me that she
would not live another day—the dropsical swelling
had then extended from her legs into the body—
a friend said to me: “ Try Warner’s Safe Cure.”
At half past ten that night I procured a bottle
and gave her one dose, then in about an hour
another. She said soon after, “ Jim, I feel a little
easier.” I continued with Warner’s Safe Cure,
giving up the doctor’s medicine, and today she is
a living monument to its efficacy. The doctors
and everyone who knew her were astonished.
I have recommended this medicine scores of
times since, and we always keep it in the house.
If my liver is sluggish I take it as a tonic and
invigorator.
After five years I consider the trial is a fair one
and that it is consistent to call my wife’s cure
permanent. I am well-known in Glen Innes,
having lived here sixteen years, been fourteen
years alderman, and twice elected mayor of the
municipality, and 1 am not afraid to sign my name
as a guarantee that Warner’s Safe Cure is what it
is represented to be.—Yours faith ully, JAMES
HEALEY, Mayor.

POLITICAL
POINTS
N.S.W. Ministerial records : Robertson’s
longest, two years, thirteen days; Parkes, four
years, fifteen days ; Dibbs, Stuart, and Cowper,
each two years, nine months ; Reid, four years
and five months, and still going apparently strong.
S.A. Tories, recognising that their conduct
in connection with the present crisis makes a good
election cry for the Kingston Govt., are all for
compromise when the Council meets on Jan. 17,
and it is intended to promptly pass the Appro-
priation Bill.
The cabled determination of Portugal to
preserve her colonies intact means that she is un-
willing to give up Delagoa Bay. If Britain
could get hold of Delagoa Bay then the Boers
would be finally blocked in their endeavors to get
a direct sea-outlet from the Transvaal.
The issue before S.A. electors at the ap-
proaching Assembly election is certain to be Leg.
Council reform. As the result of the rejection by
K.A. Tory chambt-r of household suffrage, Premier
Kingston now pledges himself to have adult suf-
frage for both Houses and nothing else.
An impressive spectacle in itself, besides
being a grim commentary on Premier Reid’s
“jolly and fat” whoop, was the recent congrega-
tion at a Sydney church of about 1000 workless
men. The assemblage was due to an endeavor by
Revs. Buchanan and Zillman to show the unfor-
tunates “ that all followers of Christianity do not
scorn the weak and buffeted and helpless.”
N.S.W. is still waiting to hear what com-
pensation the Government has paid to that
brother and sister who were lately convicted and
imprisoned at Bathurst on a charge of stealing
a bullock, and quietly released when the missing
bullock turned up alive and well ; also if any
inquiry has been made into the conduct of the
jiolice who worked up the case, and vvhether the
judge has been called on for a report about his
own conduct in this remarkable matter.
Premier Kingston’s old colleague, J. H.
Gordon —who resigned the Chief Secretaryship
owing to the Hospital squabbles—threatens to
stump the country against the Premier at next
election on the question of Council reform. The
clever little lawyer even threatened to contest
Kingston’s own West Adelaide dunghill, but he
has had a Xmas dinner since then. Gordon is a
bright debater and a splendid after-dinner speaker,
with a Democratic heart but a Tory stomach, and
is such an indolent politician that when election
time comes he wi’l probably be too tired to climb
on to the platform.

ment is popular power
cut up into electors’
rights ; but the trouble
is that the great bulk
of the population is too

got it. One of the

is that it deprives each

The tendency of Sydney softgoods firms
to form their businesses into limited liability co’s.
is a straw that shows that York-street believes the
]K)litical wind to be blowing Federation-ward.
Australian unity also means Protection, a good
round tariff, and smaller profits. The merchants
and middlemen have grabbed most of the Free-
trade oyster. In Maoriland, despite a tariff of 15
or 20 per cent., the retail selling-price of dozens of
big lines, especially in flannelettes and floorcloths,
is not more than 24 per cent, dearer than in Free-
trade N.S.W.
The gradings of N.S.W. Public Service,
under the annual-increment regulations, have not
given general satisfaction. The feelings of an
officer who, after waiting perhaps for years for
promotion, suddenly finds his salary increased
from £156 to £l6O are deep indeed. The regula-
tions as promised before the general elections are
being evaded wholesale, much to the disgust of
the rank and file, especially among the juniors.
And at least one large division of the service got
no rises at all, on the ground that the Board
hadn’t time to consider it.
In the last seven years Queensland has
auctioned 1,282,049 acres of land, of which
865,494 acres were in the Central District. It
realised £680,062, of which the Central District
contributed' £440,826. The policy of the Bris-
bane Government is to make hay while the sun
shines. The Central District may get Separation
some day, therefore the Southern Government
wants to turn into prompt cash every acre of
Central land that it can find a buyer for, and grab
tne money.
S.A. National Ass. is spending much money
in view of the approaching general election, and a
special attempt is to be made to capture Labor
seats. Of the 14 members representing the seven
metropolitan districts, eight are direct Labor re-
presentatives, four are staunch Ministerialists
(including Kingston and Works Minister Jenkins),
and the remaining two, Darling and Glynn, are
Oppositionists. In some cases the contest will go
hard with the Laborites, who are not so well
organised as they were in M‘Pherson’s time.
The population of Westralia, where the
late lamented boom hangs out, increased last year
by 6226, the smallest increase since ’92. For the
first nine months the increase was over 10,800,
but the back-rush eastward was so violent in the
last three months that the final quarter showed an
actual loss of 4598. Probably things will improve
again, but the big influx towards the sunset is
evidently over for the present. In ’96, Westralia,
then on the summit of its glory, had a population
increase of 36,711, but it is not likely to get there
again in the memory of this generation.
S.A. Upper House is probably beginning
to feel that it made a grievous mistake in laying
aside the Appropriation Bill because its little per-
sonal corn was trodden on, and when it meets
again in a few days the Bill will almost certainly
be passed in great haste. If it isn’t, S.A. will
stop payment about the beginning of February,
and if the Council stops everybody’s salary, as a
protest against the proposal to gi ve the people a
wider vote at Council elections, the people will
grow more interested in Council reform than they
ever were before. To placard the story of its own
Tory arrogance on everybody’s pocket is a foolish
kind of advt.
It is seriously proposed in Westralia to
levy a tax of 24 per cent, on all gold obtained
from mines that are being worked at a profit. A
shorter way would probably be to levy a tax of 2|
per cent, on all gold exported. Plenty of Wes-
tralian mines ought to be worked at a profit but
are not, through the awful expense of mismanage-
ment by Hooleys in London, and there is noreaspu
why the State should lose revenue on that account.
A tax on exported gold is a rough-and-ready way
of getting at the perennial absentee. Also, it
would yield about £lOO,OOO a year, which Forrest
needs badly; and gold is about the only thing left
to tax in W.A.
Premier J. R. Dickson was elected to Q.
Parliament two years ago on the Independent
ticket. He professed himself unable to see how
any honest freeman could tolerate a continuous
Ministry like that which oppressed the land. He
is now head of that continuous Ministry, and,
speaking about the forthcoming election, he
prayed the country, the other day, not to return
any Independent members. “Politics on the
British plan should never have more than two

that if it liked to demand a radically-reformed
Upper House which wouldn’t destroy all demo-
