The latest annual report of N.S.W. Post-
master-General Cook is very misleading. For in-
stance, it sets forth that the number of articles liable
to postage (letters, post-cards, packets, and news-
papers not on the free-list) posted in N.S.W. in
1897 was over 85,000,000. And all the stamps
sold, including those impressed in the office, barely
amounted to 80,000,000. Considering the number
of stamps used on telegrams, the number of cases
when two, three, or more stamps go on one letter
or paper, the number (estimated by the depart-
ment at .£24,000 worth) used as receipt-stamps,
and considering that about 200,000 stamps were
repurchased by the department, and didn t go into
circulation at all, it is probable that the number
of letters, &c., said to be posted in N.S.W. is
overstated by about 20 or 25 millions. Evidently
they are not counted —only guessed at.
The Federal conference of Premiers is ex-
pected to meet at Melbourne about the end of
this month. Both in Victoria and S.A. the
elected Federal delegates have been called toge-
ther to advise as to the policy which these pro-
vinces will adopt. But in N.S.W. Premier Reid
takes no notice whatever of the other nine repre-
sentatives into whose hands the matter was placed
by the vote of the nation, and whose work was
accepted and endorsed by the vote of a popular
majority on June 3. Of these delegates, Walker,
Richard O’Connor, and M‘Millan are out of Par-
liament, and Abbott is in a state of chronic
absence, so four of the people’s ten Federal repre-
sentatives have not even the semblance of a voice
in directing the new Federal policy. And about 18
individuals whom the country flatly refused to
trust with the work at any price have been doing
it vigorously.
Hen picnics are becoming quite common
in Melbourne since the bike came between the
maid and her mash. A score or so of girls, and a
couple of matrons club together, hire a turn-out,
and are driven into the wilderness, where they
spend the day eating pastry and having a great
time on tea, according to feminine ideas of festive
devilment. A. couple of weeks back a party of
this kind went out into the dim distance and
camped there, and began to frivol beneath the
gum - boughs, when suddenly a damp blanket
was thrown over their innocent gaiety by the
discovery that the driver—who went with the
vehicle and four—had appropriated the . demi-
john containing the picnic’s wine-supply, and was
having a riot “on his own” under a clump of
gum saplings. A matron advanced to expostulate,
but the driver’s drunk had already carried him
beyond the reach of argument, and he called the
lady “dearold girl,” and invited her to “ come-
’n’aveadrink,” whereat she fled precipitately. For
the rest of the afternoon the picnic sat apart, and
watched the progress of the driver’s wild jamboree
in great terror, and commented with whispering
humbleness. Towards nightfall the man fell
asleep, and a deputation seized the demijohn, and
poured the little wine that remained into the creek.
Then there was an indefinite and spiritless sug-
gestion about seeking assistance ; but they
were buried in the bush, and nobody knew
where to go in quest of aid, so the
picnic continued to sit huddled up in
a condition of unspeakable misery all through the
long night. Once the aforesaid matron attempted
to wake the driver, but he snorted and cursed, and
called her “M’rier,” so she desisted. She next
tried to get volunteers to help her to harness the
horses into the vehicle, but this, too, was a dismal
failure, because the girls were not sure which end
of the animals went in first. The driver awoke
shortly after daylight, a wretched, arid man with
a great head and an air of abject contrition. He
fixed the horses, and the picnic returned in that
perished condition which comes to every woman
who sleeps in the grass with her clothes and hat
on. The girls found their suburb in a state of
violent disruption in consequence of the mysteri-
ous disappearance of 28 ladies, and the hen picnic
is off.
During the month of last Sept, the New
South Wales Lager Bier Brewing Co. Ltd.
supplied the German warship Falke with '6OOO
quart bottles of Sydney Lager Bier for use On the
voyage to Samoa and Apia. During the vessel’s
stay in our harbor 600 gallons of bulk Sydney
Lager were supplied her. Another tribute "to the
excellence of the local lager comes from the Ger-
man warship Buzzard, which ordered 5500 quart
bottles of Sydney Lager for the return voyage to
Kiel. During this vessel’s stay with us she
received 50 gallons of Sydney bulk Lager daily.
Both ships’ crews cannot speak too highly of the
excellence of Sydney Lager. As the firemen,
officers and crews come from the country that has
always been famous for lager production, it is
clear that the Sydney Lager Bier Brewing Co.
deserves all that has been said by every con-
noisseur in favor of its lager.*
Amongst interesting documents in the
possession of a collector at Brown’s River,
near Hobart, are long screeds in Governor
Macquarie’s own hand, relating to the most
paltry and insignificant store-details. In this
collection (which The Bulletin hopes the
Australian Museum will make some effort
to secure) are also records which bring one
grimly face to face with the bloodthirsty old con-
vict days. A death-warrant, for instance, of 60
years ago—lB37 -written and signed on a half-
sheet of foolscap by Chief Justice Sir John Lewis
Pedder, and setting forth that one Brown or
Smith “having at the Session of Oyer and
Terminer, held before me at Hobart Town, been
con'icted of being illegally present on certain
premises,” wa3 sentenced to be hanged by the
neck until he was dead. Upon this cheerful docu-
ment the Sheriff one “ J. 8.” (Burgess) records
with ghastly terseness that “ this day execution
was duly had and done upon the body of the said
Brown.” The.collector has in his possession other
death-warrants which are throughout in the
beastly precise little fist of the cold-blooded little
Chief Justice Pedder aforesaid.


Liver* Kidneys Wrong.
H/EPATIC INDURATION!
Clements Tonic Called In.
IT CURED EVERY CASE.
The Case of Mr. JAS. SMITH.
(From the “ Liverpool Herald ,” July SO.)
(by our special reporter.)
Reports continually coming to hand from
different quarters as to the most exceptional
recovery of Mr. Jas. Smith, of High-street,
Liverpool, from a complication of disorders
which would prove fatal to a man of an
o! dinary constitution, we put inquiries
on foot to get the truth.
A representative cf this paper found Mr.
Smith in his shop in High-street, and asked
him if the current reports of his case were
true.
“Yes,” replied Mr. Smith, “and as I
think
© tVfy Remarkable Case
should have full publicity in the cause of
humanity I will give you an hour. My
complaint crept upon me in a slow, insidious
manner, like a thief in the night; it must
have pr gressed for many months before I
suspected anything was the matter, till
IViost Alarming Symptoms
Set In."
“ What were tin y ? ”
“ A strange dizziness and palpitation of
the heart. Even then I thought there was
nothing seriously wrong with me but a
touch of indigestion, which I expected
would pass off in a day or two. I found out
later, however, that my
Liver and Kidneys Were
Diseased.
In a morning the taste in my mouth was as
bitter as gall, and my food, instead of digest-
ing, used to go sour, can ing me to have a
disagreeab e breath, and he bitter taste to
rise almost all day. After eating I had ex-
treme oppressit n in the stomach, w ith fits
of intense pain. Then the undigested food
would generate a sour gas, which would
gradually distend my body till
The Agony Was Unbearable.
Mv heart would palpitate frightfully, and I
came to the conclusion that I had heart
disease. However, I found out later the
wind in my stomach was causing a pressure
on my heart that resulted in palpitation.
On another occasion I could have sworn that
a tumour was growing within me, as I could
feel a
9 Hard, Painful Lump
in my side.” (This was hardening of the
Liver — Ed.).
“ Did you suffer from any marked liver or
kidney symptoms ? ”
“Yes; a dull, aching, dragging pain
across the loins and in the small of my back
became continual. It felt exactly like a
56 Pound Weight
hanging on my backbone ; at night the pain
was woise. It then felt red hot as I rolled
from side to side vainly trying to get relief,
and there were piercing
Pains Across The Shoulders,
and the muscles got contracted.”
“ In what way, Mr. Smith ? ”
“ One morning I found my head all drawn
to one side, and my neck was fixed in this
position. When 1 wanted to turn my bead
to look at anything or to speak to anyone I
was forced to turn my whole body.”
“ You called in a doctor then, I sup-
pose ? ”
“ Yes ; don’t talk of doctors to me. I
must have taken
Bucketfuls of Their Physic,
but with no result. I went slowly from bad
to worse. Sometimes I was so giddy that I
had <o cling to something to keep on my
feet. *5 Teu ible headaches afflicted me. I
had long since lost my appetite. I ate as
little as po-sible, because of the pain and
distension that always followed.
My Long Continued illness
altered my disposition very much. I be-
came morose and dispirited. I grew
nervous, and I couldn’t bear anyone to
eve« speak to me. I lost greatly in weight,
and it you had seen me then you could
hetrejv have been persuaded that I was the
same man yon now see before yon. I was
thin and emaciated, with the deathly
pallor of
Confirmed Liver Disease.
The peculiar dusky, leaden look which
liver disease produces was unmistakably
seen in my face. My eyes had sunk into
my head. My form was bent, and my
head twisted, and I looked just like a
withered old man of eighty with one foot
in the grave. And
Now Look At Me?”
Our reporter took a careful survey of
Mr. Smith, and said : “ I should take you
for about 35, and for an exceptionally
strong and healthy man.”
“Well,” replied Mr. Smith, “that’s the
age I feel like, but I am just 50.”
Our reporter expressed surprise.
“ Yes, scores of people have 'aken me for
much under 40 years ; but I am getting
ahead of my tale. Time went on and I got
no better. My vitality w r as completely run
out. I felt I could no longer struggle
against
The Deadly Disease
that held me in its grip. One day I got
hold of a Clements Tonic book. I read it
through carefully, and made up my mind.
I sent for some bottles of Clements Tonic.
I started on it, being fully determined to
give it a fair trial. From what the book
said I thought
It Would Fit My Case.”
“ Ah ! that’s what I want to hear about;
with what result?”
“This result 1” said Mr. Smith, standing
up and displaying a tall and well-formed
muscular figure. “ With this result!” said
Mr. Smith, striking himself emphatically on
the chest. “ I was a wreck, worn and
wasted by disease, and Clements Tonic
made a man of me. I felt a change after
taking about half a large bottle. My
stiffened muscles seemed to be easier. I had
a faint appetite, better digestion, and the
pains were lessened sufficiently to enable me
to get some natural sleep, livery morning
I awoke decidedly better than I was the
previous night. 1 began to get more cheer-
ful. Improvement continued day by day,
till at last the time arrived when I could
stand up straight and
Feel No Pain Anywhere.
I was free. I had thrown off the thraldom
of the disease which had clung to me for
years. I was weak and thin, of course, but
I continued Clements Tonic till I was
Completely Restored to Health
and strength, and in a short time, consider-
ing how ill I had been, Clements Tonic
completely eradicated all disease from my
system. It was a bad case, so took some
time. Its effect on my liver and kidneys
w r as miraculous. I have recommended
Clements Tonic to many. It
Has Cured Every Case.
I did that with the same motive which in-
fluences me in giving you this history of
my case—out of pure gratitude for saving
my life—being recalled to health and happi-
ness from a condition worse than death. I
should like the whole story to be related to
the public just as I’ve told it to you.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Jamus Smith, of High-street, Liverpool, in the
Colony of New South Wales,do solemnly and sincerely
declaie that I have Carefully read the annexed docu-

A noteworthy move in journalism is Syd-
ney Daily Telegraph s issue (followed by
S.M. Herald), for the benefit of remote
country readers, of a late edition of its
morning issue containing new- right op to dis-
patch of evening rnads. This will be a nasty
knock for the Sydney evening papers, which have
liitherto left the city on their way to a lot of out-
side districts in company with the morning papers
of same day, whose news they supplemented by
many hours.

Parisians are so busy over their 1900 Exhibition
that the present is Britain’s time to bully France.
Johnny Crapaud is now a shopkeeper, and will
“ take it lying down ” for fear of spoiling his
“ Jubbert.”
If Bard Henerie had calculated upon being
taken with such astounding seriousness over his
humorous exaggeration about the number of beery
persons he recently counted in Oxford-street,
Sydney, he would probably have raised his figures
to 450 men, 390 women, and 16 parsons being
taken home in 27 cabs. And even then there
would have been wiseacres found to protest that
there must be a mistake somewhere !
S.M. Herald fell in badly last week, when
it criticised a story by Arthur Henry Paterson, a
fourth-rate English novelist, as if it was written
by Andrew Barton Paterson, of Australia—other-
wise “The Banjo.” The other Paterson’s tame
fiction —a story of the American civil war —was
described at length, and he was gravely adjured to
try an Australian subject next time. The
Herald’s blunder probably started in the fact
that the yarn in question—“The' Gospel Writ in
Steel,” pub. Macmillan, Lond., 1898—purports on
the title-page to be written by “Arthur Paterson,
author of * A Son of the Plains,’ ‘ The Man from
Snowy River,’&c.” Arthur Paterson wrote “A
Son of the Plains ” ; Andrew Barton Paterson —oor
Andrew, ye ken—wrote “The Man from Snowy
River.” The publishers will have Some difficulty
in explaining.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Undoubtedly the weakest province* as one
prominent cricketing authority tersely put it, again holds
the Sheffield shield. Since the inception of this trophy
Six vears ago, Vic. has won it four times, S.A. once, and
N.S.W. once. Facts speak for themselves. The Vic-
torians have a very strong and even batting side ; the
only man riot fit to open the innings is Johns, and he is
showing.marvellous form behind the sticks. One reason
of their success is that they don’t drop many catches.
In the first. Adelaide match there were three batsmen,
Stuckey, Giller and Worrall, who had not scored centuries
in local club cricket, but they promptly got even by
making 130, 116 and 104 respectively against Jones and
no. Against Sydney the dark blues fairly revelled in the
mud, and their p.vramid-like score after a bad start won
the second Adelaide match when tile tenth man, Trumble,
hiade 70 by perhaps the most elegant and soundest cricket
of the match.
The individual scores as the men got out ran in
this remarkahle pyramid fashion : 6,0, 13, 22, 25, 43, 52,
63, 70, 137, 11. .Trumble is in better bowling form than
ever, as his peculiar and remarkable record of 8 wickets
in one innings and 3 in the other in each of the interpro-
vincial matches shows. At present, as an all-round
player, he stands unrivalled in this hemisphere, possibly
in the world. Even last year the Vic. bowling was
severely and adversely criticised. The province had at
command : Trumble, one of the very best bowlers in the
world on all wickets ; Trott, possibly the best slow bowler
in the world ; Roche, one of the most brilliant bowlers in
Australia, and 6. M’Leod, who has started trundling for
his side in more than one internat., not to mention Giller,
Laver, Worrall and Bruce.
* * * #
Trumble’s bowling to date in the three Sheffield
Shield matches .-—Against N.S.W., 3 for 45 and 8 for 58 J
against S.A. in Adelaide, 8 for 129 and 3 for 36, and in
Melbourne, 3 for 57 and 8 for 39. Average, 11 runs per
wicket. Where would Victoria have been without
Trumble ?
* * • •
L>'g: Victoria owes her Sheffield Shield win
this season to Worrall’s luck with the toss and Trumhle’s
wonderful bowling on- “ dicky ” as well as good wickets.
He has taken 33 wickets in the three matches for 364
runs, a truly wonderful average for Australia. The long
M.C.C. player has easily supplanted Geo. Giffen as Aus-
tralia’s all-round cricketer. Trumble is in his 32nd year.
• * * «
“ The best man in Australia if he gits going—
bar Graham,” was Young Victoria’s opinion of Billy
Bruce when that, slender solicitor was scoring 52 runs in
his first innings against S.A. The difference between
Bruce’s confident hitting and “glancing” of the ball, and
big Laver’s awkward push strokes was very marked.
Later on Laver opened his shoulders and played an
attractive game.
Judged by Hugh Trumble’s order of going-in—
ninth man—the Victorians have a stronger batting “tail”
than a representative Australian Eleven could carry.
Victoria, on its own, would stand an even-money chance
of beating a team selected from N.S.W. and S.A., and
that’s a fact. Yet the next All-Australia Eleven will not
put more than four Victorians into the field for a test-
match, anyhow.
• * * *
The S.A. v. N.S.W. interprovineial match, last
week, was robbed of a lot of interest by the fact that
Victoria’s win of three matches had secured the shield
for this year.
* * ♦ *
The S.A. team, until getting the morning
papers at Moss Vale, N.S.W., was under the impression
that the match was to si art on Saturday, for which day
S.A. Cricketing Association had it down on its list of
fixtures. A curious mistake certainly.
* » * *
Gregory’s management of the respective bowling
of M’Kibbin and Howell in the S.A. match was rather
curious. Some partisans thought it almost looked as if
the bowling was worked so as to give M’Kibbin every
chance of getting a good average at the expense of the
other trundlers. Howell and Noble opened, and the
former made a start by dismissing the two champions—
Darling and Hill—for 16 and 12 runs respectively. Then,
when “on the spot ” and with two wickets for 28, he
was, to the surprise of all, taken off, and M’Kibbin
was put on to “go through the tail.” Noble at the
time had only one wicket for 31, but he was kept
on 1 M’Kibbin succeeded in capturing five tail-end
wickets for 54, which, despite “ Noss Iredale’s Chatty
Notes,” is only a very moderate performance, and
if on it he is picked for ’Ome before Billowel—whose
two wickets were worth Mac’s. 5 several times over —then
Billowel will certainly have a big grievance. Again, on
the afternoon of the second day, while Darling, Hack
and Hill in turn bashed the ball about, M’Kibbin, who,
on his bowling in the first innings should have been at
least first change, was kept off until about lOmin. before
stumps were drawn, when the light was none too good
and batsmen had steadied down to play out time.
• • • *
Trumper and Gregory were dismissed in m ch
the same manner in the N.S.W. first innings againsi*S.A.
Trumper took the first ball of the match from Jones—a
lightning delivery, which rose shoulder high, struck
Trumper on the fingers, and lodged safely in Jarvis’
hands. Gregory tried to get out of the road of his ball,
but was not quick enough, the ball touched his bat and
Darling caught him out. Another of Jones’bumpy ones
missed Donnan's jaw by a hair’s-breadth. Had it got
home Donnan would, probably, have left for pastures
new.
• • • •
M'Kibbin’s catching of Darling in the first
Innings of N.S.W. v. S.A. was perhaps the most sensa-
tional event of the match, for the hit had all Darling’s
power at its back in a direction where he is particularly
strong, pddly enough, Darling gave Howell a similar
chance in the same spot soon after commencing his
second innings, but such catches are only made once in a
way, and it was declined.
Scores up to time of going to press are : N.5.W.,.
Ist, 379 ; S.A., 164. S.A. followed on, and miraculously
reversed the prospect, putting up 469 before dismissal.
Hill’s 159 was the featured the innings. That left N.S.W.
with 255 to get to win, and it was anybody’s game. The
three first local wickets, however, fell for 127, and Noble
and Iredale were left going strong when copy was shut
off.
* * * *
Harry Donnan, one of the most consistent
N. “ Pennant ” scorers, led off with 160 (not out) in
the first interprovincial this season. He has played five
other interprovincial innings since then, resulting in 0,2,
O, 6 and 1. Victor Trumper, whose club-performances
are second to none, led off with 68 at Adelaide, and has
followed up with 0,4, if), 0 and 15. Both seemed to have
strong claims for England, but their run of bad play—or
luck—makes their show, at time of writing, anything but
rosy.
* * * *
Kensington, Sydney, had its first day under the
new regime last Wednesday, when the absence of the
“ horse ” officials was riot made conspicuous by any falling
off in the quality of the starting. Mr. Fleming sent his
fields away with the regularity of a line of cavalry. Pony
racing will continue about Sydney as of yore at well-
managed Kensington and the Soup Plates, and the only
difference will be felt by the “ horse ” officials who have
been mulct of a portion of their income by the action of
the mighty A. J.C. in debarring them from assisting at
the “ little ” meetings.
* * * •
The law never showed itself a greater “ hass ”
than last week at Rosebery Park, Sydney, when it fined
bookmakers for standing on candle-boxes to do their
betting. The ridiculous distinction between betting on
foot, and on a box, would have been better winked at by
the authorities.
* • • *
Number 5 had a day out at Brighton (Sydney)
pony club meeting last Thursday afternoon. The name
of the winner of the first four out of five races was oppo-
site that numeral, which fact, coupled with that of the
date being the fifth, set the large section of the noble
army of punters present talking about “ the office.”
Several took it and planked their cash on nnmber five in
the last race (“ Yellow Mantle ”) but the spell had petered
out, for 1, 2 and 3 finished in the order named.
* • » *
“J. E. Brewer and R. Lewis, who leave for
England in a few days, were associated with Lochaber’s
win in the Bagot Handicap yesterday, Brewer training
and Lewis riding the son of Lochiel, whose win would no
doubt have been more popular had he showed better form
at Caulfield on Boxing Day. He had previously run in
the Standish Handicap, and the stewards wanted to
know after that race why he did not shape better there.
Brewer convinced them that the chestnut did his best.
He brought evidence to prove that the stable had more
money on him for the short than for the long race. He
got. away badly in the Standish Handicap. There is no
doubt that Lochaber is a smart horse.”— The mildly
apologetic Argus.
And there is no doubt that the ’Ome-going J,.
E. Brewer is a smart owner, though the fact that Loch-
aber started at a short price for the Standish Handicap is
a point in favor of Brewer’s explanation. Assuming,
I'ien, that smart horse Lochaber did his best in the
s,x-furlongs’ race, and was ignominiously beaten as the
result of getting away badly (although he was not “left
at the post ”), the Glorious Uncertainty of the Turf would
seem to be ruled in a great measure by the starting-
machine. Punters who take 5 to 1 about a “good
thing” for a sprint-race are reckoning without the
machine, which almost invariably despatches the
field at the first .attempt, arid is becoming
notorious for its unsatisfactory work. In pre-
sent writer’s opinion, the alleged excellence of the
machine is an Australian delusion. Starts nowadays are
not worse (they may be somewhat better) than in the
days when irascible Geo. Watson and other unsuitable
officials had the management of affairs at the post.
Starts in those days were preposterously bad, as a rule.
The machine work is only a little less deplorable, and
people who deride the conservatism of Hinglish racing
authorities in sticking to the old flag system have riever
seen big fields of horses started by a competent man on
Newmarket Heath. American sports, who are not open
to the charge of conservatism, can see no virtue in the
machine. Yankee jockey Tod Sloan ridicules the idea of
it ever being adopted in England or the States.
Lochaber’s apparent inconsistency of form at
Flemington on New Year’s Day was entirely due to the
bad starting machine, we will say. The owner backed
him heavily to win the six furlongs Standish Handicap,
wherein he ran nowhere. Two hours later he backed
him to win the Bagot Handicap (1J mile) and this time
he won his money. “The victory,” says the Argus,
“ would have been more popular had Lochaber shown
better form at Caulfield on Boxing Day.” Well, the
stable didn't fancy Lochaber for the nine furlongs Hope-
toun Cup on December 26. It fancied him for a six
furlongs and a 12 furlongs race on January 2, but not for
the nine furlongs event on Boxing Day.
* * * •
That very in-and-out performer, The Provost,
had bad luck in getting beaten by Mystery for the Hurdles
at Flemington, Jan. 2. With list 71b. up The Provost
was backed down to 7 to 1, the victorious Mystery being
aloto 1 chance this time. At Caulfield on Boxing Day
the same two jumpers had run over the same distance
(two miles) carrying a trifle more weight apiece, and
neither of them showed prominently. On that occasion
Mystery was first favorite (4 to 1), whilst The Provost
went out unbacked.
• * * *
Lord Richmond, a handsome eight-year-old son
of Richmond and Gratitude, who was backed for one or
two M'elb. Cups in his younger days, seems to have re-
turned to “form.” Eli Jellett’s horse won the. mile
Welter Handicap at the recent V.R.C. meeting, and may
cut an important figure presently if he stands training.
• « » •
The disappointed person who drew Le Var in
the local “ Tatt.” sweep on Perth Cup doesn’t deserve
any public sympathy. It is understood that Le Var was
scratched (an hour before the race) because the owner
had not succeeded in levying on the ticket-holder. After
the scratching the owner received an offer, by wire, of
half the sweep-money in the event of his horse running
into a place. So the too-hasty “ squeezer ” tore his hair
and wept inwardly, whilst ..the man who plucklessly
offered to relinquish 50 per cent, of his rights had the
mortification of learning that he might just as well have
earned the respect of the publio by telling the owner of
Le Var to go to Jamberoo.
* * * *
Such is luck. The drawer of Stand Off in Tatt’s
Carrington sweep made the usual offer to the owner, but it
never reached him owing to some muddle over the
number of the P.O. box to which it was addressed, which
little mistake no doubt accounted for the 'stable deciding
upon Vivian as its representative.

In Maoriland, the other day, Fritz,. Australasia’s
champion (and it may be said only) trotter, brought the
record down to 2min. 13sec.—lsec. off his previous best,
Fritz started in a handicap and stood dancing on his
mark until his field, had gone fully 100yds., when he made
chase and ran third. At Bathurst show a couple of years
ago Fritz showed temper and played Mr. Buckland a
similar trick. A dangerous horse to put much money on
in a race.

Re suicide of bookmaker Billy M'Cormack, a
Sydney daily says that what made the occurrence even
more sad was the fact that, at the time, several of Billy’s
brother metalhcians were looking for him to help him out
of his difficulties. “Looking for him” is good. It is safe
betting that the poor devil did a lot of “looking” him-
self before he gave up hope and plunged into the Pacific,
The leather-lunged fraternity are not quite so liberal as
they would have the public believe. M'Corrhack’s
troubles were brought about by a certain person giving
the bookie a horse in which he was interested as a sure
“stiff,” and then getting a punter in to back it. The
horse won. Bookie had laid him “ till the cows came
home,” was hopelessly involved, and followed in the
wake of poor Teddy Sampson—for whom, by-the-way,
certainly profusely-charitable brother books were also
“ looking. * , *

The recent annual North Kent ploughmg-match
took place near Dartford, and was taken part in by 170
teams comprising 500 horses valued at £25,000, and all
these ploughs were at work in the one field ! The old-
fashioned Kentish gallows wood plough could he seen at
work alongside the modern steel balance plough, and the
chiefrinterest appeared to centre in the ploughing in the
champion balance plough class, where several “ old
hands” were competing. The judges awarded the first
prize to Mr. John Russell's man, Richard Mitchell. This
prize has now been won five years in succession with
ploughs made by Messrs. Howard, of Bedford.*

“ A. E. Trott with nothing to do,” writes an

F. T. Hack, the lanky junior in the S.A.
cricket-teams, has cotne into interprovincial matches
with a bound. Last season the Sturt executive did not
consider him worthy of a place In their club-team in Ade-
laide cricket, but this year he has an average of 170 odd.
He is a batsman of the Alick Bannerman order.
Suffield’s Dubious Defenders.
Mb. Griffith, M.L.A., complains that Theßulle-
tin’s comments on his Parliamentary action, and on
the inaction of the N.S.W. Labor-party, in connec-
tion with the Suffield case, are unfair. He says—
(l) The Labor Party never demanded an inquiry
for Suffield ; (2) that Premier Reid never pro-
mised a night for debating the question ; (3) that
no doubt or question as to Suffield’s guilt has
arisen since his conviction—seeing it arose before
his conviction; and (4) that The Bulletin’s
advocacy of Suffield’s claims has a nasty
flavor of party trickery, because it reserved “the
whole of its bother and ceaseless denuncia-
tion” for Preetrade Gould, and had “hardly
a word of censure to bestow ” on his Pro-
tectionist predecessors, O’Connor and Slattery.
To which this paper replies (1) that if the Labor-
party, which professes to exist to see justice done
to manual workers—and any other workers—did
not take the pains, being nobbled by Reid, to give
Suffield a chance to get justice, then it is so much
more to the discredit of the Labor-party. And
(2) that if Premier Reid never promised to give
Suffield’s case the fullest opportunity to be
inquired into, it is so much the worse for
Premier Reid. And (3) that, in the case of
the vast majority of people, the doubt or question
as to Suffield’s guilt (which the C.L. A. Act gives
as a legal reason for enquiry) must have arisen
since his conviction, because prior to his conviction
the vast majority of people knew nothing what-
ever about the case. By raising this quibble Mr.
Griffith shows himself even more unintelligent
than appeared from his blundering advocacy of
Suffield’s cause. And (4) The Bulletin edi-
torially commented on the Suffield case on Dec.
12, ’9l, and invited Protectionist Justice-Minister
O’Connor to look into it. On May 14, ’92, it
commented at length on O’Connor’s comments,
and pointed out —as in the case of Gould —that
Minister O’Connor believes in his inmost heart that the
prisoner is innocent, but that he must stay in gaol as a
sacrifice to certain legal precedents unless some new facts
come to light.
And this paper, during the intervening years, has
made numerous other references to the iniquitous
manner in which Suffield was convicted. Lately,
on receipt of a pathetic appeal from Suffield in
prison which no one of common humanity could
disregard, it has taken more pains to make itself
acquainted with the facts, with the result that its
conviction that Suffield was unfairly tried and
convicted against the weight of evidence has been
strengthened—and its advocacy of Suffield has
been strengthened. And, lastly and generally,
Mr. Griffith might be better occupied in combating
the suspicion that he has been trying to run with
the hare and hunt with the hounds —that he has
been defending Suffield only so far as was not in-
compatible with currying favor with the Ministry
—than in making loud noises when his vanity is
wounded by plain-speaking. Suffield has been a
victim to legal clannishness and Government by
Contradiction.

BLOOD
Your heart beats over one hundred thou-
sand times each day. One hundred thousand
supplies of good or bad blood to your brain.
Which is it ?
If bad, impure blood, then your brain aches.
You are troubled with drowsiness yet cannot
sleep; you are as tired in the morning as at
night; you have no nerve power; your food
does not seem to do you much good.
Stimulants, tonics, headache powders, can-
not cure you: but
AYERS
Sarsaparilla
will. It makes the liver, kidneys, skin and
bowels perform their proper work. It re-
moves all impurities from the blood. And it
makes the blood rich in its life-giving prop-
erties.
For biliousness take Ayer’s Piixs. They
promptly relieve and surely cure. Take them
with Ayer’s Sarsaparilla: one aids the other.

Price Is. ; per Post Is. 3d.
Fluid Disinfectant is invaluable to

The latest volume of ‘‘Nationalßiography ”
includes Banker Thellusson. He left his money
so entailed that an ultimate heir would come into
140 millions sterling. Circumstances burst it up.
An Act was passed in Britain to prevent such
wills in future. However, this one was held good.
The second vol. of “Spurgeon’s Life,”
just out, is replete with delicious twaddle by
widow Spurgeon on their courtship, seen through
the golden haze of 40 years. It is a unique re-
cord, comparable in its line to the Canticles ;
indeed, Mrs. S. always refers to the departed as
“My beloved.”
A thirsty sinner lately drank himself into.
D.T. in a M.L. pub., and then hung himself in
the stable. When the constable searched his
clothes, he found six shillings in the lining of his
vest. The licensee (a- woman) applied for the
money, alleging that the corpse still owed her for
a bottle of case-whisky when he died. She didn’t
get it, still the request was noteworthy in its own
small way.
A correspondent, who doesn’t give his
name, alleges that he saw a Respectable member
of the original N.S.W. God-fearing Ministry fore-
gathering with a nice barmaid in a Sydney city
street, the other Sunday night, and, after shaking
her most cordially by both hands, he went into
the adjacent hotel—presumably to have a drink.
The Bulletin views this tale with suspicion.
The original God-fearing Ministry only contained
three individuals, Reid, Suttor and Carruthers
(Want Wasn’t one of the original God-fearers)
who looked human enough to appreciate a nice
barmaid, or had enough Christian thirst to lead
them into a respectable hotel on Sunday. Most
of them were dry, bloodless ghouls who would have
been all the better for knowing some nice bar-
maids, and for having the run of the side door.
The humanising influence of girls and drink
mightn’t have made the ten God-fearers any more
politically honest or capable or democratic, or any
less clumsy or fuzzy-headed ; but it might at least
have prevented them being such dry, portentous
Bores. If old Sunday Brunker would abandon
his clean and holy' shave, and hug even one nice
girl in a public-house, and flatten out with a pint-
pot the man who wanted to dispute his claim to
her, and get home late with his hat covered with
cigar-ash, it would greatly mitigate his dreariness
and do a lot of good.

VITADATIO
HAS SAVED HUNDREDS OF LIVES.
IT MAY SAVE YOURS.
Nolan-street.
Maryborough (Vic.).
October 26,1898.
To Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Sole Australian Agent for
Webber’s Vitaclatio.
Dear Sir, When I
went to Melbourne in
February (of this year)
for a change, after a bad
attack of my old com-
plaint (the doctors called
it congested and enlarged
liver) my brother said
the best thing I could do
was to go and see you,
but I had been bad ss
long, and although my
doctor was very kind, it
seemed as if nothing
could be done to cure me,
[ bub, as I had known you
in New Zealand, I
thought, “ Well, if Vita-
datio has cured Mr.
Palmer, there must be
some good in it,” so I
decided to see you and
ask your advice about
my case. Of course, as
you are aware, I was
fersuaded to try it, and
am very thankful to
say it has been a great
blessing to me, as I have
passed the largest gall-
stone my doctor had ever
seen; the nearest thing I
can compare it to is a
pigeon-egg, and I think
when you see it you will
say I have not exag-
gerated it. One thing I
feel confident of, and
that is, that Vitadatio
has saved me a very pain- ||
ful and serious operatior
I am recommending Vitadatio to all my friends, several of whom have told me personally that it has
done them a wonderful amount of good. One old lady in particular, seventy-five years old, who has
been cured of piles. If you think this will be the means of getting any poor sufferer to try this won-
derful medicine, you have my full permission to use it. I will only be too glad to answer any inquiries
relative to my case if a stamped addressed en velope is enclosed for reply. Wishing you every success
m this great business, I remain, yours faithfully,
(Miss) E. tT. Mocking.
The price of this Medicine is—Large Size, 5s 6d; Small Size, 3s 6d.
Ask your Grocer or Chemist. If not obtainable locally, send to Head Office—
S. A. PALMER, 45 & 47 BOURKE STREET, MELBOURNE.

“ Snell”: We were camped on the side of
a heather-clad Gippsland gorge. It was Christmas
time, and financial depression kept us from town.
Ernberg volunteered to cook the Christmas
pudding, and set about the terrible task. He
boiled the composite in a kerosene-tin boiler, and
it swelled like a “ Khrishmiss ” head. That,
Ernberg said, was the beauty of it. But when he
attemped to get the large, round duff out, he lost
his temper and scalded himself in divers places,
and finally he turned the tin upside down and
jerked fiercely with a fluent accompaniment of
curses. Suddenly out shot the duff, and started
down the hill side. Ernberg fetched a yell and
started after it. But the duff increased its lead
with every bound, until the man caught his foot
in a root, and finished the rest of the descent in
two skids, three revolutions, and a wild slide. It
was a dead heat, but Ernberg had to be carted to
the township on a litter, and the duff did not turn
a currant. This was 14 years ago. I believe the
duff is there yet, although I have been told it
occupies a position of honor in a provincial
museum labelled as a new thing in meteorites.

Apathetic series of invitations to the eight old
ruins to gather up their creaking bones and come
back home. They seem to have long memories in
the Old Countree. Brother Charles, in particular,
who wants to hear of the relative who has been
unaccounted for during 67 years, is a fine example
of patience. Probably all of them will be heard
from more of less; Australasia is full of aged per-
sons who ate longing to be found by their brother,
Or by anybody else’s brother—most of them don’t
cate whose brother finds them so long as he has
the price of a sandwich about him.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

TESTIMONIAL .
Mr. ARNOTT.
Dear Sir,
I am sending a photo, of my baby, Cecil
Roddam, aged 11 months. He has been fed on
your Arrowroot Biscuits since he was two months
old. When he was born he was too weak to
suckle, so we started with one, and gradually in-
creasing till now he has a large cupful three times
a day. You can see by his photo, what a healthy
child he looks now. We can thank you that he
has got on so well —he is also very strong with it.
I can with very much pleasure recommend them
to any parents who want their baby healthy, strong
and good-tempered.
I am, sincerely yours,
Mrs. H. RODDAM,
Pyrmont.
CECIL RODDAM, aged 11 months— Red since birth on
arnotts milk
The Children’s Food of Health.
Arrowroot Biscuits!

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A gaunt, grey figure; pepper-and-salt hair and
beard, peppery grey eyes and salt-white teeth,
pepper-and-salt clothes; holding in the long
acquisitive fingers of his hand a grey wide-awake
hat. Above the average in height, above the
average in intellect. Concentration of purpose
strongly marked in every feature—the broad fore-
head, the keen eye with the alert look rarely seen
except in native races, the high-bridged nose, the
firm chin, the hard mouth. It is a woman who
knew him well that gathers the gist of him for
The Bulletin.
* * * *
“ People say I’m a woman-hater,. do they ?
Then they do not know me. Reckon you’d want
to know women to hate them. I don’t know
women. I’ve never had time to give to women,
and I’ve never regretted it. When I was at an
age to marry I was too busy, and now I’m too old.
The women I’ve known well I’ve liked, but it’s
few I’ve known. I don’t hold with those town
women who put all their husbands’ earnings on
their backs—over the city walls with them to the
dogs ! say I. They wear top-knots like the cocka-
toos, and shriek as loud. I don’t hold with them ;
but a real good woman who is not too much for
starch, and frills, and top-knots, who sets her
mind to making a good man happy, I hold with
her —ooh! ooh ! She’s great, and the more say
she has in things the better for all ; but those sort
won’t say much.
“ Do I think women should have a vote ? That
I do. Anything to turn their minds to more
serious things, and the women with their hus-
bands and sons would put down the drinking-
shops. Woman’s got her brain all right ; for all
that some of them are for starch, frills and top-
knots, there’s many good sensible women with
good head-pieces.
“ Some men tricked me into travelling along a
railway-line with the lady as the Times sent out.
Ooh ! ooh ! But she’d a good head, she had, and
only a hand-bag for luggage —and she a woman.
And she could listen, too ; it’s not many men nor
women you find do that. I saw it was real first-
hand information she wanted, and I give it to her.
I told her about our forests, the value of them;
how the cedar alone that we have is worth thou-
sands ; how I went myself and picked timber-trees,
Jiants of the forest hardly trodden by men. Ooh !
lut our timber is a great asset—and some day
they’ll know it, and that, more than likely, when
it’s wasted —butchered for nothing. A new coun-
try is like a young man come into his money too
young—a prodigal, a spendthrift. Ooh ! ooh ! It
is not just wool and gold we must look to to pull
us up to a nation, it is timber that will help, and
butter and such like —think of the butter made
from the milk of saltbush and grand natural grass-
fed cows—the world can’t equal it.
“Ooh! ooh! We’ve a grand future, and we
must not forget who has helped to build it up—
the good hard-working Scotch colonists, they’re
the men ! First, I place an Australian that’s been
trained to use his eyes and hands, and to hold his
tongue ; then a Scotchman, who can make money
and keep it. They said I was going to sell out all
my Australian securities, did they ?—buy an island
in the Mediterranean and settle there. Well, I
thought of doing so when all the Governments
seemed going mad, the way they were harassing
the squatter. I thought just to clear out and
]eave > em to fighfc it out> ]ike those Irish cats as
had only the tails left—the members are mostly
tails, no headpieces. They’re poor men, and
they’re after the gold, and go all ways to get it—
££.*££££s 'taST-S* S feS?
m i xed) and the country messed up, and good
men are sacrificed. .1 meant to be off, and get an
island to myself just where the climate was tem-
perate, and I could grow melons. Id miss the
melons. Melons are good. I irst comes salt beef,
and & bifc ()f cabbage boiled with it . then melons!
F or dr i nk) tea, not too strong, made by a bush-
man in a billy. Women’s tea is washy.
“No, I never tasted spirits in my life, nor a
g i ass 0 f w ine nor beer. No, nor swore an oath,
no r kissed a strange woman, nor smoked ; but see 1
I carry tobacco. No, I don’t chew, but I like to
have a bit on me in case I meet an old man who
wants a smoke. It s no credit to me to have done
withoufc those things . 1 never had no taste for
them, and what a man hasn’t a taste for he never
misses.
“If I’d married, I’d have been an indulgent
husband-you’re laughing but it’s true; but I’d
have suffered in my work, and never have been
the man that j am ' There ’s many made money,
and wd j again, but none has made as much in one
line as me—none—nor ever will. Men are aye for
change, but I kept on. Cattle I knew, and sheep,
and country above ground. Those three I stuck
to; let them as like go into mining, but 1 keep
my securities above ground . About a future I
know nothing, and I reckon there’s none can tell
me. We’ll all know some day, but on what I can’t
prove I don’t waste my thoughts.
“ Yes, there was once when I had thought of
marriage. I thought I cared for a young girl once—
a fine, strapping, bright-eyed young woman, but I
had to go away to work, and, before I came back,
she had married the blacksmith. I gave no more
thought to marriage. It was all for the best ; I’d
never have been the man I am, married. Yes,
I’ve seen er since, and was able to do her a
good turn once ; things come round, things come
round. I’d no ill-feeling to her husband nor to
her ; why should I ? And I’m not against mar-
riage for others ; I reckon your husband is the
better for it ; you’re cheerful, and no topknots,
“ Starched collars ? No, I never wear ’em ;but
you see this collar, that’s celluloid ; that’s the
thing to wear when you want setting off with a
collar—you are not beholden to any woman then
to wash it for you : just wet the end of your towel
and rub it round, and there you are—good as new.
I’ll give your husband some ; they’ll set him off
when he goes out with you. Soap ? No, I never
use soap ; plain water’s good enough for me to
wash in ; and these cold baths men blow about
taking are just a bit of flash ness—no good to them
at all—just a new-fangled notion, a showing-off.
“Music? Yes, I’m fond of music—the old
Christy songs like ‘ Hard times, come again no
more’! and ‘The Old Folk at Home,’and the
old tunes like ‘ The Last Rose of Summer ’ and
‘ Home, Sweet Home.’ They’re good ; they’re the
music for me. Poetry ? Bobby Burns is my man.
Give me his book and I’ll pick out the good ones
for you. What, no book of his in the house ! But
I’m astonished, and you so fond of books ! * A
man’s a man for a’ that,’ and ‘ The Author’s Fare-
well to his Native Country,’ and ‘ To Mary in
Heaven’ those are good—ooh ! And ‘The
Cottar’s Saturday Night! ’ That’s how Scotland
built up her fine race of men—the good, homely
life ! A great man for the lasses, Burns ! and
that ruined him. A fine man ; but never one to
get on—too many petticoats about. But you must
read him. There’s a lot in him, all against the
shams —a great man ! and he but a poor bit o’ a
ploughman. And the great ladies got hold of
him, too, ooh ! They’re allalike ; once a man takes
their fancy he’s as good as lost to business.
“ Did I meet Lord Carrington ? Yes ; but not
at my own wish. I’m not much for Governors
and such like ; their ways are not my ways. I’m
a simple man, and too old to change. He said he
wanted to have a long talk to me and hear my
view of Australian subjects, and asked me to go
to Government House. But I told him I was a
busy man, but the newspapers were good in the
colonies, and if he read them carefully he’d get a
lot of information from them, and I said, ‘ Good
day, mister,’ and I came away. I’ve no time for
them sort. Then his wife wrote to me a kind
enough little letter saying that if I’d go to Govern-
ment House she’d see that only her husband and
children were there to meet me, and we could all
have a chat. No doubt she meant it kindly, but
I’m not for strangers, so I took no more notice
of it.
“Yes, it’s true enough I’d sooner camp out
than stay at a station any day. I come here ; yes,
I’ve known your husband many years, and now
you are a friend of mine too. Before I go you
must make me a list of books you want; 111 send
them to you ; you like books, and I’ll like to send
them to you. Look after your husband, don’t let
him talk too much. There’s a deal to be learnt
by listening. Don’t let him be too set on driving
four-in-hand ; two’s enough. The men that drive
four-in-hand go out and owls come in, yes ; the
owls hoot round where the four-in-hand men once
laughed. They go down—the men who drive
four-in-hand, wear starched shirts, and dress up
their bottles at the table in white rags. They go
down, that sort! ooh ! ooh ! they go down !
“You’ve made me very comfortable. There’s
things I’d alter on the station, but I can’t fault
the house, unless maybe you feed us too well.
I’d grow fat if I stayed here long, but I’ll come
again and see you, and you must come and see the
Downs country and stay with me. I’ll show you
my books ; I’ve some good ones. Not that I’ve
much time for reading; but some day, maybe,
I’ll take my rest and enjoy it that way. Enjoy
life ? Of course I do in my own way. Going to
theatres, breathing in an air foul with diseased
people’s breath ; balls with women baited to catch
men, and dinners of a bit o’ this to eat and a bit
o’ that, half an oyster here and a bit of messed-up
sweetbread there, and a something you don’t
know, and a something you don't like, and sick in
the morning and no head for figures—that’s no
pleasure to me. I like to make money, and let
that money make money. I don’t like it to lie
idle, not to be making more and more and show
what a grand country Australia is to make money
in if you go the right way to work.
“ Won’t I grow tired some day and want to
rest? I’m tired often, but never want to rest. I
want to see the other side of the world some day,
but I can’t find anyone I can trust to look after
things while I’m away ; I’ll have to die in harness
—keep going, keep going—poor feller me, poor
feller me ! .
“ People say I don’t care what becomes of it all
after I’m gone—the crowd can scramble for it —
do they? They don’t know me that say that.
Maybe I put people off so, saying that I owed
them no answers. It was none of their busi-
ness ; but you’ll see no man who races horses
or drinks will have the handling of my money. I'd
sooner throw the lot over Sydney Heads. Who
do I owe most to in life ? My mother first—a
good woman, a clean woman : a poor woman in a
wee cottage, but clean and honest, and a great
woman with her needle. Ooh ! ooh ! But we
were poor, and not much could she give us ; but,
as a wee laddie, somewhere I got an old gftn, and
then I was a proud boy. I’d shoot pigeons and
gill-birds. My mother was too poor to buy pow-
der and shot for me, so I used to sell birds to the
travelling dealers in exchange for it. Such a fancy
I had for guns that my mother thought to ’prentice
iiio to a gunsmith, but the Bush was calling, and
to the Bush I went. I was not very old when I
made up ray mind that I could save and save and
save until I could buy all I wanted, and I did.
And then the saving habit was on me, and 111
save always, turning my money over and over.
“There’s ways of doing things. When I was
travelling and the meat wanted to travel on its own
account, some men would have minded, but not
me. There’s no dirt in it; just put the meat on
the coals and they’ll out, and there you are—meat,
as good as ever. Many a meal I’ve had off beef
another man would throw away. Ooh ! ooh !
there’s ways of doing things. Before I’d be going
out to the dry blocks to muster, I’d drink my fill,
I’d drink again, and again, until I felt sick, and
I’d drink again, and spue it up, and then I could
go long times without water, retching at the very
thought of it. That’s how I did in those days.
“The blacks? Yes, I like the blacks. When
I was a struggling man they stuck to me ; many’s
the bit of overlanding I’ve done with blacks. No
hunting of blacks from my stations. They are a
great people if you take them right; I’m proud to
see you have them round you as you have. They
are a sensitive people. There’s many does not
understand them. There’s ways of working them.
They are a great people for a joke, and they ve
great memories; never break a promise to a black-
fellow, he’ll never forget it if-you do ; and crack a
joke with him sometimes ; and don’t forget he has
his feelings the same as yourself.
“ Where are your pretty flowers ? They make
the home bright and cheerful, and ay, it’s great
to make a man’s home bright and cheerful; it
helps along the moral sunshine —that’s what we
want to make life wholesome, moral sunshine ! I
never see you pulling a long lip ; always a smile
for an old man. Now, there’s one station I have
to go to sometimes, and there’s a woman there
looks so sour she sets my teeth on edge. What
good is that sort of woman at all ? What s the
good of a woman if she doesn’t help along the
moral sunshine ? ”
* * * *
Tyson never went away without tipping the
little aboriginal maids in my house a sovereign each,
and the oldest black man in the camp, too. It
gave him great pleasure to hear the little black
girls sing the Christy songs I had taught themj
he sent them many new books of songs. We had
another sympathy in common—a love of the wild
birds. I never kept a caged bird in my life, and
when I came to the station I stopped the aimless
shooting for shooting’s sake of the wild birds
around the homestead. I encouraged them to
come about by scattering in winter, when grass-
seed was scarce, crumbs about my garden paths,
and in summer placing stands of water along the
verandah, and there might be seen at times top-
knot pigeons, doves, minahs, magpies, butcher-

Symptoms of Kidney Disease.
Its symptoms are numerous and varied, and but little understood by the
majority of people. That “ Backache ”is a sure indication of weakness. That un-
usual desire to urinate, especially at night, indicates an irritation caused by
brickdust or other sediment. That hot, dry skin, that uncertain appetite, that sour
stomach, that scalding sensation, that abundant light-colored morning flow,
that heartburn and dyspepsia, those rheumatic pains accompanied by chills
and fever, that coated tongue, that loss of memory, that bad taste in the mouth, are
all evidence of the presence of some form of kidney disease.

birds, jackasses, peewees, and others, just as if they
were tame, some hopping about the verandah and
hardly stirring when one went out. When Tyson
saw these birds so tame he expressed immense
delight, and after breakfast always used to look
out to see which of my bird-friends, as he called
them, had come, and just as a child might have
done he would call back to me to hurry up—my
birds were waiting.
I can see him now—that tall, grey figure—as I
saw him last, saying good-bye to me on my veran-
dah, shaking me by one hand as he patted me
paternally on the shoulder with the other, and
with a twinkle in his eye saying, “ Your husband
is a good fellow, a good fellow ; but teach him to
listen.”
And now he has passed out of my life to remain
in my mind one of the most interesting personali-
ties who has ever come into it. A man of millions,
devoid of social ambition. A man of millions, as
simple as a child in some things : simple indeed
must be the man who treats a club-man past
middle age to a shilling’s-worth of peaches and a
packet of brandy-snaps, as he did more than once.
A man who has been over-praised and under-
praised. A man who meant well in as much as he
knew. A man who was so sensitive to rebuff, so
suspicious of hidden laughter over his social short-
comings, on the score of which he was deeply sen-
sitive, that he shrank within himself, neither
often knowing others nor letting himself be known
—so leaving the impression that he was a grey
man, in a grey setting, leading a grey life, with
the single purpose of reaching a golden goal.
But, behind it all, I see the privations of his
youth—the toiling mother, the pleasureless child,
the incipient love nipped in the bud, the life-de-
sire to reach the antithesis of his youth—from the
poorest child in Australia to become the richest
man. And of him it may be said, as of how few—
“ He attained ” though at the cost of living a
lonely life and dying a lonely death. May he
rest in peace—poor millionaire !
At Our Repeating Station.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The man who is born and bred in the back-country
does many things for himself and his mates which
the city fellow leaves to the woman and the man-
specialist. Thus he will cut your hair, and
cut it passably. If you must be shaved, he will,
after some protests, shave you. Give a new-
chum those jobs though, and But did you
ever try a prentice hand at shaving another ?
Our township is a repeating telegraph-station,
with a blacksmith’s and a saddler’s shop, and five
pubs.—four or five. But its glory is the repeating-
station.
The line-repairer, who is naturally a person of
very much consequence, had come in with an in-
jured hand, and, for shaving, was at the mercy of
the operators. Now, the only available operators
were raw hands from Sydney. Denton is an old
hand in these parts ; but, then, Denton is S.M.—
signifying station-master—and that is only a little
lower than the angels : certainly not so much
lower as to allow him to shave anybody, except
always the Minister and the Public Service Board.
So Farquharson couldn’t approach him.
Rhodes—Rhodes is one of the operators—is an
obliging little fellow, though I don’t think he will
last long in our township, as the dust and heat
are playing upon his nerves. He was not very
confident of his powers to shave, bub he would
try. Rhodes is very obliging.
Luckily the lines were working well; and when
that happens, all one has to do is to keep an ear
on the familiar ticking of the instrument, and the
delicate mechanism does the rest. But when
storms sweep suddenly over those vast stretches
of rolling sand, and jangle the wires, and under-
mine poles, or when the rare rains fall, then the
repeating-operator guesses what is sent to him,
and wearily retransmits his reading of the puzzle.
And you wise men in the city take it without
salt every morning.
Rhodes spread his victim out upon a chair
beside the instrument, The lather was put on
without any mishap to speak of. It was good
soap, anyhow. Even the first few sweeps of the
razor were made successfully, and Rhodes was
encouraged ; though it is true that he had nob
noticed before how dark the evenings were at 5.30.
Sweep, and the razor rang again—
“ One moment.”
A “ press ” was going through; and somehow
the Sirdar and the Tsung-li-Yamen were bumping
one another. They were straightened out and
sent on their way in proper order.
And the razor rang once more to the accompani-
ment of the ticking instrument; but—
“ Hang it; off again.” It was a code quotation
that dragged.
A pimple. That, at least, was Rhodes’ explana-
tion. Heavens, how it did bleed !
“Look here, old man, better postpone it,”
Rhodes urged. “Hear it now. I oughtn’t to
have my hand off the key.”
“ Postpone it! And me show up at the pub.
with five days’ growth on one side and scraped on
the other—nob much ! ”
Now the lines refused to carry an enigmatic re-
ference to the Czar and Pobyedonosteff, Procu-
rator of the Holy Synod. Backwards and forwards
between face and instrument the distracted, ob-
liging Rhodles darted. Pimples—pimples seemed
to start upon that face like rabbits from a warren.
Started! up—and disappeared. And with every
blood-spurt that infernal line worked worse.
Heigh, what ? The man at the other end had
begun to pass remarks, as angry operators do.
And a thrilling hotchpotch which might be the
result of a race, or the death of a prince, or a
commons election, or the name of a new opera
bumped and spluttered along.
The man in the chair had also been making re-
marks for some time.
“All right, all right—here, put on some more
lather. Now ”
More pimples ! No ; half a chin this time.
“ Great Edison,” Rhodes gasped as he pressed
the pieces together and mopped at Farquharson’s
throat with the office-duster —“ I hadn’t any idea
a man’s chin went out so suddenly as that. Here,
you hold this rag there, while I get on to the
other side Oh, damn that instrument ! ”
Anyhow, the victim was subdued when that
hitch had been straightened. There was blood on
his shirt, blood on his pants, blood on the floor,
blood on the lamp-globe, blood even on the keys.
“Now, stretch your neck a bit —yes, that’s it.
Listen ! Curse those wires ! No, I’m hanged if I
do. He can swear and report if he likes ; I’in-
going to finish you, Farquharson.”
Rhodes handled the razor with the energy of
despair, and
The local telegraph-messenger stepped from be-
hind the screen at the door, stealthily. Hu
looked at the victim ; he saw the attitude ; he
saw the blood ; he saw the operator’s eye ; he
heard his words—“ I’m going to finish you, Far-
quharson ! ”
Three minutes afterwards our constable rushed
in to arrest the murderer. Half-a-minute after-
wards our doctor puffed up to see if life was quite
extinct. He had just had visions of countless fees.
Just then the line had begun to go smoothly
again. A fresh coat of lather had been put on the
places that weren’t too badly gashed—
And that was the nearest our township ever got
to a tragedy.
P.
Terse Verse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Snag”: Be broken country and
fissures. Was cross-cutting for a reef lately, and
discovered a large fissure—a very extensive mine,
minus the reef. Rolled a few rocks down it, and
went back to camp satisfied I had discovered the
place where the blooming country went broke.
Came back next day ; could hear the rocks still
going.
“B.” writes:—Here is a case I should
like “Steele Rudd” to give definite and know-
ledgeable opinion upon. I had better begin at
the beginning, so that your “ selection ” authority
and historiographer may be seized of all the facts.
lam well-nigh -10 years of age. I was born a
student —this often happens in old-world decadent
countries. I have read everything that I have
happened on, and dried up what soul I ever pos-
sessed with all sorts of economic speculation. But
still I did not achieve perfect happiness. The
discontented roar of the city and the fetid dust of
the streets are always in my soul. Now let
“Steele Rudd,” from the abundance of his ex-
perience, counsel me. Is content to be gained in
hewing trees, and ploughing root-reticulated bush-
lands, and delving and digging until the body
refuses to delve and hew and plough and dig any
longer ; and then to the slab humpy to eat Gar-
gantuan meals of pumpkin and corned beef, suc-
ceeded by such sleeps as the Seven Sleepers slept ?
And this day after day, and year after year, until
Death did the final digging and turned the last
furrow and buried his axe, as far as I was person-
ally concerned, for ever and a day ? In short, was
Old Dad happy on that selection of his, and, if so,
how much ? And if he was how can I become an
Old Dad also ?

LOVE.
Love is a fire, as we conceive,
One day we’re scorched by vivid flashes,
Then sit a year, and make believe
We find some warmth among the ashes.
PROHIBITION.
That prohibition up to now
Succeeds quite well
In one place only, you’ll allow—
And that is Hell!
WORLD MAKING.
God made the world in just six days—
So much the faithful parson says—
And through these many thousand years
Has man been busy on repairs.
FRIENDS.
With amiable conceit we boast
If we possess of friends a host,
Forgetting that it is from these
That we recruit our enemies.
EFFICACY.
His tiny troubles and his great
Paraded he at heaven’s gate.
So oft he knelt in prayer that he
Has now contracted housemaid’s-knee !
THE J.P.
A conscientious Judge is he,
A fact that he admits himself.
For proof attend his court and see
How often he commits himself !
ON GIVING.
If careless how you give, your load
Of obligations won’t be light.
A benefit that’s oft bestowed
Is next expected as a right!
LIKE FATHER LIKE SON.
Our little follies run in lines—
As the tree’s bent the twig inclines !
THE SLANDER CASE.
The simple fellow goes to law
To stop one venomed tongue from nagging,
And finds when in the box, with awe,
He’s only set a fcvorse one wagging.
THE ACCURSED DRINK.
My temperance friend is delighted to dub
“The route of all evil ” the road to the pub.
PARADOX.
Figures cannot lie, ’tis true,
And yet we know that numbers do.
WAYWARD WOMAN.
So contrary is her condition,
So void of frankness, so unfair,
That woman manifests contrition
Most when declaring “ I don’t care ! ’
FAT MAN'S EPITAPH.
A many greedy, drowsy years
He spent accumulating weight,
And now below the worms, his heirs,
Are dissipating the estate !
THE DOUR'T.
There must be doubt of Heaven’s space
So careful are the men of grace
From where the human torrent rolls
To save the very smallest souls.
ON A LADY.
Raise teeth she wears —I think perchance
To keep her tongue in countenance.
EDWARD DYSON.

The Message of the Spear.
Away back in the year 54 8.0. —1948 years ago—
a little detatchment of Roman soldiers was
beseiged by a host of Gaols at Chaleroi, in the
north of what is now called France. Believing
that no help could possibly reach them, the
Romans expected to fight until they were all
striken down. One day a short spear came
whizzing over the ramparts and stuck in one of
the wooden towers. Nobody paid any attention
to it at first. They just went on slaughtering
Gauls and getting slaughtered themselves. At
length an officer saw something fluttering from
that spear. It proved to be a note from Julius
Caesar —written ip Greek. He said he was on his
way with an army to relieve the garrison. That
note had been there unheeded for several days.
In due time Czesar came and made short work of
the Gauls.
That people should suffer and struggle hope-
lessly when help is near at hand is sad to think of,
but they must know of the coming help before they
can draw courage from it. Take the torments and
dangers of disease for example. We fight them
with all the weapons we have. Sometimes we hit
on the right thing, and more often we don’t, even
when it is as dose as the spear in the tower was to
the exhausted garrison.
If Mr. Robert Lavis, postmaster at High Ham,
Langport, Somerset, could have found a remedy
for indigestion, dyspepsia and liver complaint, he
certainly would not have suffered from it, as he
did, for twenty years. Why, that is more than
half an average lifetime. A single day of illness
is always long enough, goodness knows. But
fancy such an experience stretching itself into
weeks, months, and years ! Common enough ?
Yes, dreadfully common. So is poverty ; but
does that fact reconcile anybody to either '! Mr.
Lavis is not a man to sit tamely down and brood
over a misfortune. Besides being postmaster lie
is a grocer, with work enough to keep him busy.
Under what difficulties this work was done he
tells us in a letter dated Nov. 8, 1893.
“ I had a bad. taste in the mouth,” he says,
“ particularly in the morning, and my mouth was
dry, and I spat up thick, tenacious phlegm. After
eating even the simplest food I had great pain in
the chest and around the heart. I suffered greatly
from sick headache and giddiness, and if I exer-
cised much I got out of breath.”
[This was asthma, caused by the stupefying
action of foiil blood upon the nerves that move the
lungs ; the impurities in the blood having come
from the festering mass of undigested food in the
stomach. The heart trouble, the sick headache,
and the giddiness were symptoms of the same
thing.] • „ T • «T
“As time went on, continues air. Lavis, 1
Oecame very low, weak, and mentally discouraged
and depressed. ”
[A doctor, writing about this case for a learned
medical journal, would use many tough Latin
word’s, but he would not make the facts as plain
as Mr. Lavis himself has made them. Naturally
a man who can eat but little—which little mostly
rots in his stomach—will lose flesh and strength
and come to be of small use to himself or to any-
one else. We can all see that even by candle-
light.]
“ I tried many medicines,” he says finally,
“ but they did me no real good. At last I heard
of Mother Seigel's Curative Syrup, and used it. I
am happy to tell you that it soon relieved and
oured me. Whenever I feel a sign of my old ail-
ment I resort to the Syrup at once, and the disease
gets no further hold upon me. Publish my state-
ment if you like, and believe me yours, &c. f
(Signed) Robert La vis. ”
The Roman soldiers left Cassar’s message unre-
garded for days. It was written on parchment and
fluttered from a spear, as I have said. But, see!
We live in the age of print. Are you ill and in
pain as Mr, La vis was? Every newspaper, every
magazine, almost every publication your eye falls
on, contains accounts of what Mother Seigel’s
Syrup has done, and daily does, for those who
suffer. Read the messages. This is one: and,
having read it, you know where to look for help.
